The original duty (2½%) was not applied to companies paying more tax in the UK (see e.g. 310 of Ordinance at 23). Presumably the additional duty as well would not apply to them.

Economic Dept should see the proposals. My main comment on them is that if intended to be an equivalent for the Income Tax, they may be rather fortunate as the Income Tax legislation in the S.S. and to F.7.5 has not got passed and is raising considerable controversy. The imposition of their duty before the introduction of Income Tax might weaken opposition to the latter measure among local matters and their interests. Whether the rights or wrongs of the controversy we have on passing stood about business the decision to the validity of the offences. To be consistent with this we should, in my view, agree to the introduction of the duty (the things being equal) only after the Income Tax legislation has passed and will refractory for it.

From the revenue point of view, there is probably much to be said for the duty. The Malaysian Administrations concerned have been a little exaggerated at the amount they have been drawing on their reserves recently, large things there may be?

W. Bh. Toman.

I confess that I dislike this very much. An export duty is a very poor security for an income tax and I think it would be a bad precedent for the future to adopt this device. I am not sure either what would be the position of United Kingdom companies under this proposal. It rather looks as if they would

===== Page 5 =====
Would suffer rather badly. If they pay income tax in Malaya, they can get relief under the mutual arrangements between this country and British Empire countries, but they would not be able to get deducted from the United Kingdom income tax any export duty paid in Malaya.

If Malaya thinks that the income tax is not going to take enough off the companies, then the solution will be to impose an excess profits tax, as we have urged in a recent telegram. I think that the days for the rough and ready method of export duty have passed, especially if the machinery for adjusting taxation more accurately to real profits is going to be created. All this, of course, is on the assumption that there is going to be an income tax in Malaya. As Mr. Monson says, if the income tax proposal is not adopted, this is in any case premature. I would put these objections to the Governor and say that we should like further detailed explanation if it is desired to proceed with the proposal.

8.1.1941.

I agree with Mr. Caine.

If so, declare it please, as A could be for a little different?

Mr. Grant

We discussed.

If this proposal and the Appropriate Prime Tax Legislation go through, there will be three categories of companies held to User information: viz:

1) UK companies which will pay UK Tax and nothing in Malaysia.
2) Malaysia registered firms and other companies who will pay 5% export duty tax as minor tax.
3) Other Malaysia registered companies who will pay Malaysia minor tax.

You brought that the proposals under (2) were intended to deal with the problem of the Chinese commercials where it is generally admitted cannot really be brought into the scope of the income Tax proposals without a great deal of wisdom in thinking place. The matter and its extent duty imparts a rough and ready method of voting approximately their disability of time, and will give them less scope for "cooking" their accounts.

It will also, in the Governor's admit, reduce the opportunity to the Income Tax Bill! Our firm consent to their admitted clarity may therefore adequately somewhat impinge the establishment of neutrality on what have hitherto applied in the matter. Subsequent to this, however, I think that the necessary limitation can be approved.

The Income Tax Bill is brought to them on Monday and it may be as well to bear the Governor there an answer before them.

===== Page 9 =====
14. This has already been dealt with in the D.O.T. letter at 3 and no further action is necessary.

15. This crossed our telegram at 12 approving the proposals for the increased rubber and tin export duties, and no action is necessary.

16. The Governor has now asked for permission to allow a free vote to be taken on the Income Tax Bills in the S.S. and the F.M.S.

As he explained when the income tax issue was first raised, the proposals were that the entire proceeds should be handed over to H.M.G., and in these circumstances we instructed that the vote should be left to the Unofficial Members.

He now explains that with the change in the situation in the Far East which has meant an increased necessity for defence measures in Malaya itself, it will be necessary to devote the proceeds of war taxation, whether income tax or otherwise (e.g. the export duties at present imposed) to local expenditure. This is confirmed, as far as the S.S. is concerned, by the speech of the Acting Financial Secretary in opening the Estimates debate (enclosure 4 to 1 on 50216/40 which I am sending on below). It is clear from this that the S.S. Government are convinced they must not draw on their reserves for war expenses any longer. We have given general approval in circular telegrams to the use of income tax for meeting increased war expenditure, and in the changed circumstances I do not see any reason why we should refuse the request for a free vote on the measure. The matter is somewhat urgent as it has already been introduced in the Straits Settlements Legislative Council. There is nothing in the reply which we sent to those Unofficials in the F.M.S. who telegraphed direct to the S. of S. protesting against the proposed introduction of income tax in the F.M.S. which compromises this decision (see our reply at 65 on the 1940 file). I have sent a copy of the telegram to them to let me know whether they have any views. I don't expect that they will in fact have any, and am sending on a draft telegram in anticipation of this.

===== Page 10 =====
A fresh telegram here was sent in dealing with the F.I.S. Here, of course, apprehensive continuation on war taxation generally has been free strength there in the Settlements.

However, the question of income there is not raised in the latter text. Of the two points at A.V.B referred to the Sq.S., we cannot have any objection to the first and the record accounts with accepted policy.

Off here LOSH DENNON.

Mr. Evans

Horse General Department: any comments to make on this Ordinance which introduces Income Tax into the Straits Settlements for the first time since the years immediately following the last winter. The Secretary of State has already agreed in principle to the introduction of the tax and the Ordinance was passed by the Legislature. Co. G. within one dissolution (since through it and not go far enough).

Mr. Evans does not follow the "strike motion" laid down in the S.J.S.'s calendar of petitions in the first, that the introduction of income tax was a matter of great public interest locally and the measure was adopted by a Joint Committee of Members from the F.T.S. and the S.S. to meet local conditions.

A copy of the Ordinance shall be available, importantly the same E.T.R. Wheeler/Beattie with my copies.

W.B.C. mon.

P.690/29

No. 3. Q. 21 to note.

His request to the Ordinance in N.W. 177 the following points might be written:

(1). §2. Definition of "British Empire" does not cover Palestine.

(2). §7(4) appears a wide exception particularly if income from

===== Page 20 =====
Such laws' structure in the Empire is variable depending on the country of origin.

(c) 7. There was some difficulty experienced by the staff of the DoT in obtaining information over this issue; they drew allusions from U.K. law that they were given exemption under S6(1)(c).

(d) S.9. Employers have to give a return of their employees or premises.

(e) S.61. Official Secretary: Spanish authorities were given powers under this head to make decisions in the legislative council.

In general, the Ordinance does not strike me as a foolproof piece of legislation. I am not particularly clear how payment of tax on members, especially regarding the lower limit, can be invariably secured. However, it is a temporary measure, and I would be in favour of letting the S.S. Government proceed on a basis of trust and error with these manufactured articles.

Legal Clerk should see, and I Cause has also asked to see?

Santon Y.
1874.

There is nothing in the legal report which seems to call for comment.

As regards the definition of the British Empire, to which Mr. Monson refers, I doubt whether they intend to exclude Palestine. The definition is, I believe, taken from a model prepared for use only in customs legislation, and it does not cover Palestine because we are advised that Palestine should not be given the benefit of Empire preference on account of most-favoured-nation treaties. I believe there are circular despatches on this question.

In glancing through the Ordinance I noticed Section 51. I have seen similar sections in innumerable Ordinances, and Sir S. Abrahams has shown me several during the last week or two. This sort of omnibus provision may almost be regarded as common form. Nevertheless, it is open to objection and it is a matter for consideration whether it would not be a good thing to send a circular despatch.

The section here provides a general penalty for (1) a person guilty of an offence against the Ordinance; (2) a person who contravenes or fails to comply with any of the provisions of this Ordinance; and (3) a person who contravenes rules made under the Ordinance.

===== Page 21 =====
(1) Applies where other sections of the Ordinance provide in terms that certain acts or omissions shall constitute an offence, and is not open to objection.

(2), however, means that if anyone does not do what he is directed to do by the Ordinance he is technically guilty of an offence; see for instance Sections 3(2), 13(2) and 14 of this Ordinance. It is very much better to apply the general penalty only to (1) and it is unusual to find both (1) and (2) included.

(3) though occasionally found in Acts of Parliament is also open to criticism on the ground that the section imposes a penalty for offences the nature and seriousness of which cannot be known, and probably, as in the case of (2), penalises failure to observe directions in the rules where such failure should not be regarded as a criminal offence at all. The better course is for the enactment enabling rules to be made to give power to attach to the rules themselves penalties up to specified maxima.

(Sgd.) K.O. ROBERTS-WRAY,
19.5.41.

I think it is a great pity that the Straits have chosen to model their legislation on the inadequate ordinance which they passed in the last war instead of either on the model income tax or on some other ordinance such as the Ceylon Income Tax Ordinance which has worked in practice. I fear that the result will be simply another comparative failure leading to the withdrawal of the measure as soon as the war is over. I think also that it is a pity that they have not attempted to cope with the problem of personal and family allowances which is not really any more insoluble in Malaya than in other Eastern territories. This again will afford a further excuse for throwing the thing over in due course. It is symptomatic of the general attitude that exemptions should have been given in respect of income from war loans of any and every kind. This is, I think, unprecedented elsewhere in the Colonial Empire and has been found to be a most awkward door to evasion in the U.S. and elsewhere.

I think it might be useful to get the views of the Board of Inland Revenue and so I would send this ordinance to them saying that we propose to comment generally in the above sense and also to draw attention to the omission of Palestine in the definition of the British Empire, and should be glad to know whether the Board would suggest that any other observations should be made.

(Intld.) S.C.,
20.5.41.

The parallel F.M.S. Enactment is now registered at No. 32 - 27.3.41. It is virtually identical with the S.S. Ordinance.

(Sgd.) W.B.L. MONSON,
22.5.41.

(Sgd.) G.E.J. GENT,
27.5.41.

As regards the Colony's War Taxation Ordinance in 1777, it is designedly a temporary war tax and is the fruit of a great deal of compromise and heated controversy in the several parts and Governments in Malaya. It is not intended as a permanent form of Income Tax and was only accepted on that clear understanding. Comment of the sort Mr. Caine suggests would be fruitless (we could not use the official majority for the purpose of levying an income tax to obtain funds largely for presentation to H.M.G.), and it would be ungenerous both to the Governor, who has at last got this measure through with a fair amount of local agreement, and also to the unofficial members who have, with great misgivings, consented to it.

I see no objection to mentioning the point about Palestine in replying that H.M. will not be advised to disallow the Ordinance.

As to the Board of Inland Revenue, I would simply send them a copy of the Ordinance with a copy of the sanctioning despatch in reply.

(Sgd.) G.E.J. GENT,
27.5.41.

I agree with Mr. Gent.

(Intld.) A.C.B.,
27.5.41.

While I sympathise with Mr. Caine's view, I feel that in the circumstances explained by Mr. Gent, we can only proceed as he proposes.

===== Page 27 =====
It will be seen from this telegram that the Straits Settlements and F.M.S. Governments have followed up their introduction of income tax last year by somewhat increasing the rather low rates which were introduced then. The main changes appear to be that the lower limit of taxable income has been reduced to $3,000 from $4,800, and the rate of taxation on high incomes has been raised from 8% to 12%, and a surtax rate introduced as in para. 2(b) of this telegram. There is also interest to notice that although last year they decided that the introduction of a system of marriage allowances would be impossible having regard to the number of polygamous marriages existing in an oriental society, they have now introduced a special allowance of $2,000 to "individual residents". I am not sure of the significance of the word "individual", but I presume it means that a polygamous resident would receive an allowance in respect of his first wife only. Provision has also been made by the aggregate for tax purposes of incomes of husbands and wives as opposed to separate assessment which was introduced last year.

New taxation on incorporated companies has been introduced at a flat rate of 12%, which has also been adopted as the assessment of a non-resident individual on incomes arising or received in the Colony. There have been references in the press to the proposed new taxation of companies. The "Financial Times" reported that this tax would be paid by companies in Malaya wherever formed, and the Malayan Chamber of Mines has written to us in some alarm lest this will apply to tin companies registered in this country who are already paying U.K. rates of income tax and excess profits tax. The Governor's telegram is not quite clear on this point, but I should think that if there had been any intention of abolishing the provision under Section 7(b) of relief in respect of incomes on which income tax has been paid in another part of the British Empire, or indeed of removing the exemption because of payment of export duty on rubber or tin, he would have mentioned it in his telegram. We might, however, make sure of this by telegraphing as in d.h.

I agree to the
H. H. C. S. S. G. B. H.

It may be that (9) is an obligatory reference to this law.

===== Page 31 =====
My Lord,

With reference to Your Lordship's despatch Straits Settlements No. 207 dated the 24th October, 1941, transmitting a memorandum on certain questions arising out of the administration of the Straits Settlements War Tax Ordinance, 1941,

I have the honour to inform Your Lordship that the assumption of the Board of Inland Revenue in paragraph 6 of the memorandum is correct.

2. Under the War Tax (No. 2) Ordinance for 1942, companies incorporated in the United Kingdom are required to satisfy the Collector under Section 7(1)(b) that tax has been or will be paid in the United Kingdom on the income in question.

The alternatives proposed in paragraph 11 of the memorandum have been considered. It is preferred that the Inspector of Taxes should certify that United Kingdom tax has been paid on the income in question. Time is, and will be, allowed to the taxpayer until a reasonable interval after the following 1st January to obtain such a certificate.

19th December, 1941.

An Ordinance for imposing an additional duty on rubber for war purposes.

[22nd December, 1941].

It is hereby enacted by the Governor of the Straits Settlements with the advice and consent of the Legislative Council thereof as follows:

1. This Ordinance may be cited as the War Tax (Additional Rubber Duty) (No. 2) Ordinance, 1941.

2. (1) In addition to the duty imposed by section 8 of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, there shall be charged, levied, and paid on rubber produced in and to be exported from the Colony during the year 1942 a further duty of an amount equal to five per centum of the value of such rubber.

(2) The provisions of section 3 so far as they are applicable and of sections 9 to 14 of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, shall have effect mutatis mutandis in relation to the additional duty imposed by this section as if it were the duty imposed by section 8 of that Ordinance.

3. The net amount of the duty collected under this Ordinance after deduction of the expenses of and incidental to its collection, together with any fines and penalties imposed and paid in relation thereto, shall be applied to defraying the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the war and for no other purpose.

===== Page 34 =====
4. The operation of this Ordinance may be continued by resolution of the Legislative Council for a further period or periods of one year, and when any such resolution has been passed section 2 shall have effect as if for the year 1942 there were substituted the year mentioned in such resolution.

Passed this 8th day of December, 1941.

H. P. BRYSON,
Clerk of Councils.

No. 916 S.S. Confidential.

Your telegram No. 1725.

War Tax Bill 1942 section 7(1)(b) reads as follows:

"In calculating the income on which a Company or Association shall pay the tax or the income on which an individual shall pay the tax and surtax, a reduction shall be made in respect of the following:

(b) any income not arising in the Colony on which income tax has been paid by deduction or otherwise or on which it is proved to the satisfaction of the collector that income tax will be so paid in any other part of the British Empire at a rate not less than the appropriate rate of tax and surtax under this ordinance;"

It is proposed to add to this subsection the following:

"Provided any income arising or received in the Colony of a Company incorporated in the U.K. shall be allowed as deduction if it can be established to the satisfaction of the collector that income tax has been or will be paid on that income in the U.K."

The above subsection and proviso will appear in the F.M.S. Bill as well as the S.S. Bill.

The proposal to tax in the Colony all incomes arising in the Colony follows the recommendations in paragraph 9 of the report of Interdepartmental Colonial Office Committee on income tax, 1922.

Copies of the ordinance will be forwarded by fast air mail as soon as it is enacted.

16th October, 1941.

An Ordinance to amend the War Tax Ordinance, 1941, (No. 3 of 1941).

It is hereby enacted by the Governor of the Straits Settlements with the advice and consent of the Legislative Council thereof as follows:

1. This Ordinance may be cited as the War Tax (Amendment No. 2) Ordinance, 1941, and shall be read as one with the War Tax Ordinance, 1941, hereinafter referred to as the Principal Ordinance, and shall be deemed to have come into force on the 1st day of January, 1941.

2. Section 7 of the Principal Ordinance is hereby amended as follows:

(1) by adding after the word “Colony” in paragraph (a) thereof the words “or in the Federated Malay States or in any Malay State”;

(2) by deleting paragraph (g) thereof and substituting the following:

“(g) any income which a company, association or person enjoys as a member of a company or as a member of an association when such company or

===== Page 41 =====
Association is liable to the tax under this Ordinance or under the Federated Malay States War Taxation Enactment, 1941;

(3) by adding the following paragraphs at the end thereof—

“(n) Income arising from wounds or disability pensions paid from public funds to any person or past officer, non-commissioned officer or man of His Majesty’s Forces;

(o) Any income derived by a company, association or person whose principal place of business is not situate in the Colony, or in the Federated Malay States, or in any Malay State, from the sale in the Colony through consignees of rubber, copra, pepper, tin, tin ore, gambier, or sago flour produced or won by it or him outside the Colony or the Federated Malay States or any Malay State.”.

3. Section 15 of the Principal Ordinance is amended as follows—

(a) The words “arising or” are inserted after the word “income” in the seventh line thereof;

(b) The words “to arise or” are inserted after the word “and” in the seventh line thereof;

(c) The words “arising or” are inserted after the word “income” in the thirteenth line thereof;

(d) The words “arising or” are inserted after the word “income” in the twenty-first line thereof, and the “14th day of April, 1941” is substituted for the “1st day of April, 1941” in the twenty-third line thereof;

(e) The words “arising or” are inserted after the word “property” in the thirty-ninth line thereof.

4. The word “person” in sections 22, 23, 27, 28 and 29 of the Principal Ordinance shall unless the context otherwise requires include any body of persons corporate or unincorporate.

===== Page 42 =====
5. Section 25 of the Principal Ordinance is amended by deleting the word "trustees" in the third line thereof.

6. The following new section is inserted immediately after section 25 of the Principal Ordinance—

25A. (1) Every company which or person who acts as trustee of a deceased person's estate or of a settlement and has control, management, or custody of the income of such deceased person's estate or settlement shall furnish to the Collector in the prescribed form a true and full return of the amount of income received as trustee and in case it or he pays or credits any part thereof to any person entitled during the year of assessment to a share of the income shall deduct from the amount representing the share of income to which such person is so entitled the tax at the rate of eight per centum and shall within the prescribed time pay the sum deducted to the Collector, provided that the Collector may in any particular case direct that the tax is not to be deducted or is to be deducted at a rate to be specified by the Collector.

(2) Where there is no beneficiary or person entitled during the year of assessment to any part of the income of the estate of a deceased person or of a settlement, or where there is part of that income to which no beneficiary or person is so entitled, the trustee shall be assessed and chargeable with the tax on the income of the estate or settlement or on that part of that income as the case may be, as if it were the income of an individual.

(3) Where tax has been paid to the Collector under the provisions of subsection (1) of this section the trustee shall be entitled to deduct from any income that he pays, credits or distributes to any person the tax appropriate thereto and any such person to whom any such income has been paid, credited or distributed shall be entitled to a set-off against the tax payable by him of the amount of tax so deducted.

===== Page 43 =====
(4) For the purposes of this section the expressions "deceased person's estate" and "settlement" include any deed, will, agreement for a settlement, or other instrument or any number of instruments, under or by virtue of which any property or any interest therein stands for the time being limited to or in trust for any person or persons.

7. Section 61 of the Principal Ordinance is hereby amended by the addition of a new subsection as follows:

"(7) Nothing in this section shall apply to the printing or publishing of any judgment of the Commissioners or of the Court of Appeal in any bona fide series of law reports, or in any publication of a technical character bona fide intended for circulation among members of the legal profession, but the name of an appellant, other than the Collector, shall not be so printed or published."

Passed this 13th day of October, 1941.

H. P. BRYSON,
Clerk of Councils.

FROM STRAITS SETTLEMENTS

FROM: Governor (Sir S. Thomas)
TO: Secretary of State for the Colonies.

D. 12th November, 1941.
R. 12th November, 17.50 hrs.

No. 848 S.S.

War Tax Bill for 1942 was read the first time in the Legislative Council on 5th November.

8. Main features which distinguish the bill from 1941 Ordinance are as follows:

(a) Revision of Schedule A so that scale extends from a minimum of 2% on incomes not less than $5,000 to a maximum of 12% on incomes not less than $20,000.

(b) Surtax as follows:
First $20,000 of total income nil.
Next $5,000 5%.
Next $5,000 10%.
Next $5,000 15%.
Next $15,000 18%.
Remainder 20%.

Provided that if taxable income is different from total income surtax payable will bear the same proportion to surtax calculated on total income as taxable income bears to total income.

(c) A marriage allowance of $2,000 to individual residents of the Colony or in the Federated Malay States.

(d) Aggregation for tax purposes of incomes of husbands and wives.

(e) Assessment of a partner of a business individually on his share of the partnership income.

(f) Assessment of a non-resident individual on income arising or received in the Colony at a flat rate of 12%.

(g) Taxation in the Colony of all incomes arising in the Colony.

(h) Taxation of incorporated companies at a flat rate of 12%.

5. The bill will be read second time and passed through all subsequent stages on 8th December.

4. A bill with the same rates and similar to the Colony bill except for minor differences will be introduced into the Federal Council on 18th November.

5. I do not expect any serious opposition.

Report on an Ordinance to amend the War Tax Ordinance, 1941 (No. 3 of 1941).

[No. 35 of 1941.]

The old definition of company, which was in similar terms to that contained in the previous War Tax Ordinance, 1919, was unsatisfactory in that it included only companies with a share capital divided into shares. This was by no means exhaustive, particularly in the case of foreign companies. It was also doubtful whether the old definition included a corporation sole.

No. 117.

Establishment Branch.
14th October, 1941.

===== Page 57 =====
T.2015/17/41.

Dear Farmer,

1. Three questions have arisen on that rather obscure measure, the Straits Settlements War Tax Ordinance, 1941, all of which can be dealt with most conveniently in one letter. If the matter is outside your province perhaps you would pass the letter on.

2. The first question, raised in your Department's official letter 50640/41 of the 23rd September, is whether this Straits Settlements tax is regarded as corresponding to United Kingdom Income Tax for the purposes of Section 27 of the Finance Act, 1920. The short answer to this question is Yes; we do regard it as an Income Tax in respect of which relief from our tax is admissible in principle.

3. The second question is more complicated and relates to the treatment under the Ordinance of dividends from Straits Settlements companies. The scheme of the Ordinance is to charge tax on "income" at a rate which depends on "total income" (Section 5). In the case of an individual, to speak of individuals only, "income" includes dividends arising in or brought into the Colony. "Total income" includes all dividends, wherever arising, and also includes "any sum paid in respect of ... any tax under this Ordinance" (Section 2). In calculating the income on which a person is to pay the tax a deduction is made of any income enjoyed "as a member of a company or association when such company or association is liable to the tax" (paragraph (g) of Section 7). Section 45 provides for refunds where tax "was paid in excess of the amount which is liable to be paid".

4. The Ordinance contains no provision for deduction of tax from dividends, as Section 7(g) produces a not dissimilar result by other means. It is clear, however, that an individual whose proper rate of tax was, say, 2% might receive a dividend from a Straits Settlements Company which had paid tax at, say, 8%. The first point, therefore, on which we seek information is whether the tax paid by the Company is available for repayment to the individual, whether under Section 45 or otherwise, i.e., whether the shareholder in the example is entitled to recover the difference of 6%.

F. Farmer, Esq.

===== Page 58 =====
5. This first point affects all recipients of dividends from Straits Settlements companies, but there is a further point which is of interest particularly to United Kingdom residents. Under paragraph (b) of Section 7, a deduction is granted from the income on which tax is to be paid of "any income on which Income Tax has been paid or on which it is proved to the satisfaction of the Collector Income Tax will be paid in any other part of the British Empire at a rate not less than the rate specified in Schedule A." If the answer to the first point is Yes, will a United Kingdom resident (who will pay United Kingdom tax on his dividends) be entitled to repayment of the whole of the tax appropriate to the dividend? If so, he will in the end bear no Straits Settlements tax at all and no claim to relief will arise under Section 27 of the Finance Act, 1920. If not, he will have borne a measure of Straits Settlements tax notwithstanding the general intention of the Colonial Government to refrain from taxing income which is taxable in the United Kingdom. (The shareholder, of course, will get the tax back by way of relief under Section 27 of the Finance Act, 1920, and I ought to add that we do not for a moment challenge the right of the Straits Settlements to tax dividends going to United Kingdom residents.)

6. Probably the correct interpretation of the law is that the shareholder has no right to repayment of tax paid by a company on income which it distributes in dividends, and that this is so whether or not the income will be liable to tax in the United Kingdom. The matter, however, is not free from doubt and can only be cleared up finally by enquiry of the Colonial Authorities. If you could do this for us we should be very grateful.

7. You might refer to your correspondence with Owen of the Treasury on the case of the Raefirth Estates in December and January last. This, you will remember, was concerned with a special export duty on rubber in the Federated Malay States. The legislation imposing that duty contains a similar exemption where the producer pays Income Tax in the United Kingdom or elsewhere in the Empire; but the Federated Malay States Authorities held that where the export duty was paid by a partnership in Malaya, and United Kingdom Income Tax was paid by the individual partners (who were resident in this country) on their "dividends" from the partnership, the exemption did not apply, since the export duty and the Income Tax were not paid by the

===== Page 59 =====
Same person. I believe the legislation in the Federated Malay States and the Straits Settlements is similar, if not word for word the same; and very likely the Straits Settlements Authorities will take the same line as regards the Income Tax exemption as the Federated Malay States people did on the rubber duty exemption.

8. Our last question arises on paragraph (b) of Section 7, already referred to. Substantially that paragraph exempts income which is liable to tax elsewhere in the British Empire, and if the income liable to Straits Settlements tax falls wholly within this class, there is no necessity to file a return (Section 12).

9. The Chartered Bank of India, Australia and China is a Company resident in the United Kingdom and, as such, liable to United Kingdom tax on its profits from all sources, including the Straits Settlements. In principle, therefore, it is quite clearly entitled to complete relief from Straits Settlements tax on the profits it makes in the Colony. It accordingly asked its Inspector of Taxes for a certificate to satisfy the Singapore authorities as to its title to relief. The Inspector gave a certificate to the effect that it was "liable to United Kingdom Income Tax at the standard rate of tax on the whole of its income, including any income arising in the Straits Settlements or Federated Malay States."

10. The subsequent steps are shown in the enclosed copy of a letter from the Bank to the Inspector. It seems that the Collector in Singapore insists on a certificate to the effect that United Kingdom tax will be paid on the income arising in the Colony, failing which the Bank will be put to the trouble of making a return, paying the tax, and subsequently reclaiming the whole amount. Now the Company's income on which any colonial assessment for 1941 will be based is the income of the Company's year to 31st December 1940. These profits come into assessment in the United Kingdom for 1941/42 and the tax on them will not be payable till 1st January 1942; so a certificate that tax has been paid will not be available until after that date.

11. I don't know whether Singapore would accept a certificate that tax "will be paid" from the taxpayer himself, but we do not think we can instruct our Inspectors to give certificates in such terms. It seems to us that the Collector at Singapore is a little rigid in his attitude.

===== Page 60 =====
He has the law on his side but it is worth noticing that the wording of the Straits Settlements Ordinance differs both from Section 27 of the Finance Act, 1920, and from Clauses 46 and 47 of the Model Ordinance (reciprocal relief in the Colony). All these provisions grant relief on proof that the taxpayer has paid or is liable to pay tax in the other country. In the circumstances, would you be prepared to advise the Singapore authorities to accept certificates from Inspectors of Taxes to the effect that a person was "liable to pay" United Kingdom tax on the income arising in the Straits Settlements; or, alternatively, to take no steps towards obtaining a return or making an assessment until a reasonable time after the following 1st January, when the Inspector of Taxes would normally be in a position to certify that United Kingdom tax had, in fact, been paid on the income in question. The first alternative is preferable—both because it is simpler (it might not be necessary to insist on a fresh certificate every year) and also because it sometimes happens that the final settlement of the United Kingdom liability cannot be achieved until some time, or even a long time, after 1st January.

Yours sincerely,

R. Wim

===== Page 61 =====

23rd August, 1941.

MR. Dear Howell,

MR. It is a long time since I have bothered you with any "points" but you may like to consider this one.

Out of curiosity more than anything else I looked at the Stamp Ordinance (Cap. 228) to see what was the effect of Section 4 of the War Tax (Additional Duties) Ordinance, 1941, and I am not sure that section says quite what it means. Naturally I have not given Cap. 228 more than a cursory glance, but have found the phrase "chargeable with the duty of 4 cents" in Sections 45(1), 47(a) and 66. The effect of the new Section 34A(1) of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, on Section 45(1) is that the latter now applies to Bills of Exchange under item 14 of Cap. 228 as well as those under item 13 and I presume this is intended. The amendment also puts right Section 34A as regards Section 66, the previous section being incorrect if, as I gather is the case, duty on proxies had not been altered. The effect of the new Section 34A(1) of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, on Section 47(a), however, is that it will now read - "where the instrument, not being an instrument chargeable with stamp duty only etc.". This does not look right to me.

With kindest regards to Mrs. Howell and yourself.

I hope you are both keeping fit.

The Honourable C.G. Howell.

An Ordinance to increase certain duties to provide for local war expenditure.

[2nd May, 1941].

It is hereby enacted by the Governor of the Straits Settlements with the advice and consent of the Legislative Council thereof as follows:

1. This Ordinance may be cited as the War Tax (Additional Duties) Ordinance, 1941.

2. The Second Schedule to the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, shall have effect as though the proper stamp duty indicated in this section were substituted for the proper stamp duty indicated in such Second Schedule in respect of the following items therein—

No. 14. Bill of Exchange—of any other kind whatsoever (except a cheque and a Promissory Note).

10 cents for every $100 and for every fractional part of $100 of the amount or value of the money for which the Bill is drawn or made.

Item 23. Cheque... 8 cents.

Item 26. Conveyance, Assignment or Transfer—

(a) On sale of any property (except stock and marketable securities).

1 per centum of the consideration for the conveyance, assignment or transfer.

===== Page 77 =====
Provided that there shall be appropriated to His Majesty’s Government in the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland as part of the contribution contemplated under the provisions of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, an amount not less than the amount by which the duties on the instruments specified in this section were increased by the Second Schedule to the War Duties Ordinance, 1940.

3.—(1) In addition to the duty payable under the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, and under section 30(1) of the Stamp Ordinance on every bet made on any totalisator or pari-mutuel promoted by any racing club or association exempted under section 15 of the Betting Ordinance, there shall be charged, levied and paid a further duty of 2½ per centum of the amount paid.

(2) In addition to the duty payable under the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, and under section 30(1) of the Stamp Ordinance on every contribution towards any sweepstake promoted by any racing club or association, there shall be charged, levied and paid a further duty of five per centum of the amount paid or contributed.

(3) In addition to the duty payable under the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, and under section 30A(1) of the Stamp Ordinance on every sweepstake promoted by any club or association or society on the result of a horse or other race (whether such race takes place in or out of the Colony), there shall be charged, levied and paid a further duty of five per centum of the amount paid or contributed.

4. Subsection (1) of section 34A of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940 is hereby repealed and the following subsection substituted therefor—

“(1) The Stamp Ordinance shall have effect as though for the phrase “chargeable with the duty of four cents” there were substituted the phrase “chargeable with stamp duty.”

Passed this 28th day of April, 1941.

H. P. BRYSON,

An Enactment to amend the War Taxation Enactment 1940.

T. S. W. THOMAS, [26th February, 1941.]
President of the Federal Council.

It is hereby enacted by the Rulers of the Federated Malay States by and with the advice and consent of the Federal Council as follows:

1. This Enactment may be cited as the War Taxation Enactment 1940 (Amendment) Enactment 1941 and shall be read and construed as one with the War Taxation Enactment 1940 hereinafter referred to as the Principal Enactment.

2. In the Preamble to the Principal Enactment the first reciting paragraph is amended by deleting the colon at the end thereof and by adding the words, "and that certain expenses be incurred within the Federated Malay States in the prosecution of the said war" and the second reciting paragraph is amended by deleting the words "the purpose" and by substituting therefor the words "these purposes" and by deleting the words "of such contribution".

3. A new section is added to the Principal Enactment immediately following section 10 and numbered and worded as follows:

"11. (i) The amounts of the taxes imposed under this Enactment in respect of the year 1940 shall be paid to His Britannic Majesty’s Government as a contribution from the Federated Malay States towards the expenses of the war in which the British Empire is engaged."

===== Page 82 =====
(ii) The amounts of the taxes imposed under this Enactment in respect of any period after the 31st day of December, 1940, during which this Enactment remains in force shall be applied to defraying the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the said war and for no other purposes: Provided that such amounts or any of them or any part of them may be paid direct to the Government of His Britannic Majesty or may be reserved to defray such war expenditure within the Federated Malay States as the Federal Council may from time to time resolve.

Passed this 30th day of January, 1941.

J. F. HANNYNGTON,
Clerk of Council.

===== Page 83 =====
Report for the Secretary of State
On Federated Malay States Enactment
No. 4 of 1941.

Enactment
The War Taxation (Amendment) Enactment 1941.

This Enactment amends the War Taxation Enactment 1940 (No. 6 of 1940). When that Enactment was introduced, the intention was to contribute the total proceeds of all war taxation to His Britannic Majesty's Government direct.

The increasing needs of local defence, both active and passive, many of which have to be paid for by the Federal Government, have made it essential that special war taxation should also be imposed for defraying local expenditure.

The new section 11 leaves it to the Federal Council to decide what proportion of the total amount collected shall be paid over to His Britannic Majesty's Government and what proportion must be reserved to meet extraordinary local expenditure necessitated by the war. Approval of this amendment was contained in a telegram from the Secretary of State No. 102 of 1941 of 23rd January 1941.

Legal Adviser,
Federated Malay States.

Kuala Lumpur,
23rd April, 1941.

===== Page 84 =====
be read a first time.

This amendment is a little difficult to follow unless you have before you three different Ordinances, at least two Ordinances and this Bill. The amendment deals with one of the grounds for nullity of marriage. One ground is when the former husband or wife of one of the parties to a subsequent marriage is still alive, and by the amendment which we passed in this Council in 1939 we provided that marriage under that section should cover not only a monogamous marriage but also a marriage contracted under a recognised system of polygamous marriage.

The difficulty which has been pointed out is that by section 3 of the Divorce Ordinance the Courts here are enjoined to apply principles in granting relief as near as may be similar to those in force in England. There is the possibility of a conflict between those two provisions because on this point the law in England differs from that which is in force in the Colony, and for the removal of doubts this clause is being inserted, which appears as clause 2 of the Bill.

I move that the Bill be read a first time.

The Colonial Secretary:—Sir, I rise to second the motion.

The Bill was read a first time.

The Attorney-General:—Sir, I beg to give notice that at the next meeting of Council I shall move the subsequent stages of the Bill.

CHRISTIAN MARRIAGE (AMENDMENT) BILL.

The Attorney-General:—Sir, I move that the Christian Marriage (Amendment) Bill be read a first time.

This Bill is, to some extent, complementary to the Bill which has just been read a first time and incorporates in the Christian Marriage Ordinance some provisions which are already set out in the same terms in the Civil Marriage Ordinance. It was thought unnecessary to incorporate them in the Christian Marriage Ordinance because they are merely declaratory of what is thought to be the law already, but we have been asked to reconsider this point in view of possible doubt and this Bill incorporates in the Christian Marriage Ordinance similar provisions in regard to prior polygamous marriages as already exist in the Civil Marriage Ordinance. The other amendments are formal.

I move that the Bill be read a first time.

The Colonial Secretary:—Sir, I rise to second the motion.
