===== Page 85 =====
WAR TAX BILL.

The Financial Secretary: Sir, I move that the War Tax Bill be read a second time.

The Colonial Secretary: Sir, I rise to second the motion.

Mr. F. D. Bisseker: Sir, I rise with mixed feelings. There is in me the inherent desire, which lies at the back of the mind of every true Briton, to support and co-operate with the administration—a desire, quite naturally, greatly enhanced in these days when there should be complete unanimity; but even stronger than this on the present occasion is the conviction that the Bill which is before us today for its second reading is bad legislation.

This War Tax Ordinance, 1941, is so incomplete, has such glaring anomalies and such obvious omissions and, consequently, is so un-British in its makeup, I am unable to comprehend how it could ever have come before a Legislature of the British Colonial Empire. It taxes the few and leaves out of consideration large sections of the population well able to contribute their share towards the expenses of the War. It favours certain sections of the community to the detriment of the interests of others and, when attention is drawn to this inequitable state of affairs, one is naively informed that it has not been found possible to devise legislation fair to all concerned, so that some are bound to suffer. I refer specifically to partnerships, and as an example of the most amazing inequality of treatment which the passing into law of this Bill would bring about I cite the junior partner in a medical or legal firm in comparison with a Government servant in the legal or medical departments of Government. The omissions are numerous but I have no intention of wasting time by detailing a lot of them, neither have I done this in the case of the anomalies, but my point can be made merely by drawing attention to the lack of allowances for married men with families and to the failure to provide adequate allowances for wear and tear and obsolescence of plant and machinery for enterprises of an industrial nature.

As regards the administrative side, it has been made clear for a long while that Government has held the opinion that Income Tax is the only form of taxation which can be introduced in order to place Malaya’s financial effort on its proper basis. Public opinion in the form of numerous representations made by responsible public bodies and associations as well as by private individuals has not agreed so, having made its attitude clear, Government went no further and it was evident that public opinion received its due in consideration. It appeared, consequently, that the policy of the administration was not in any way autocratic but, suddenly, after inexplicable delay, the Bill which is before us

===== Page 86 =====
To-day is launched, this constituting a complete volte face on the part of Government as it is still so evident that this type of taxation is not by any means acceptable to the majority. It seems, therefore, that the administration has had no clear-cut policy but if, as has been recently claimed, Government has always been determined upon this form of Tax, why was it not introduced at least a year ago so that by now funds would have been accumulating towards the expenses of the War? But instead of prompt action, after all these months of procrastination, there now comes this attempt to rush through this obvious stop-gap measure, which will not produce money for a long time, without anything like sufficient time and opportunity being afforded those more concerned with it even than is Government, in that they have to do the paying, afforded to those who need to discuss and consider a matter of such far-reaching importance with their Principals who, in many cases, reside abroad. I wonder if Government is aware that in 1939, when only amendments to the tax in India were contemplated, even after Income Tax had been in existence for many years, Government there obtained the services of two Somerset House experts who worked for months on the details and that thereafter the amendments were under consideration by a Select Committee, upon whose findings adequate time was provided for deliberations by various bodies representative of mercantile and industrial opinion. And yet here there is the intention to impose the tax which is before us to-day, not only without first-class expert advice but also without giving those who are so directly concerned an opportunity of making representations in regard to their views. In the light of the long period the administration has had to devise and put before this Legislature taxation of a reasonable nature, the present action is truly incomprehensible.

As I see the position, there are just two things which this country can do in order to assist to its maximum the united Empire effort which is being made to annihilate those powers whose object it is to dominate mankind. We must do all we can to defend ourselves and we must pay. Of the first of these duties I need make no further mention now and as regards the latter, since the War commenced it has been made evident that this country is willing to pay to its capacity. This determination has been given life by private enterprise and by the force of public opinion in the forms of the newspaper collections and the Loan. There have been no whisperings or murmurings from the Home Country that Malaya has fallen below expectations in this regard. On the contrary there have been public expressions of admiration for what Malaya has done financially up to the present and all of us have had private communications which have expressed the same feelings. What has been accomplished furnishes very emphatic rebuttal of the most regrettable implications which are forthcoming from some quarters that the country does not want to pay. Such implications are, to say the least, deplorable. There have been innumerable assertions that the country is ready to pay and it is only because Government has so far failed to produce measures of a satisfactory nature that this determination has had to be carried out by the private enterprise and the force of public opinion of which I have made mention. It is only natural that efforts of this nature must be limited in their scope and so the country, having done so well up to now by the means I have just mentioned, has been looking to Government to continue the good work. Government has had sixteen months of opportunity in which to consider this situation while Malaya has been maintaining its reputation practically entirely by means other than taxation and now, at the end of this period, all that Government can place before us as a means of continuing the financial effort is this incomplete and pathetically inept tax.

We here, in this Legislature are responsible to-day either for bringing the tax into effect or for rejecting it. It has been the expectation, not only of those within the country but also of those outside, interestedly watching for the birth of the official Malayan financial effort, that a strong and healthy child would be the outcome, instead of the ricketty anaemic offspring now before us. After all

===== Page 87 =====
Our expressions of determination, with all our wealth and with the growing strength of this virile young country, there cannot be satisfaction with the effort which this tax represents. Consequently, we here today, the members of this Council, each individually shouldering his responsibility to the general public, should reject this tax and, in so doing, show our determination to pass into law only such a tax as will be truly representative of the effort of which we are capable. We should remain unsatisfied until we are able, as the parents of the effort, to show the world a lusty infant, confirming to all that our expressions of determination to aid the Empire's War Effort to our utmost have been no idle boast.

I am not against Income Tax as such—indeed it is the obvious and best form of taxation given reasonable circumstances, but I am much against the imposition of a general tax of this nature in such a country as this. That a British Legislature should impose a form of taxation so evidently against the wishes of the majority makes the back hair begin to tingle, and that attempts should be made to collect an Income Tax from a community whose accounts are kept in such a manner as to make it impossible to differentiate between capital, profit, bad debts and credits indicates lack of appreciation of the circumstances. If an imposition of this nature should be finally decided upon, it will point to there being something wrong somewhere with the consequent searching round to find out what this is and the inevitable conclusion—the consideration of methods of control of affairs of a nature preventing a repetition of the imposition of undesirable legislation.

I am with those who insist upon raising all the money possible that this country can provide for the prosecution of the War. The War Tax Ordinance, 1941, does not even begin to accomplish this and never can do and I propose, consequently, as alternatives a graded tax on Property as this is so easily based upon recorded values and, finally, I would have also a graded tax on Rents. But in case Government insists upon its one desire I see nothing whatever against Income Tax upon those sections of the community who are amenable and accustomed to this form of taxation. There are those to whom it is second nature and although I hesitate to say "they like it" they certainly understand it and will accept it as bringing them into line with the effort which their fellow countrymen are making elsewhere. To these sections of the community, it is possible to introduce this type of taxation in a form which will be fair to all concerned as there will be no necessity to give consideration to those influences which affect the general proposal, due to the attempt to apply it to other communities. Furthermore, with the introduction of such a tax applied to those communities whose normal activities will not be unduly upset, a proper scale of rates can be put into effect instead of the scale now proposed. The rate should be considerably higher on big incomes and large profits with a just scale of allowances and deductions and in making this suggestion, I am strongly in favour of reducing the proposed minimum from four thousand eight hundred to three thousand dollars annually. Payers of Income Tax under such a scheme should be free of the graded Property and Rents Taxes. If these suggestions were adopted and Income Tax for the duration of the War imposed upon the amenable communities, it could so easily be withdrawn when hostilities cease and those who had paid could then come under the Property and Rent Taxes regraded in order to meet peacetime requirements—as surely it is better in a country such as this that the majority should provide the necessary revenue rather than the few.

In considering these proposals I should like it to be borne in mind that the British in an International Settlement on foreign soil further East have instituted a self-imposed Income Tax which has normal regulations. I have it on the best authority that the scheme works satisfactorily. To my certain knowledge there must be many seriously handicapped in their business activities in shouldering this obligation, but they do not consider the advantage presented to their competitors of other nationalities; their country's need comes first. With this

===== Page 88 =====
Example before us, there can be nothing here against the institution of an Income Tax for Europeans living and working vis-a-vis Asiatics not paying this form of tax and, in any case, I do not see why in wartime we should individually worry a scrap about what the next man is paying. It is up to each one of us to pay all we can—regardless.

I have remarked already the tax before us touches the few and leaves out a vast majority of the population. This is war and all are affected, and I see no reason, therefore, why all should not help in attaining victory as all will be equally affected by defeat. My proposals are all-embracing and, as I consider the suggestions which I have to make, it seems to me that they would cover the entire population—even the poorest—but they could be omitted from a graded Rent Tax by setting a certain minimum.

There are a thousand loopholes in what I suggest and there can be unlimited criticisms but at least the same applies to the tax before us and, as it has been shown it is not possible to institute an equitable form of tax upon incomes and profits in a country such as this with its varying communities, I hold the belief that, in these days when the means to victory is the one consideration, there is justification for introducing different types of taxation for different communities as long as the form of taxation used is fair in its incidence to the particular community which it touches. If experts are employed they can assist in adjusting the details of my proposals.

In wartime strength must count and I think it would be more than a pity—in fact, it would be disastrous—to inflict a form of taxation which would create disunity and antagonism towards authority as well as general dissatisfaction and disappointment with the war effort. With these forms of taxation I have proposed, in effect, every man in this country would be able to throw out his chest and say "I am doing my bit" and what better atmosphere than this can be created?

It is necessary for me to make it clear that my opinions are not essentially the views of the body which I represent on this Council. I hold no mandate from the Penang Chamber of Commerce to speak as I have done but I am required, as a result of a general meeting of the Chamber, to state that, while of the very definite opinion that Income Tax as a means of raising revenue is objectionable as applied to the peculiar conditions of this Colony with its cosmopolitan population, the Chamber fully recognizes, and indeed has always done so, the urgency of the need for additional taxation as a contribution to the Empire War needs. Consequently, if the imposition of a War Tax on Incomes is a matter of Government policy in preference to any alternative methods of taxation, the Chamber gives its general support to the introduction of such a Tax on the very clear understanding that its application will not be continued for more than one year after the cessation of hostilities and that such a Tax shall not be imposed except simultaneously with the adoption of a similar Tax in the Federated Malay States.

These conditions plainly show that the Penang Chamber of Commerce favors a tax of this nature solely as a War measure, and one of the conditions conveys the impression which it is intended shall be conveyed that there should be greater unification within this country, at any rate during the continuation of hostilities, than is the case at the present time. Your Excellency has been pleased to give most welcome assurances about the existing system in so far as Defence is concerned. The Chamber looks forward to the time when similar assurances can be given about the system as a whole—a time when by greater unification the serious delays upon matters of paramount importance to the industrial and mercantile communities will no longer be experienced.

I doubt if there is one single member present here today who is satisfied that this measure is good and sound legislation and I say without fear of contradiction in any quarter that it is entirely inadequate. In the circumstances, the administration owes us a better measure than this as a means of contributing our share.

===== Page 89 =====
To the Empire's effort, let each one of us here have the courage of his convictions and not permit the administration to involve us in such an unsatisfactory legislative measure. We shall be responsible if it goes on the Statute Book, so let us have nothing to do with this Bill in the interests of the future and demand that full consideration be given to the suggestions I have made as being more all-embracing and more truly representative of what we are able to do. While consideration is being given to my proposals—and as money is so urgently needed—more quickly than it can be provided by this Bill, let us replace the ten million dollars which it was hoped to raise in this Colony during 1941 by this tax by a Loan for the same amount, which I am confident would be fully subscribed in a short space of time if it were announced that Government is giving consideration to forms of taxation which will be in no way half-hearted but truly representative of our determination and ability to pay. (Applause).

Dr. Lim Han Hoe: Sir, I have not come here this morning with any prepared speech, but rather to see what other members of this Council would have to say.

I was a member of the first War Tax Committee which Your Excellency appointed to go into the question of increased taxation for raising funds for the prosecution of the war. The first Committee—on which I sat—did not originally consider the question of a tax on incomes but rather certain forms of indirect taxation which we thought would be applicable to Malaya. The result of these additional taxes did not meet what we all thought was the maximum effort that this country should make for the prosecution of the war and to help the mother-country.

A second Committee was appointed by Your Excellency to consider a loan. That secured a loan of $25,000,000 together with some two or three million dollars in War Saving Certificates.

Now, as the result of a third Committee, we have before us the recommendation of that Committee: this Bill. I feel, Sir, this Bill is so important that it would not suffer from a further expression of views. As we all know, the pros and cons have been debated for months both in the Press and elsewhere, but I feel, Sir, the Government would wish to hear as completely as possible views expressed from all public bodies and responsible associations.

In view of the importance of this Bill before Council today, the Chinese Chamber of Commerce called a further meeting on Friday last at which my hon. friend, the Junior Chinese member for Singapore, and I were present. We felt it our duty to learn and obtain as much information as we could in order that we may best interpret views of a large section of the Chinese people, particularly the Chinese merchants and traders who would be affected by the imposition of this tax. My assessment of their opinions, Sir, is that we all—and the Chinese community—are willing to pay any additional tax for the further prosecution of this war and to secure as big and maximum an effort as this Colony can make. But, Sir, they voiced certain misapprehensions, and their misapprehensions and fears have been the result of past experience with the previous War Tax Ordinance in 1917. The chief fear and dislike was the result of inquisition. Although they said they are not opposed to inquisition, they fear unnecessary, irksome inquisition. Inquisition as they experienced in the past has caused difficulty in their trade and wasted time, particularly with the middle-class and smaller merchants. It was pointed out, Sir, that the big companies had all their accounts accepted without inquisition, and as they go down further in scale the inquisition seems to increase; in other words,

===== Page 90 =====
The smaller the man, the more the inquisition. This is, of course, an administrative point and seems to me does not affect the principle of this Bill. It is a matter for the removing of these difficulties rather than to reject this Bill.

There were certain other points and suggestions with which I have agreed, and which my hon. friend, the Junior member, will suggest to Government.

My own view, Sir, is this: that this Bill has been designed to impose a tax on those who are best able to bear it. The principle depends upon the capacity to pay, leaving a limited amount below which it is felt would be sufficient for ordinary living requirements. I feel, Sir, that the principle is right and that this Bill should be supported.

There has been some talk about no taxation without representation. There has been some talk that this Bill will cause some surrender of the individual liberty. I feel, Sir, that at a time such as this, in this hour of trial, I cannot withhold my consent to the passage of this Bill. As citizens of the British Empire we have a responsibility. The essence of citizenship is responsibility—a responsibility born out of freedom. I agree to this Bill not because I value political and economic democracy less but because I value victory over aggression more. (Applause).

Mr. Khoo Sian Ewe:—Sir, I feel it is incumbent upon me to call attention to the existence of a strong feeling against the introduction of income tax, among Chinese commercial interests in Penang. Such opposition to the proposed measure is not due to any reluctance to render financial aid to the mother-country, but on account of the unsuitability of income tax to the Colony.

Speaking as a private individual, I sympathise with the views of the oppositionists, but in my capacity as a member of this Council I have to pay due regard to the larger and more vital interests of the Colony as an integral part of the Empire. That being so, I am impressed by the fact that the Empire is now engaged in a mortal struggle with the forces of evil. Self-preservation is the first law of nature and in obedience to the dictates of such law the Colony must be prepared to repel any possible aggression.

Since funds are urgently needed for our own defence, I am inclined to support the Bill. (Applause).

Mr. Tay Lian Teck:—Sir, I wish at the outset to say that the Chinese community do not object to taxation for the purpose of raising money as the Colony's contribution for war purposes. What they do object to is the Government's idea of the method by which this money should be raised—i.e., by war tax on incomes.

It is evident from the proceedings of the meeting of the Federal Council held on the 30th January that Government favours the imposition of a War Tax on Incomes. This being so, and as the Chinese merchants both alien and British subjects have no direct representation in this Council, I consider that it is not out of order for me to record the protest of the Chinese mercantile community that they do not consider war tax on incomes the proper method for raising money for war purposes.

The reasons against the imposition of a war tax on incomes were clearly set out in their petition to the Secretary of State for the Colonies and I think no useful purpose would be served by a repetition of them.

Having placed on record the protest of the Chinese merchants, I desire now to make some observations on this Bill which, I think, have the support of many leading Chinese merchants.

The Financial Secretary in introducing the War Tax Bill in Council on the 20th January gave a very clear explanation as to the reasons why direct taxation should be introduced. The Committee appointed to consider whether the Colony should raise a loan recommended the floatation of a $25 million loan bearing interest at 3% and also the issue of War Savings Certificates. To service this loan and also provide for redemption of these War Savings

===== Page 91 =====
Certificates, this Loan Committee recommended a war tax on incomes. I think I am correct in saying that the object of the Colony Loan Committee, of which I was a member, in making this recommendation was that as soon as the present war is terminated, income tax need only be continued for a limited period of years and would be discontinued as soon as a sufficient sum of money has been collected through income tax to service this loan and also provide for the redemption of War Savings Certificates. To make this point clear I offer the following explanation.

I believe that to service the present Colony $25 million loan, the sum of $1½ million annually for sinking fund and $3¼ million annually for interest would be required. If, therefore, by the imposition of income tax, the Colony should be able to raise say $10 million, then a sum of approximately $7¾ million will be the amount available as the Colony's contribution for war purposes. Assuming that the war should end three years hence and that the amount collected from war tax on incomes remains stationary at $10 million per year for these three years, then at the end of the three years we would have contributed $23¼ million for war purposes and also created a sinking fund of $4½ million in respect of the Colony $25 million loan. This means that we have still to find $20½ million for the repayment of this loan in 1952 and a further sum of say $5½ million for the redemption of War Savings Certificates (assuming the sale of War Savings Certificates reach that figure at the time of termination of the war), making a total of say $26 million. Then, according to the recommendation of the Colony Loan Committee, war tax on incomes need only be continued for such period after the termination of hostilities as would be found necessary in order to raise sufficient money from income tax to pay off this $26 million.

As there is a fear that income tax when introduced will come to stay forever, I ask that either in the preamble to the War Tax Bill this point should be stated clearly in unequivocal terms, namely, that the introduction of the War Tax Bill is for the purpose of servicing the Colony $25 million loan and provide for the redemption of War Savings Certificates in accordance with the recommendation of the Colony Loan Committee, or you, Sir, give this Council an assurance on this point today. This assurance would make it quite clear to future administrators of this Colony that the present members of this Council agreed to the imposition of a war tax on incomes purely as a war measure and thereafter for a limited period of time only.

In the course of discussions with Chinese merchants, it was pointed out to me that in the Hongkong War Revenue Ordinance, 1940, clause 61 states clearly that no tax shall be collectable in respect of any year of assessment in which the war which began on 3rd September, 1939, is terminated, and therefore the assurance I ask cannot be considered unreasonable.

My next point is that the Bill before this Council today provides for a maximum rate of 8% and as there are fears that this rate might be increased at a later date to a rate that might be unreasonably high (as was the case of Estate Duty when the maximum rate was trebled to 60%), I ask Government, if in the future it is considered necessary to increase this rate, to leave this decision to the vote of the Unofficials.

Thirdly, I am informed by one whose source of information I have no reason to doubt, that the Government has received cabled instructions from the Secretary of State for the Colonies to introduce a scale of Supertax on individual incomes and/or a scale for Excess Profits Tax on profits of companies and partnerships some time after this War Tax Bill is approved by this Council. If my information is correct then I suggest to you, Sir, that it is not reasonable to introduce Supertax or Excess Profits Tax as an addition to war tax on incomes in this Colony. I hope, however, that my informant is wrong and I ask Government to give me an assurance that with the passing of this War Tax Bill there is no intention on the part of this Government, nor has it received instructions from the Secretary of State for the Colonies to enact

===== Page 92 =====
Fresh legislation introducing a scale for Supertax and/or Excess Profits Tax. Today, I do not propose to give all the reasons against the imposition of Supertax or Excess Profits Tax but, so far as I am aware, other than the benefits to the rubber and tin industries, the mercantile community of this Colony are to-day handicapped very considerably by the regulations restricting and prohibiting the importation and exportation of a large number of articles, the restrictions on foreign exchange, the Food Control regulations, price control of engineering stores by the Director of Engineering Stores, and other war-time restrictions, so that the possibility of excess profits being made by the merchants in the Colony as a result of the present war is more the exception than the general rule. It must be realised that this Colony produces practically no tin and comparatively little rubber.

If Government can give me satisfactory replies to the three points raised by me, I am prepared to give this Bill my full support.

On this assumption, I should like now to refer to one of the principal objections to income tax raised by the Chinese mercantile community. I do not know whether Government is aware that in the case of numerous small Chinese merchants, their accounts are kept in very simple form and that possibly when they strike up their trading and profit and loss account at the end of each year they do not have a physical stock-taking but place an approximate value on the goods they have in stock. Most of these merchants do not employ a full-time competent book-keeper because they cannot afford to employ one. The accounts are kept by some person who has a little knowledge of simple entry book-keeping and it is not unusual to find numerous erasures in these books. To those accustomed to English methods of book-keeping, numerous erasures give rise to suspicion of intention to defraud.

I was informed that when income tax was introduced during the last war, the staff in the Income Tax Department, noticing these numerous erasures, resorted to threats against the proprietors of these businesses on the grounds that these erasures indicated an intention by the proprietors to falsify their accounts with the object of evading the payment of income tax. These Chinese merchants, because of their ignorance and put in fear of prosecution, naturally resorted to bribery in order that their accounts might be put right by the income tax staff thus avoiding prosecution. I feel certain that unless some new procedure is devised for dealing with the Chinese merchants, bribery and corruption must arise again. I therefore suggest that where there is doubt by the Collector as to whether the accounts or statement submitted by the Asiatic merchant are true or otherwise, the panel of assessors as provided for in clause 17 of the Bill be consulted. Further, the names of persons constituting the panel of assessors should be made known to the public in order that genuine complaints as to unfair treatment or unnecessary inquisition by the staff can be brought to the notice of the Collector through an Assessor.

I also consider that the staff of the Income Tax Department should be advised that mere erasures of figures or words is not an offence unless it can be proved there is intention to defraud.

Then again, there are cases of firms whose incomes verge on the border line of the exemption figure and these merchants, because of the nature of their trade, are unable to keep proper sets of accounts. In such cases, I suggest that under the power to make Rules certain forms containing simple questions such as—amount of rent of premises, amount of annual turnover, gross profit, overhead expenses, net profit, etc., be sent out to these merchants so that they can fill in the necessary particulars, and these replies submitted to some of the members of the panel of assessors for their advice as to whether the returns appear reasonable. If the returns appear unreasonable then the Collector, on the advice of the Assessors, can either suggest the figure he considers reasonable or call for the account books of the firm for his inspection. The proposed rates of income tax are so reasonable that I think there will be few cases of false returns. This procedure might mean much work for some.

===== Page 93 =====
Of the Assessors, I am confident the Chinese Chamber of Commerce, if requested, will be ready to give every assistance to the Government in this matter. For the first year, it is my view that it would be preferable for the Income Tax Department, where there has been no evidence of intention to defraud in the case of the small traders, to err on the side of leniency (if advised by the Assessors) than to force these merchants to keep a full set of books.

I consider the panel of Assessors as provided in clause 17 a very valuable contribution to the smooth working of this Bill, provided that for the first year of the operation of the Bill the fullest use is made of the services of these gentlemen.

Finally, Sir, I wish to add that a large number of Chinese merchants with whom I discussed the subject of war tax on incomes have assured me that if satisfactory replies to the three points I raised are given by Government and the suggestions I have made regarding the panel of Assessors are accepted, that they would, for the welfare and peace of the Colony, not carry their objections any further. Therefore, Sir, I plead with you to give the fullest consideration to the points I have raised, which, in the light of my explanation, cannot be deemed unreasonable.

For myself, I consider this Bill, although not perfect, should be passed today. I hope the Government will appoint a standing committee to consider suggestions to improve the Bill and make it equitable to all parties affected.

Before I sit down, I wish to state that I have been requested to put forward some suggestions regarding the Bill itself, but I think it would be better if I brought up these points at the committee stage. (Applause).

Capt. N. M. Hashim:— Sir, in welcoming this Bill, I wish to say that when it becomes law, it will be a very equitable, fair, and just form of legislation, enabling the citizens of this country who should and can contribute, in equal proportion of their wealth accumulated in this country, to do so for the successful prosecution of the present war for the dignity, honour, and safety of the persons and possessions of the citizens themselves and this country, and to repay some of the debt they owe to this country for the prosperity, advancement, and happiness they enjoy, due to the generosity and unselfish good nature of the sons of the soil, and the unlimited enormous wealth of the State, in addition to the protection, peace, freedom, and liberty they enjoy under the good and enlightened Government, and other amenities, the extent of which those who have made this country their country of adoption, temporary or permanent, or in many other parts of the Empire, can never dream of getting in their natural countries.

This war tax on incomes and profits as a war measure only, when introduced, will bring the citizens of this country on the same level as, but in a very mild form, those in other parts of the Empire, who have been paying real income tax as a matter of course—in some cases up to 82% of their incomes—in addition to the great daily sacrifice of all that is dear and precious to them.

During the last war, tax on incomes and profits proved to be the right and legitimate form of taxation for the purpose, leviable from people whose battle the Empire was fighting. It was a success. Indirect taxation hit the poor. I am now glad to know that the Joint Committee appointed to enquire into this question in all respects has now confirmed this conception.

Allow me to clarify what I have just said. It is admitted beyond all doubt that even during the present progress of the war, from trade returns and other evidence, the rich are already multiplying their incomes and profits. I say legitimate incomes and profits. I hope I am not misunderstood as referring to war profiteering, which is also not non-existent. Another glaring evidence of this is that, in spite of continuous soaring in the price of building materials, buildings of several storeys are springing up all over the country, particularly in business centres. This indicates accretion of money, or stagnation of accumulated wealth, and also a demand for more houses for business. This further indicates more business is being done every day and that house-building is a paying proposition. This, of course, benefits the rich.

===== Page 94 =====
From what we learnt after the last war, it was the rich who became richer, with trade expansion, with land booms and with great demands on the produce of the country. What is the underlying motive that provoked the enemy to wage this war against us? It is the greed for the wealth and possessions of the rich—the fruits of just government throughout the Empire. Therefore, I am right when I say it is the battle of the rich that this Empire is fighting and it is the duty of the rich to join in the fight as we see it being done in Great Britain, in the Dominions and in other Dependencies; the non-rich join the fight for the preservation of their existence and the peace and liberty they enjoy, which are precious to them, and also for their gradual improvement and upliftment.

Coming to another aspect of the case, who are the people who are being taken as hostages by the invading or retreating forces of the enemy in any theatre of war? It is the rich, after the confiscation by the enemy of their obtainable wealth on the spur of the moment, in the hope of getting what is suspected to be hidden. The poorer are only enslaved for labour, which they are more or less used to, of course; only, they may be subjected to the additional hardships of ill-treatment and loss of liberty. The rich—the hostages—are ultimately placed under this category when there is no more to be got out of them.

We have to admit the truth and we must face facts. Broadly speaking, the Belgians are more hardy than the people here collectively. In spite of their adversity, the Belgians are still determined to fight and are fighting to recover their land, freedom and independence. We can be as sure as our gallant and worthy ally that they are going to get them sooner or later.

Now allow me to quote M. Georges Theunis, former Premier of Belgium and now Ambassador-at-large in the United States, in his effort to enlighten the people in America as to the state of affairs in Belgium: "The German attitude towards the Belgians was becoming more and more harsh. Apart from indirect pillage, which was represented by purchases of goods with worthless currency, the Germans had imposed war taxes, amounting to several billion francs. They had fined the Government officials who did not appear sufficiently pliable. Churches had been closed as reprisals against certain patriotic speeches."

Let us now turn to Poland, another strong and gallant nation, and we learn, in addition to various atrocities, pillage and brutalities, "The eviction of Poles from all parts of Poland incorporated in the German Reich is attaining vast proportions, according to Polish circles in London. In the first twelve months of Nazism, 1,500,000 people were stated to have been expelled from their homes. This has been done under the system of general expropriation. All Poles were deprived of property rights by Goering's decree of February, 1940. Nothing but a fragment of the former Polish Republic had been left as 'living space' to them, and contemptuously dubbed as restjebit (area left over)." Now, pause and take stock of ourselves, picturing all aspects and situations.

What has happened to Czechoslovakia? See what is going on in Holland, Denmark and Norway. I need not go into details. They are well known and accepted facts.

As to what is going on in Belgium and other occupied countries after the invasion and after pillage and slavery, the people who are not put into concentration camps because they can serve some purpose useful to the invaders would be compelled to pay excessive war taxes, as disclosed in Belgium. This is sure and certain. So let us, with good grace and without demur, pay the war tax in its normal and equitable amount—a drop in the ocean and, as a war measure, a very small or no sacrifice at all—to keep the pest out and maintain our honour, dignity and safety.

We have to remember that the present war is the continuation of the last war. In the last war, the sacrifice was for the crushing of Prussianism. This, in its first step, has since been transformed into Nazism, later into Nazism-Fascism and now into the Axis, a four-fold coming tyranny and brutality, which we are facing. By paying the war tax under this Bill for a year, we would be

===== Page 95 =====
Contributing for hardly one day's war expenditure. The total payment in dollars and cents is nothing to compare with the moral support we shall give and the blessings, protection and safety we shall get in return, which even now we are enjoying and will enjoy, not to say anything of the additional amenities which are in store for us when victory comes.

We must win; we shall win; we are fighting the most evil thing that has troubled the world for many centuries. With the Axis view of life there can be no compromise. We must crush it before there can be any peace and goodwill on earth.

To achieve this we have to nurse and tabulate and establish our three M's—the three essentials of war—namely, Man, Money and Material, soundly and satisfactorily. This country is the arsenal of these three M's. We have to utilise them to the full to win this war. At the last meeting of Council I spoke on the first and the third M's, though partially. Today we are considering the question of the second M.

I congratulate Government for being patient for so long, in spite of the fact that money is needed badly and urgently for the safety of this country, and for all the steps it has taken to hear the views of the people, whether right or wrong, and to prepare and enlighten the public on this very vital question. I also congratulate Government on the modest form in which it has presented its case in this—this vital matter during these days of troubles—in the form which appears to some or many as coaxing or persuasion. But according to the decision of a certain section of traders and merchants, at a meeting in the early days favouring war tax on incomes and profits, they agreed that this form of taxation would not only help them to contribute the equitable and fair amount to the war cause but would also enable them, in course of time, to improve their system of bookkeeping, which means improving the form of their business and their financial returns.

It is gratifying to know now that an Information Bureau under the charge of a Public Relations Officer, for the purpose of assisting and guiding the public will be established. I attach great importance to this Bureau from the administrative point of view. The prestige of the Government and the appreciation of the public depend not to a small extent on the result of the working of this Bureau. In this connection, I should like to make a strong appeal regarding the officer to be placed in charge of this Bureau. He should be one with vast experience in handling human elements in civil lives, fairly well known to and by the public of all races and one to whom the public will be ready and eager to go for advice and help, being convinced of his civil, helpful and resourceful nature.

The citizens of this country realise their duty and responsibility to the Empire and the ways and means to save their lives and property. They have been debating all these years as to the equitable and fair and just form in which to contribute their mite for the prosecution of the war to victory. Their representatives, the cream of this country, have now recommended this war tax on incomes and profits. I believe no right-thinking and loyal citizen will shirk his duties and responsibilities, especially after the assurance given by Government against his various anxieties relating to his business secrets and other susceptibilities. There would now be no objection or obstacle to this form of taxation.

There are always exceptions to general rules. I am sure the Bill—when it becomes law and after a certain period of adjustment to meet the requirements of the people—would turn out to be a blessing to the country and the people.

As the eye-opener, I should like to quote Mr. HENRY MORGENTHAU, the United States Secretary of the Treasury, in his testimony to the Senate Foreign Relations Committee on Tuesday last on the Lease and Lend Bill. Every loyal citizen of the Empire, as in duty bound, should ponder over his statement.

Another thing is that everyone in this country has to take his hat off to the British citizens in the United States for the loyal and noble spirit that has prompted them to decide to sell within twelve months every bit of property they

===== Page 96 =====
Own in that country to finance war contracts. What sacrifice! Do we like to be left behind and allow ourselves to look too small?

Another important point I should like to bring to the consideration of everyone is, with Your Excellency's permission, the following portion of Your Excellency's address at the Budget Meeting of this Council on 16th October, 1939, "... Individually we may not be able to do much, but collectively we can do a great deal; by being yet more zealous in our work, by accepting quietly such decisions as may be imposed in the common interest, by refraining from making difficulty and from spreading rumours. In a letter which a few days ago I received from a correspondent, he spoke of the need for "quiet efficiency" and I can think of no better words. Anyone who in these days by careless talk or to suit personal inclinations throws sand into the wheels so that the machinery which has been set up shall run less smoothly is doing harm to the State and thereby to the cause for which the Empire is fighting".

Enough talking has been done. Everyone with some sparks of intelligence and with genuine feeling of loyalty and self-respect ought by now to be able to understand the position and realise the seriousness of the situation. No more talking is needed. What is needed and needed urgently is decisive action. So, let us start with real action and do no more talking and try to practise "quiet efficiency". In this period of trouble politicians are to cease to display their wares. Earnest statesmanship is wanted.

In spite of the fact that this country has established herself as a "Dollar Arsenal" and the people in this country are rolling in money, I feel that she has not reached the peak of her capacity to contribute to the Empire war effort.

Relaxation means crime, according to the Premier. So everyone will undoubtedly put his heart and soul into this War Tax Bill when it becomes Law and help its success. (Applause).

Mr. E. N. C. Woollerton:—Sir, I do not think anyone will disagree with me when I say that this Colony is not at present contributing to the war effort to anything like the full extent of which it is capable. I feel quite sure that in this, as in all other countries, it is not possible for us to work up to our maximum contribution without compulsion in the form of direct taxation and to my mind there is no other system which ensures that the individual will make his right and just contribution towards the cost of the war. Moreover, the great merit of direct taxation is that it does not place an unfair burden upon the poor, but makes individuals pay in accordance with their ability to do so.

The system of direct taxation has been successfully adopted by practically every country in the world, in the East as well as in the West, and I fail to see why its introduction should not be successfully achieved in this Colony. The Enactment now before Council has been drawn up by a Select Committee after the most careful consideration and due regard to the opinions of many leading bodies.

If the Bill is passed, I do not think anyone will be able to say that he or she is being compelled to pay tax for the prosecution of the war at a higher level than is their bounden duty. The most that a man can pay under the Bill is 8% of his income or 1/7d. in the pound and to my mind the weakness in the Bill is that the rich are under-taxed thereby.

We should all keep our main object in the foreground, namely, to work up our contribution to the war effort to the full extent of our capability in the shortest possible time and with this end in view I should have preferred a fully-fledged Income Tax Bill which allows a measure of relief for the married man, reduces anomalies and inconsistencies to a minimum and imposes a scale of super-tax. However, no legislation of this nature can be expected to be perfect at the outset and if the Enactment now before Council becomes Law, it will be possible to re-model the present Bill at a later stage in the light of the experience gained.

Sir, I favour the principle of a war tax on incomes and I therefore support the Bill. (Applause).

===== Page 97 =====
Lt.-Col. E. A. de Buriatte:—Sir, I support this Bill in principle. There are certain provisions which, in my opinion, require discussion, but this should properly be dealt with in Committee.

It has been suggested in a certain quarter that the passing of this Bill will be ultra vires the Letters Patent. Even if the whole of the money to be raised by this legislation were to be paid to the Home Government for war expenditure, then in such case it would be, and it is my view that this Bill when passed would still come within the ambit of the Letters Patent since the main object of raising and paying the money would be to bring peace to the inhabitants of the Empire in general and Malaya in particular. In my view, therefore, we can safely ignore this query which has been raised, but it would be interesting to hear the views of the Hon. the Attorney-General on the point. (Applause).

Mr. C. H. da Silva:—Sir, the subject matter of this Bill has received very thorough investigation and, indeed, not a little castigation in another forum—much in the fifth column and adjoining it in sentiment, though there is nothing sinister in this coincidence.

Much of the misconception and vilification had its roots in the improper appellation of "income tax". It had led chiefly to the fear that Government has rapacious tendencies and would seek to retain the tax as a permanent institution. There have been clear assurances that such is not Government's intention but they raised the ugly head of the dreadful ogre and the exploitation went so far as to invoke poetic inspiration from Paradise Lost likening our rulers to fiends and tyrants with devilish deeds. And here we live in this happy land which is anything but Paradise Lost and is more like Paradise Regained as many who have returned from leave can readily vouch.

Of course, it has ever been the privilege of the British taxpayer to grouse and this inheritance has not been denied to sojourners in this country. Detestation of the tax collector and the benediction of ugly names are not denied under the flag where freedom of thought and speech still prevail. But beneath all this the taxpayer in Britain has paid and even smiled. With the levy raised to 8/6d. in the pound he still carries on and wonders why he isn't taxed even more heavily. The contrast which we have experienced in this country has not made a pretty picture.

How ugly it would have been had there been a suggestion of super-tax, which many feel would be the fairest and best! Then the burden would be on shoulders best able to carry it, but we have been saved the raising of another spectre and further taunts of tyranny which such a levy would doubtless have evoked.

When I first voiced my preparedness to support a measure of direct taxation, the cost of the war was estimated at 3,000,000 per day. To-day the figure has doubled and once again I ask must we wait and dally till our backs are against the wall? But it is protested that the public is willing to contribute more and is ready to participate with its silver bullets but not by this method. Alternatives have been sought and suggested but none would appear to be as ready and conclusive as this measure of direct taxation.

I would leave the problems of administration to those charged with them. I would leave the excuse of the evasions of others. I would let such as they run the risk of detection and face the result of their misdeeds. Because some may evade is this a reason to refuse our mite? Must the misdeeds of malefactors cause us to abolish the law? Must there be no law because there are lawbreakers? Must we abrogate so many revenue laws because of smugglers? There is only one answer.

I spoke of dallying till our backs are against the wall. There are defeatists who would believe that this is already so. I would remind them of the tradition that the bulldog was never more dangerous. A cartoon appeared in a London newspaper showing the two gangsters and John Bull facing them with bayonet

===== Page 98 =====
One says, "We've got him with his back to the wall." The other says, "Yes, but look what's at the back of the wall." And over the top of the wall stand the soldiers of the Empire at the ready—Canada, Australia, New Zealand, India, South Africa, Newfoundland.

We may not be in the picture with manpower from this country, but must we be entirely out of the picture? May we yet hope that this country may find not an unworthy place by its war efforts. May the small contribution which this Bill will provide earn Malaya a modest niche in the halls of service, gratitude and loyalty. (Applause).

Mr. E. C. H. Charlwood:—Sir, it cannot be gainsaid that the recommendations of the Joint Committee and in consequence the Bill now before this House fall far short of Malaya's obligation to the Imperial Government. But the reason is largely attributable to the peculiar structure of the country—different political entities each with its own fiscal system. A common basis with the Federated Malay States has, however, been found, and it is right that we should enact a law upon the foundation laid in the hope and expectation that it will ultimately produce something worthy of our debt.

Much criticism is from quarters that are out to damn direct taxation in any shape or form without any counter-proposals capable of producing steady revenue for war purposes over a period of years.

Admittedly, the Bill is a compromise; it is, in effect, a taxation scale applied for a defined period to incomes, profits and emoluments generally, but it is not income tax in the fully accepted meaning of the term.

Most, if not all, of the inequalities evoking criticism would be avoidable in a proper income tax Ordinance, and it is noteworthy that many of the complaints heard are from sources that have most clamorously opposed real income tax. In other words, they stress faults that would be absent from the very scheme which they decried. Therefore, the Committee rightly took the view that to try pleasing everyone would mean perpetual sessions without reaching any practicable solution, and in view of the pressing need for war revenue, contented themselves with a workable compromise.

The moderate scale of taxation laid down in the Ordinance means that any inequalities are likely to be minor ones, so far as residents in Malaya are concerned. The total yield, even on the scale proposed, will not, I am confident, be the first instance of a creditable child living down malicious gossip current at its birth. (Laughter).

There is a surprising amount of self-pity over the incidence of this light tax as it affects local residents, but little for the individual who may suffer most, namely, the man at Home, whose savings are in dollar industrial companies. Dividends declared by such companies will, it is generally understood, be paid from profits after tax has been levied so that it will presumably be again subject to the Home scale: 8/6d. in the pound in many cases.

It is not out of place to remark that those at Home have troubles additional to financial ones—air raids for example.

The problem of raising money for defence purposes is as old as war itself and in the end all wars have to be paid for by direct contribution in some shape or form, so why not face the issue squarely?

The plea of "unsuitability" is heard with monotonous frequency, and I am not in the least degree exaggerating in recalling that precisely the same objection was raised, not many years ago either, by people of this Colony to what we still like to call "modern" sanitation. It was not suitable to the country. And judging by some of the stuff that finds its way into print, I think we have a few of these die-hards with us yet. (Laughter).

Reading legal opinions voiced by correspondents to the Press a month ago, I am most uneasy lest even now some earnest student should disinter a long-forgotten Ordinance, telling us a water-borne system is ultra vires the laws of this Colony. (Laughter).

===== Page 99 =====
The alternating current of loyal resolution and tax antipathy whereby sections of Malaya derive light, supposedly sufficient for their needs, is a little bewildering. The inconsistency recalls to me some of the observations in Mr. Pickwick's diary on the ancient borough of Muggleton, a community (he says) "mingling a zealous advocacy of Christian principles with a devoted attachment to commercial rights."

Time enough has been allowed for evolving alternative methods of raising sums of money commensurate with the country's capacity to pay, and as these have not been forthcoming, the supporters of this Bill may rightly assume that direct taxation for war purposes is the wish of the Colony. (Applause).

The Attorney-General:—Sir, I am very glad to have the support of my honourable, gallant and learned friend in thinking that the suggestions which have been made in the Press, but not put forward by any member of this Council, are without grounds.

The Letters Patent, Article VIII, which was passed under the Warrant of His Majesty, says:

"We do hereby delegate to the persons who shall from time to time compose the said Legislative Council full power and authority to establish all such laws, institutions, and ordinances, and to constitute such Courts and Officers, and to make such provisions and regulations for the proceedings in such Courts, and for the administration of justice, and for the raising and expenditure of the Public Revenue, as may be deemed advisable for the peace, order, and good government of Our subjects and others within Our Settlements or within any territory which may at any time be part of or dependent upon the same: subject nevertheless to all such conditions, provisoes, and limitations as We shall see fit to prescribe from time to time by these or any other Our Letters Patent or by any Instructions under Our Sign, Manual and Signet."

I cannot imagine a wider form of delegation than those words, subject only to the provisoes which are referred to at the end of the Article. Certain restrictions have been placed on the legislative powers of this body, but, apart from such as His Majesty has seen fit to impose, I submit that the intention of those Letters Patent were to give this body the widest possible powers of legislation. In any event, the objection which is taken to the present Bill seems to be entirely misconceived because this Bill does not purport to raise funds to be remitted to the Imperial Government. If there is anything in the objection at all, it applies equally to the contributions under Contributions to Imperial Defence Ordinance which has been in force for eight years; it applies to all the war legislation which was passed during the last war (and I do not pretend to have gone through all the Ordinances, but there are probably others), but it certainly does not in any event apply to this Ordinance. (Applause).

The Financial Secretary:—Sir, I am no lawyer, but perhaps it would help to clear up this controversy which is raging round the legality of this Bill if I were to say that it is the desire of His Majesty's Government that our prime duty is to ensure that Malayan defence is made as complete as possible, and that the proceeds of war taxation should be devoted to this in the first instance. I would ask hon. members to consider the purpose of this Bill in that light.

I wish to thank hon. members for their comments on the Bill, both favourable and critical. It must be a source of satisfaction to many—I would like to be able to say to all—that it is now generally recognised that the maximum contribution that this country can make towards the successful prosecution of the war can only be attained with the aid of direct taxation. The only point which appears to be open to argument is whether the present Bill meets the need.

I would urge everybody to bear in mind that this measure is unlike and does not offer comparison with legislation in other countries where an income tax

===== Page 100 =====
is a permanent feature of the fiscal system. Only thus will it be realised that any anomalies or inequalities that the Bill may disclose are inherent in the nature and the purpose of the Bill.

As I understand the position, what is wanted at the moment—and it is needed quickly—is a reasonably fair and a comparatively simple direct tax, and if this is appreciated it at once becomes clear why the Committee suggested that legislation on the lines of an Ordinance which had already been once tried in the Colony should be tried again. Whatever imperfections the Bill may disclose should not be laid at the door of the Committee. I repeat that they are inherent in the nature of the measure.

The Committee in the course of its deliberations considered a draft Bill based on United Kingdom and Ceylon practice, the Hong Kong War Revenue Ordinance and the whole series of Colony Ordinances from 1917 to 1922. Warned by the fate which befell the Hong Kong Income Tax Bill, the Committee considered it prudent to go very carefully into the Hong Kong War Revenue Ordinance which had been accepted in its place. The Committee’s report indicates quite clearly why it rejected this Ordinance as a model. The Ceylon draft, which, as my hon. friend the Senior Unofficial Member would say, is a strong and healthy child, was in the circumstances as the Committee understood them unlikely to receive support, and it turned therefore to the Colony Ordinances which were introduced and operated in similar circumstances.

It is charged by the hon. Senior Unofficial Member that there has been procrastination in deciding upon a measure of direct taxation. There are the facts. The Joint Committee, appointed in 1939 soon after the outbreak of war, was advised that a tax on incomes would receive no unofficial support and naturally it spent no time in discussing such a measure. In the Spring of last year it was made known that Government favoured a tax on incomes as a means of contributing to the Imperial Government. I do not think I need take up the time of the House in relating how the news was received. A War Loan was raised in October and a tax on incomes and profits is now proposed for this year. As long ago as June of last year I made it quite clear to this Council that it was unlikely that the machinery for operating a tax on incomes would be ready before the beginning of this year. I cannot therefore see why the hon. member should say at this stage that there has been undue delay. It is even more difficult for me to understand how if there had been delay there would be any point in adding to the delay by abandoning the present Bill.

I now turn to the points which have been raised by the hon. the Senior Unofficial Member in what, if I may say so, was an excellent speech, although I may not agree with what he said. One of the points was the assessment of partnership profits at the source with no rebate for the individual partners. This is inherent in the scheme proposed for taxing profits, and personally I do not regard it as unfair. The idea underlying it is that the tax is imposed on the unit, that is to say, the individual, the company, the partnership, firm or association. I think it is fair to say that, generally speaking, two or more persons who combine to do business and who pool their resources and knowledge are likely to obtain a proportionately greater return on their capital than an individual carrying on business single-handed. This is particularly the case in a country such as this where people have to take spells of leave for health reasons. In the case of a one-man firm there is also the danger that when he returns from leave, having left his business or his profession to a locum, he will find the goodwill of his business impaired. I have seen it on more than one occasion happen in this country, and I have no doubt hon. members can very easily recall instances to their own minds. For my part, I refuse to believe that any established firm would dissolve in order that its members might carry on the business or the profession individually and thereby evade a comparatively trifling amount of tax. I should like to remind hon.

===== Page 101 =====
Members, this system was accepted during the last war; in fact, I think I can say it was accepted with pleasure because it avoided inquiry into the composition of partnerships. Another point is that it addresses the very vexed question of the undivided Joint Hindu family. It also prevents bogus partners from being introduced into a firm so that the share of the profits of each individual may be reduced and thus possibly escape tax altogether; it also prevents evasion of tax by non-distribution of profits.

The absence of a provision for marriage and family allowances was another point which my hon. friend called attention to. The Committee gave its reasons for the omission: the high exemption limit of $4,800 a year and the low initial rates of the tax are such that there is no real hardship, and I fully endorse the Committee's views on this point. Another point I can recall in this connection is that, as in the present Bill there is no aggregation of the incomes of a husband and wife, it would appear illogical to grant these allowances. That is a point made by the 1921 Royal Commission on United Kingdom Income Tax. It may be news to some that up to 1909 there were no allowances in the United Kingdom for either wives or children. A children's allowance was introduced in 1909. I am speaking off-hand now, but I believe that the standard rate of tax in 1910 was 12½%. That may give hon. members an idea of the size of the tax at that time compared with what is proposed here. I say that it was not until 1909 that an allowance was granted for children, but not for wives, and I think it was not until 1918 that an allowance for wives was brought in, and the wife was then valued at the sum of £25. (Laughter). It is a fact, Sir.

Another point which my hon. friend mentioned was the question of obsolescence and amortisation. Hon. members know that the Bill does provide for fair wear and tear, and certain recognised standards will, of course, be adopted in calculating allowances for fair wear and tear after consultation with the Assessors and with the trade. It seems to me that it is too much to expect that amortisation and obsolescence should be provided for in a one-year Bill, but the matter will not be overlooked when legislation for next year is under consideration. I am not aware that amortisation is provided for in any permanent forms of income tax legislation, and it is not allowed even where an excess profits tax is imposed.

My hon. friend also called attention to the care with which amendments were considered in India—amendments to their Income Tax law. Well, in my opinion, comparison with India is beside the point, because in India they have had an income tax in force since 1886, and it is obviously quite simple for them to sit down and work out amendments at leisure. They can, if necessary, take three or four years while they are collecting the tax. Here the position is quite different. What we want to introduce is a war tax on incomes and profits, and our first aim should be to get it done as quickly as possible. Once we have a tax in force, amendments or better legislation can be considered at leisure as they do in India.

I come now to my hon. friend's proposal that there should be a full income tax for, I understood him to say, Europeans only. Well, had my hon. friend said that he favoured a fully-fledged income tax for all, as does the Hon. Mr. Wollerton, I would have been with him all the way, but I fear that any criticisms of the present Bill would be as a mild breeze wafted over the summer air compared with the storm which my hon. friend's proposal would provoke if Government took it in earnest.

If the present Bill is regarded by my hon. friend as a piece of ineptitude, what can I say of his suggestion but that it is a complete surrender? A property tax, however well-graded, however heavily graded, would not make up for the exclusion of Asiatics from a tax on incomes, because there must be a large number—a very large number—of Asiatic businessmen and tradesmen who do quite a thriving business but who do not own property. The Bill

===== Page 102 =====
Having got so far, I suggest that it would be in the best interests of the war effort to give it a run and collect revenue and experience this year while we are considering what would be a suitable form of legislation next year.

The hon. the Chinese member for Singapore has put in a plea for the small trader. Clause 17 of the Bill has been drafted to deal with such cases as he has in mind. In this respect I think it will be admitted that the present Bill is an improvement over the 1919 Ordinance, and we have to thank the Committee for this innovation. I feel sure that in any cases of doubt the Collector will not hesitate to avail himself of the discretion given him under clause 17 to consult the Assessors in computing income and total income.

The names of the persons to be appointed as Assessors will certainly be made known to the public.

My hon. friend’s suggestion that a simplified form of return should be devised to be used by border-line firms is a valuable one and will most certainly be taken up.

I can assure my hon. friend, the Malay member, that the officer to be appointed as Public Relations Officer will be chosen with care.

I wish, Sir, to express my gratitude to the Hon. the Unofficial members for having given up a day of their valuable time after the last meeting of Council to a discussion of the details of the Bill. The amendments which were agreed upon will be moved by me in Committee, and I hope that it will thus be possible to pass the Bill without further delay.

I would propose, Sir, that when the tax becomes operative, a Standing Committee with an Unofficial majority should be appointed to receive and consider reports on the working of the Bill and to recommend in due course what form of legislation should be brought in next year if a tax on incomes is to be levied for a further period. (Applause).

The Governor:—It is clear from the speeches to which we have listened that the general desire in this House is to support the principle of this Bill, and I venture to think that the debate has been worthy of the occasion. It seems unnecessary for me to say more than a few words.

The Hon. Mr. TAY LIAN TECK has made three requests of the Government. The first is that it shall be stated in the Bill that the purpose of this legislation is to provide for the service of the Straits Settlements War Loan and for the redemption of War Savings Certificates. He feels that, if this is done, it will be clear to future Administrators of the Colony that the present members of this Council have agreed to the imposition of income tax purely as a war measure and for a limited period only. I thought that this had already been made abundantly clear. In actual fact, this Government is not in this legislation looking as far ahead as the Hon. Member. Not only has this Bill been drafted to meet the safeguards laid down in para. 9 of the joint Committee’s Report, which the Government of the Colony accepts, but clause 5 of the Bill provides that the taxation which this Bill imposes shall be collected only in respect of this year 1941. At the end of this year the legislation automatically expires, and to revive it a fresh Bill will be necessary. We are not now looking further ahead than that, and there is certainly no intention of securing through this legislation any form of taxation which will persist after the war is over, or even after the end of 1941 if this Legislative Council is then opposed to it. What should be done from year to year must be left to the Legislature of the day.

The Hon. Member’s second request is that, if at some future date it is considered necessary to increase the maximum rate of 8 per cent. for which this Bill provides, the decision to do so should be left entirely to the unofficials. I will preface my reply by quoting these words from the first speech which I made in this House: they will be found on page B 162 of the Proceedings of the Legislative Council for the year 1934. I said:—“In the course of my service I have served on, or have been connected with the work of, a good many Legislative Councils, and as President I have always made it a practice to regard the Council not as a machine created for the automatic registration of the decisions.

===== Page 103 =====
Of the Governor and his Executive Council but as a body of persons called together fully and formally to debate whatever measures may be placed before it. In the event, the very unlikely event, of my finding myself confronted with a solid bloc of opposition from the unofficial members, I think I may safely say that I should defer action, or if necessary refer the matter again to the Secretary of State before taking a decision. I should hope that that would suffice as a reply to the Hon. Member's request but, if it does not, then I would ask him to reflect for a moment. There are thirteen unofficial members of this Council. Suppose they were divided in opinion as might well be the case. The decision on an important matter of policy might then rest with seven or eight out of a Legislature of twenty-six members. I suggest that it is asking too much of any Government, that it should surrender its powers so completely. If the unofficial members are solidly opposed to a proposition put forward by the Government, then as I have just said I should defer action. But if they are not, then I consider that it should be left to me as President to decide whether or not to take the opinion of the Council as a whole. On this particular point raised by the Hon. Member, as it is impossible to foretell what would be a reasonable or unreasonable rate of tax in relation to the war, I can only say that any alterations in the rates will no more be conceived in an unreasonable spirit than have the present ones, and that in fixing them local and contemporaneous conditions will be most carefully taken into account.

In regard to the third point I can assure the Hon. Member that no instructions have been received from the Secretary of State to impose an excess profits tax or a Super-tax. The statement made to him is quite untrue. It seems difficult to disabuse the public mind, but to the best of my belief the Secretary of State does not regard it as part of his prerogative to order the imposition of a tax on any people within the Empire. The Imperial Government has, time and again, shown its readiness to promote the political development of the Colonies and to leave them to manage their own affairs. To fetter the discretion of a Legislature in so personal a matter as taxation would be totally at variance with this policy.

There is no reason, however, why the Secretary of State should not ask Colonial Governments to consider various forms of war taxation. It will be realised that a number of these Governments receive grants-in-aid from the Imperial Exchequer and it is only right that so far as is possible this burden on the British taxpayer should be lightened. In common with all Colonial Governments, we have been recently asked to consider the feasibility of an excess profits tax. We shall do so, but unofficial opinion would certainly be consulted before any decision is taken. At the moment I need say no more than that there appear to be many difficulties in the way in addition to those mentioned by the Honourable Member.

I hope sincerely that the Honourable Member will be reassured by what I have said and that he will be able to give this Bill his full support. I am grateful to him for his evident desire to feel able to accept it in principle, and equally grateful to those members of his community to whom he referred in his closing sentences. It is yet another indication of the regard which the Chinese of the Colony have for Great Britain and of the friendly relations which exist between them and this Government that they should be ready, if they can, not to persist in their objections to a measure about which they may have misgivings but which has come into being solely by reason of the position in which the British Empire finds itself today. I have given my support to this Bill and I want to see it passed because I am an Englishman and as an ordinary citizen of my country I must give her all the help I can. That is all; surely it is a very natural desire. The speeches which we have heard today show that my countrymen feel as I do. And now the Honourable Mr. TAY LIAN TECK has told us, in effect, that his countrymen have the same desire to help. That is a very refreshing statement. I accept their assistance and in return I offer them mine. I promise them that I will do my best to see that their willingness to

===== Page 104 =====
Co-operate is not abused. If any of them has a grievance, by reason of this legislation, let him take it to one of the Chinese members of this Council, or to the Colonial Secretary, or to me, and we will see to it. I ask you, gentlemen, to accept the principle of direct taxation and to support this motion, in recognition of the peace and happiness which you enjoy and for the good name of the Colony of the Straits Settlements.

The question is that this Bill be read a second time. Those in favour say "aye", those to the contrary opinion say "no".

The motion was agreed to with one dissentient, and the Bill was read a second time.

The Council adjourned until 2.30 P.M.
