ADJOURNMENT.

The Colonial Secretary:—Sir, I move that this Council do now adjourn and I give notice that the date of the next meeting of the Council will be April the 28th, 1941.

Mr. F. D. Bisseker:—Sir, if I may take up a moment or two of Council's time at this late hour, there are one or two suggestions which I have to put forward, but before doing so I should like to refer very briefly to this morning's debate when I expressed my views upon the Bill before us. That I have been against it, as I considered it inadequate, does not mean that I shall oppose or endeavour to interfere in any way with its application. On the contrary, now that the majority has declared in favour, I shall do all in my power to help and be of use in any way open to me. Consequently it can be said that support for the successful application is unanimous in this House. (Applause).

Sir, I have a suggestion to make in regard to the disposition of civilian man-power. I believe it is not always easy to influence that man-power to the particular units of the passive defence measures which require men. Might it be considered, consequently, that the broadcasting system should be used to put out an appeal during the news periods that men are needed in a particular unit? I feel that if the broadcasting system were used and would say that men are

===== Page 108 =====
The Colonial Secretary:—Sir, I rise to second the motion.
The Bill was read a first time.

The Attorney-General:—Sir, I beg to give notice that at the next meeting of Council I shall move the subsequent stages of the Bill.

WAR TAX BILL.

The Financial Secretary:—Sir, I move that the War Tax Bill be read a first time.

Shortly after the outbreak of war, the Governments of the F.M.S. and the Colony appointed a Joint Committee to consider the question of contributions to the Imperial Government and the raising of such contributions by means of war taxation. As a result, certain existing duties were increased and other new duties and taxes were imposed and the whole of the gross proceeds are being remitted periodically to London as a gift to the Imperial Government. The next step to be taken was the appointment of a Committee to consider whether the Colony should raise a loan, the proceeds of which would be presented to the Imperial Government as a further contribution towards the prosecution of the war; and, if so, to suggest what measures should be taken to guarantee the means of servicing the loan. This Committee recommended a $25,000,000 loan at 3% and the issue of War Savings Certificates. As hon. members know, the loan was successfully launched in October last and the whole of the proceeds have since been presented to the Imperial Government. The amount of War Savings Certificates sold to date is over $2¼ millions and this money is also being handed over to the Imperial Government. To service the loan and to provide for the redemption of the War Savings Certificates, the Committee recommended a tax on incomes. In order to determine the best method of implementing this recommendation, a joint F.M.S. and Colony Committee was appointed to study problems connected with a tax on incomes which are common to both Administrations, and this Committee was given a free hand to formulate its proposals for legislation which it regarded as best suited to the needs and circumstances of the two Administrations. In its search for a type of simplicity which would be appropriate to a temporary tax for war purposes the Committee examined the Colony War Tax Ordinances of 1917, 1918 and 1919, which had been framed to meet the same problem as that with which the Committee was faced. In the words of the Committee's report, "These Ordinances were designed to impose a simple and straightforward tax upon profits and incomes in order to produce revenue for war purposes at a minimum cost and as fairly as the circumstances permitted". After the most careful deliberation, the Committee advised that no better solution of the problem could be found than the adoption of legislation on the lines of the 1919 Ordinance. This Ordinance was chosen as a model as it was the last of the War Tax Ordinances and the first to appreciate the special position of those producers of rubber and tin who were paying an additional war tax on their products. Another feature of the Ordinance which commended itself to the Committee was the fact that it exempted from local taxation all income on which tax had been paid elsewhere in the British Empire.

The Committee's report and the draft bill have been in the hands of hon. members for some time but I hope that my hon. friends will bear with me while I describe briefly the principal features of the Bill and the organization which it is proposed to set up to put the tax into operation if the Bill passes into law.

As to the Bill itself, I wish to lay a preliminary emphasis on two important points. The first is that the Bill is designed to provide for the imposition of additional taxation for the purpose of contributing towards the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the present war and for no other purpose. The second is that the Bill is to be operative for the whole of the present year only and if it is to be carried on into next year, it will be necessary to re-enact it. Thus the temporary nature of the measure, consistent with its object, is to be maintained.

===== Page 109 =====
The sources of income to be taxed may be divided into the categories: Salaries, Business Profits, Rents, Interest, and certain Miscellaneous sources. These categories will, of course, have their subdivisions; salaries, for example, will include pensions, rents will include the net annual value of immovable property, and so forth.

The classes of taxpayers to be affected are: individuals, companies incorporated within and outside the Colony; life assurance companies; and partnerships, firms, and associations other than corporations.

It will be observed that a distinction is drawn between "total income" and "income," and it is necessary to understand clearly the nature of this distinction. Taking, for example, the case of the individual, "total income" is the income from all sources within or without the Colony, while the "income" is the income derived in the Colony only or brought into the Colony from any other source. And here it would be as well if I explained that it is the "total income" which will determine the rate at which tax is to be levied, while the "income" is the sum upon which the tax will be assessed. I shall return to this distinction in a moment in order to give a simple illustration of its application.

Clause 7 of the Bill provides for certain deductions from income before the tax is calculated, the more important of which are income on which income tax has been or will be paid in any other part of the British Empire, payments for life assurance and provident funds, the dividends of a shareholder in a company, and the income received by a partner in a firm where the tax has already been paid by the company or firm. Also, the income derived by rubber cultivators and tin miners from their produce on which a special export duty has been paid, and this extends to dividends drawn by shareholders in rubber and tin companies.

In dealing with this question of deductions and allowances, the Committee, after careful consideration, arrived at the conclusion that it would be impracticable to devise a satisfactory schedule of marriage and family allowances, having regard to the existence here of polygamous communities and to the complexity and uncertainty of the marriage laws and customs of some of these communities. In order to compensate for this omission, the minimum "total income" which renders the subject liable for taxation has been put at the high figure of $4,800 a year, and the initial rates of tax have been kept very low.

There is a provision dealing with the method of collecting the tax which will render payment by salaried persons more convenient and deserves to be mentioned here. I refer to Clause 10 of the Bill which provides that an employer shall deduct the appropriate portion of the tax when he pays salary or pension to a person. Under Clause 20, credit will be given for such deduction when the Collector assesses that person's tax.

I would invite the attention of hon. members to one aspect of the rates of tax. It may be thought that certain inequities will arise at what may be termed the critical points at which the rate changes. For example, it might be supposed that while a person with an income of $7,199 would pay tax at the rate of 3%, another with an income of $7,201 would have to pay full tax at the rate of 4%. In other words, the latter, with an income of $2 more than the former, would have to pay $72 more in tax. There need be no fear of this, as it is proposed to prescribe a rule under Clause 62 (2) (b) whereby any increase in tax resulting from imposing a higher rate will not exceed the additional income which has occasioned the increase in rate. Thus, in the example which I have just given, the person with an income of $7,201, instead of paying 4%, would only pay 3% on $7,200 plus $1, that is, $1.03 in all more than the person with an income of $7,199.

I stated a few moments ago that I would give a simple illustration of the principal distinction between "total income" and "income." Let us take the case of a person whose salary arising in the Colony amounts to $4,200 a year. If he has no other source of income, that would be his "total income" and he would pay no tax. If, however, he has an income of $1,000 from investments in the United Kingdom, his total income would thus be brought up to $5,200,

===== Page 110 =====
Which is above the exemption limit, and tax would be payable at the rate of 2% on his local income of $4,200 only, provided that he had paid tax in the United Kingdom on the additional $1,000, or, if no tax had been paid, that the money were not brought into the Colony.

I come now to the provision for appealing against a Collector's assessment. Any dissatisfied person can petition a Board of Commissioners in his Settlement. The Board will consist of a Judge of the High Court and at least two prominent members of the community selected from a panel to be appointed by the Governor in Council. Either the Collector or the petitioner can appeal from the Board on a point of law to the Court of Appeal and there is further an appeal to the Privy Council. I might mention here that it is significant that during the whole of the period 1917 to 1922 when there was a tax on incomes in the Colony, there were no more than two appeals to the High Court. Commenting on this fact, the Committee very aptly observes:—"This would appear to show that there was no particular difficulty in either the interpretation or administration of the Legislation".

Now as to the organization which is proposed for the proper carrying out of the provisions of the Bill. There is to be a Collector-General at the head of the organization and there are to be three main divisions of the Taxing Department corresponding to the three Settlements of Singapore, Penang and Malacca. There is to be a Collector for each division whose duties will be to assess, collect and account for the tax. He will have the assistance of lay Assessors to be appointed by the Governor in Council to advise the Collector in fixing assessments. These Assessors will be selected from the leading representative businessmen of the Colony and will thus be in a position to give the Collector expert advice in matters with which they are specially conversant. It is also intended to obtain from the United Kingdom three trained taxing officers who will be styled Assistant Collectors. For the examination of returns from Asiatics it is proposed to engage a staff of examiners drawn from graduates of Raffles College. In addition, a locally born graduate in Economics is proposed for appointment as Special Examiner to advise the Collectors and Assessors on economic and trade matters. There is also to be an officer of the Straits Settlements Legal Service on the staff of the Collector-General. It is considered that the appointment to the Taxing Department of locally born young men of good families and possessing the best education that the country can offer should go a long way towards securing the confidence of the public and reducing the danger of corruption to a minimum.

Having dealt with the Bill itself, I feel, Sir, that I owe it both to the public and to this House to make some remarks on the more general aspects of a tax on incomes and profits as applied to this country. I shall begin by enumerating the main points in favour of such a tax and then I shall deal with the arguments which have been ventilated against it. Briefly, the main points in its favour are that it is generally recognised as the most equitable form of taxation; it places the burden where it can best be borne, that is to say, it applies the criterion of ability to pay; it taxes certain forms of wealth which would otherwise go untaxed; it tends to raise the general level of the cost of living to a far less extent than a system of indirect taxation producing an equal yield; it is a form of taxation which should produce in this country a large sum without any undue sacrifice; it would cause less interference with the normal course of trade than indirect taxation; and it has stood the test of time in almost every politically developed country in the world.

Even in the earlier days of income tax in the United Kingdom when successive ministers were of two minds as to the justification for such a measure in peace time, it was generally conceded that for war purposes it is the ideal form of tax. In this connection let me recall what Gladstone said of it in 1859: "Where for the dignity, honour and safety of this country we are called upon to make great and rapid efforts in the augmentation and extension of our defences,

===== Page 111 =====
The income tax is beyond all others the regular and legitimate resource to which to resort.

There is really no need for me to enlarge on the case for a war tax on incomes. Even the bitterest opponents of the tax will admit that, but for certain attendant evils, it would be the fairest form of taxation in the existing circumstances. I shall therefore pass to a consideration of the main objections which have been voiced to the tax. They may be summed up under the headings: the danger that once it is placed on the statute book it will become a permanent feature of the Colony’s fiscal system; the complexity of the legislation; the obstacle presented by Asiatic methods of accounting; the fear of inquisition; the disclosure of commercial secrets; the resultant flight of capital; evasion; corruption and the cost of collection.

As to the fears that the tax would become permanent, I have already pointed out that the Bill provides for the imposition of the tax for one year only and it will lapse unless it is re-enacted by this Council. Also that the proceeds of the tax are to be used towards defraying expenses incurred in the prosecution of the present war. I maintain that these two safeguards are a sufficient guarantee that there is no intention on the part of Government to pull a fast one over the taxpayer.

As to the complexity of the legislation, I think it is fair to say that the Committee made every effort to keep considerations of simplicity and convenience in the forefront, and it is just this simplicity which underlines the difference between the Bill before the House and what I may term the more permanent forms of income tax legislation adopted in other countries. The Committee recommended that the Taxing Department should be staffed by officers competent to give every assistance to businesses which keep their accounts in languages other than English, and that advice and explanations of the provisions of the law should be freely available to the public. Acting on this suggestion, it is proposed to establish an Information Bureau under the charge of a Public Relations Officer, for the purpose of assisting and guiding the public. There would be no connection between this Bureau and the staff to be employed in assessing and collecting the tax.

As to the difficulties which might arise owing to the use of Asiatic systems of accounting in the Colony, I fail to see that there are any problems here which are not present in surrounding countries which already levy a tax on incomes. I believe myself that a good many of the difficulties would be obviated at the outset by the high exemption limit of $4,800 a year which would place a considerable number of small businesses beyond the range of the tax. The Committee recommended that where the income of an individual or association cannot be readily ascertained or where accounts have not been satisfactorily kept, the Collector assisted by Assessors might compute the income on the basis of the usual rate of net profit on the turnover of the trade or business concerned. This proposal, which is reproduced in Clause 17 of the Bill, is expected to prove of great value in difficult and unusual cases and should go far towards meeting the frequently expressed criticism that precise ascertainment of profits is not possible with certain methods of bookkeeping.

Regarding the fear of inquisition, it has to be admitted, of course, that no form of taxation which necessitates the disclosure of personal interests or property can be administered without a certain amount of resentment on the part of some members of the public. For example, the examination of personal baggage by a Customs officer is accepted by most people in a proper and helpful spirit, but there is always the person who objects to having his personal belongings handled. In the case of a tax on incomes, the honest taxpayer need fear nothing more than an enquiry which would certainly not amount to an inquisition. The staff of the Taxing Department would be instructed to regard it as fundamental that the taxpayer is entitled to every consideration but, on the other hand, it would also be fair to expect that the public should co-operate with the department. The appointment of lay Assessors

===== Page 112 =====
To assist, the Collectors should go a long way towards eliminating inquisition, and the type of officer whom it is proposed to appoint should encourage the tax-payer to place his confidence in the department. I am prepared to assert with assurance that no honest tax-payer need fear the bogey of inquisition.

As to the fear of disclosure of commercial secrets, the staff of the Taxing Department, the Assessors, and the Commissioners would be required to subscribe to an oath of secrecy, and the Bill prescribes heavy penalties for any unauthorized disclosures of information. In addition, a special confidential staff is to be included in the proposed organisation of the department.

As regards the flight of capital, I need only say that during the years 1917 to 1922, when the Colony alone imposed a tax on incomes, there was no noticeable flight of capital to other parts of the Peninsula. Today, such a flight would in any case be barred by the Defence (Finance) Regulations which are common to all the Malayan Administrations.

As to evasion, it is surely superfluous for me to say that no form of taxation, whether direct or indirect, and no matter where it is imposed, is free from the possibility of evasion. False customs declarations, undervaluation of goods, and smuggling are just as common as false income tax returns. There are no conditions present in Malaya which, so far as a tax on incomes is concerned, are not present in the surrounding countries, to which a tax on incomes is no novelty, and if they can deal with evasion, so can we. I believe that we are in a better position today to create a satisfactory organisation for the collection of the tax than we were during the last war, and I am not prepared to admit that the tax was a failure then. Moreover, the rates which are proposed in the Bill are comparatively low, and the temptation to avoid payment should be correspondingly less. And, above all, this would be a wartime tax to be imposed for the specific purpose of assisting in the Empire's supreme struggle for liberty, and any person who, in such circumstances, deliberately evaded payment of the tax would, to say the very least, be working against his own interests.

As to corruption, a good deal has been said and written of late on that subject, and here again, as in the case of evasion, corruption is possible wherever taxation, direct or indirect, is levied. Corruption has two aspects: the offer and the acceptance of an illegal gift. Those members of the public who have been so loud in their insistence that the tax would give rise to corruption could assist very greatly in allaying their own fears by determining that if a tax on incomes were imposed, they themselves would be no party to such a practice. Perhaps it would help if I gave a little information as to the manner in which it is proposed that assessments should be made. It is intended that they should be drawn up and checked independently by at least three persons, including a senior officer of the department, and there would also be numerous indirect checks and cross references. I am prepared to give an assurance that the work of the department would be so arranged that organized corruption among the staff would be most difficult. Let me conclude my remarks on this point by quoting from Cobden—"There is nothing worse in the income tax than in the tax on tea or even wine. The income tax is no more demoralising than customs evasion and inspection, or more destructive to trade." 

As to the cost of collection, let me say at once that over the period 1917 to 1922 the cost of collection amounted to a little more than 1½% of the gross proceeds of the tax on incomes then in force. Any suggestion that the cost of the administration of the tax would be out of all proportion to the yield is entirely without any basis in fact. It is not borne out by the figure which I have just quoted, nor is it supported by those who are best qualified to advise Government as to the probable cost. It is estimated that this Bill would produce in the present year the sum of $10 millions, and that the total cost of collection is not likely to exceed 2% of the yield.

===== Page 113 =====
I should like, Sir, to close my remarks with these words: I am confident that all right-thinking people of this Colony who are capable of appreciating what they have at stake in the war will loyally submit to a tax on their incomes. I, as much as they, deplore the circumstances which make such a measure necessary—we got on very well without it in peace-time—but my attitude in the existing circumstances is what it has consistently been since the outbreak of war and the reason for this is that I know that a tax on incomes today is right.

When merchants embark on a common adventure by dispatching their merchandise in the same ship and it becomes necessary during the voyage to sacrifice a part of the cargo in order to secure the safety of the vessel and the remaining cargo, the loss which results has to be borne proportionately by all who are interested. The members of the British Empire have embarked on a common purpose and it is necessary for the preservation of the Empire, and thus of their property, that certain extraordinary sacrifices should be made. The people of this Colony are now being asked to bear their shares in proportion to their interests and I submit, Sir, that it is beyond all question that the best and most reliable instrument for computing these shares is the tax on incomes and profits, and it is in the confident belief that the Bill will be received in that spirit that I now move the first reading. (Applause).

The Colonial Secretary: Sir, I rise to second the motion. I wish to present another aspect of it than the financial motives of which the Hon. the Financial Secretary has spoken. This Bill gives the people of the Colony the chance which the people of Great Britain have grasped so eagerly of making a direct gift towards the colossal task of financing the war. In making that contribution we shall only be doing our duty and paying a debt. This land when it became part of the Empire was an empty land. It has now become a country to which many generations of immigrants have journeyed to enjoy the advantages of a peaceful and equitable order of government and have prospered, many exceedingly, all sufficiently in comparison with the prospects which awaited them had they stayed in their own country. Of that wealth they have been required to give very little to the land whose power and influence in the world and whose conception of government have secured for them the conditions under which they have flourished. But it is not a question of Great Britain expecting any such return. It should not have to ask for it. If there is a flicker of conscience, a spark of gratitude in us we should be eager to offer it and, because against the needs and the efforts of Great Britain itself the most we can do cannot but be pitiably small, we should be anxious to make it seem large by the manner of our giving.

Hitherto we have contributed by paying a little more for our luxuries, by making others pay a little more for what we sell them, by causing the heirs of dead men to get less than they may have expected. But we have not yet done anything that hurts, we have not yet attained sacrifice or even self-denial. This tax may demand that. It will make the present difficult for many and the future disappointing for others. But if it is accepted as doing this and willingly given, it will have a value far surpassing any contribution we have made in the past, for we shall at last be making a sacrifice and taking one of the few ways open to us to bring the conduct of our lives in these times a little nearer to the heroic sufferings of the people in Great Britain. We shall not be within sight, by many horizons, of the blood and the sweat, the toil and the tears which the Prime Minister prophesied with such terrible truth would be their lot. But we shall have taken the road.

There may still be those who criticize this as mere sentiment. Sentiment it undoubtedly is. But sentiment has come in again. It came in during the dreadful days of last year's early summer. It was sentiment that saved England and this Colony. The love of country, honour, self-respect, these were some of the sentiments which drew us out of the shadow of destruction into the prospect of victory. I cannot believe that anyone can behold the spirit of those who are

===== Page 114 =====
Enduring in Great Britain, their stoical acceptance of suffering, their efforts and their abnegations, their choice of death rather than defeat, and not be conscious of a profound emotion, not be anxious to make the sacrifices that are within his power in order to make him one with those brave hearts.

And that is why the Government has never dropped the idea of direct taxation. In our view it could never be dropped, for here was an essential gesture of loyalty and devotion to a common cause which one would have thought was clamouring to be made. That is why, too, it has never been our conception of our duty that Government should bring a Bill for direct taxation to you, cap in hand, with blandishments and coaxings. There is a duty to be done and a debt to be paid. His Excellency made that clear enough in his broadcast speech. But at the same time it was realised that contribution through direct taxation would lose its merit—and it is sufficiently a departure from trodden ways to acquire merit—if it were forced upon the country. We have not been the ventriloquist's doll for any Secretary of State, we have not lobbied, we have not plotted to fasten any financial fetters upon other States or to employ any of the subtle devilments of which we have been accused. What need was there for them? All along Government has held the view that there was only one creditable course for the Colony, that of acceptance of direct taxation and it believed the day would come when a majority of right-minded men would agree. But it had to wait for that day. The consequences of a severe rejection of the Bill would have been grave. In the eyes and in the memory of the mother country and the rest of the Empire this would have been the Colony which would not agree to tax itself directly. And however highly the opponents may esteem their arguments against the form of taxation the Bill imposes, it is unlikely that they will gain much sympathy elsewhere. For good cause must be shown why a tax which has been accepted and administered well enough not only in the Empire but in the countries surrounding the Colony should be rejected in the Straits Settlements. It is not enough to argue that there will be evasion and corruption. It must be shown that the people of this Colony so far surpass neighbouring countries in their capacity for those vices that where the latter are able to administer direct taxation we are powerless to do so. I don't know whether you honourable gentlemen accept that proposition. I certainly do not and it is not a pleasant thought that anyone should.

But whether I am right or wrong in this, I feel that this Bill must pass. The good name of the Colony demands that it should pass. In the last few days the gravity of our position has been made clear. We have been told that we cannot pay for the armaments which are essential to victory. Surely we cannot stay to argue against handing over some part of our pay or our profits when that is the plight of Great Britain. Surely we cannot turn to others for help when our own contribution is a few dollars if we smoke or drink and, if we nurse neither vice, not even that. Let us pass this Bill so that it may be known that we welcome the principle of direct giving. Let us not talk about conditions and reservations. On the contrary, let us resolve as individuals and leaders of communities to join in the fight against the evasion and corruption, if any there be. Even if the gloomiest predictions of the critics come true, we shall have at least laid up this treasure that we provided the means whereby the people of this Colony can link themselves by their own direct action with the endeavours and sacrifices of those who are fighting our common battle across the seas. (Applause).

The Bill was read a first time.

The Governor:—I gather that it would be to the convenience of hon. members if we adjourn now. I suggest we should meet again at 2.15.

WAR TAX (ADDITIONAL RUBBER DUTY) BILL.

The Attorney-General: Sir, I beg to move that a Bill for imposing additional duty on rubber for war purposes be read a first time. This additional tax on rubber will be an alternative to the income tax which has already been explained this morning. If that Bill is passed this Bill will entitle anybody who pays this tax to claim exemption under Clause 7 of the War Tax Bill when it becomes law. In Committee on the Bill I shall move an amendment which will provide for exemption within a limited period in the case of persons exporting rubber which they can prove was delivered to them and paid for before the coming into force of the Bill. Brunei ... 8,370
North Borneo ... 33,600

REVENUE COLLECTED IN RESPECT OF RUBBER, TIN AND TIN-ORE.

1. Mr. W. G. C. Blunn: During the quarter ended 31st December, 1940, or, failing that, during the quarter ended 30th September, 1940, what was the amount of revenue collected in respect of the 2½ per cent. ad valorem duty imposed under the War Taxation Enactment, 1940, on

(a) Rubber?
(b) Tin and Tin-ore?

Written reply: During the period April to December, 1940, the amount of revenue collected in respect of the 2½ per cent. ad valorem duty imposed under the War Taxation Enactment, 1940, was:

(a) $2,784,607.98.
(b) $2,260,425.01.

2. Mr. W. G. C. Blunn: What is the estimated additional revenue which, except for the exemption of exporters liable to pay income tax in the United Kingdom as provided by the said Enactment, would have been collected on

(a) Rubber?
(b) Tin and Tin-ore?

Written reply: The estimated additional revenue which but for exemptions would have been collected during the period April to December, 1940, is:

(a) $1,617,902.47.
(b) $1,065,760.21.

RESOLUTIONS.

AMENDMENTS TO THE WAR TAXATION ENACTMENT 1940.

The Federal Secretary: Sir, the resolution standing in my name, and the immediately succeeding one, is, as this Council is aware, the outcome of the recommendations made by the Joint Committee whose Report has been laid on the table to-day. It is, I think, unnecessary for me to say anything more on that subject.

There is, however, one small point which I should like to mention and which I know has been exercising the attention of at least one honourable member here. I refer to the question of stocks in the hands of millers and in the hands of the two smelting companies in the Federated Malay States purchased before the date of the introduction of this resolution but not exported until after these new duties come into force. I had the opportunity when I was in Singapore last week of discussing this question with a representative of the Straits Trading Co., and my honourable friend the Legal Adviser had a similar opportunity of discussing the matter in Kuala Lumpur with a representative of the largest milling concern in the Federation. Both these representatives agreed that sufficient notice of the imposition of these new duties had been given to enable the smelter and rubber millers and dealers so to adjust their purchase price that they would base it on the assumption that the new duties would be enforced before they could export the rubber or tin purchased. Should there be any bona fide case of difficulty, appropriate action can be taken under clause 7 of the Enactment. I think, Sir, that this is satisfactory to all concerned.

===== Page 118 =====
Those sources, like those export duties which we have dealt with today, postage and licences, this Council might wish to increase for reasons not necessarily those which prompted the additional special war taxation. For that reason, it was felt wise, for the future, to make it possible to increase taxes from those sources for other purposes than as a gift home, but at the same time to leave it entirely in the control of this Council to decide what proportion of any new or additional taxes should still be sent home as a gift and what proportion should be conserved for local use, although also for war purposes.

That being so, the Government thought it well to consult the Home Government as to whether it would have any objection to such an amendment, and a cable has been received from the Secretary of State saying that he has no objection to the proposed amendment to the War Taxation Enactment, No. 6 of 1940, and he fully agrees that the first call on the Malayan war taxation should be for the purpose of Malayan defence. The cable adds that His Majesty's Government attach the utmost importance to maintaining those defences at the highest state of efficiency.

The amendment, if passed, will mean that any additional taxation can still be used only for war purposes but that it can in part or in whole be used for local purposes of defence instead of for Home purposes of defence.

While it is not for me to go into any financial question, it must be obvious to any thinking man that we cannot tell what new expenditure in connection with the war we may be faced with in the future and perhaps in the very near future here in Malaya, and for that reason, and that reason only, it was considered necessary to give more elasticity to the scope of this Bill in providing that all revenue imposed by it, although it must be used for war purposes, might be used locally.

As I have said, however, the control of its allocation remains, as before, in effect in the hands of this Council.

I hope that explanation has cleared up any misapprehensions in the matter. (Applause.)

The motion is carried by nineteen votes to five.

COMMITTEE.

Council resolves itself into Committee.

Clause 2 of the Bill is deleted and the following is substituted therefor:

"2. In the Preamble to the Principal Enactment the first reciting paragraph is amended by deleting the colon at the end thereof and by adding the words 'and that certain expenses be incurred within the Federated Malay States in the prosecution of the said war' and the second reciting paragraph is amended by deleting the words 'the purpose' and by substituting therefor the words 'these purposes' and by deleting the words 'of such contribution'."

The remainder of the Bill is agreed to without amendment.

The Legal Adviser: Sir, I beg to report that this Bill has been considered in Committee and has been agreed to with amendments.

Council resumes.

PASSING.

The President puts the question that the Bill, as amended, do now pass.

The Bill, as amended, is passed and becomes Enactment No. 4 of 1941.

THE WAR TAXATION BILL.

FIRST READING.

The Legal Adviser: Sir, a certificate of urgency in this case is in the possession of the Clerk of Council. Before moving the first reading and in order to avoid any possibility of misunderstanding I should like to take your ruling on the following point: I submit that on the first reading, under Standing Orders of this Council, so long as the motion is seconded, the first reading is a mere formality.

Under Standing Order 29, "the Mover of the Bill, on moving the first reading thereof, shall state the object and intention of the measure and the reasons on which it is founded."

Under Standing Order 30, "if such motion is seconded by another member the Bill shall be read a first time."

If this is so, I do not wish to address the Council at length on my motion for the first reading.

The President: That, I think, is the correct procedure. The explanation of the provisions of the Bill is normally taken on the motion for the second reading.

The Legal Adviser: Therefore, Sir, I beg to move that this Bill be read a first time and for the moment I will refer only to the printed Objects and Reasons.

The Financial Secretary: Sir, I second the motion.

The Bill is read a first time.

===== Page 119 =====
SECOND READING.

The Legal Adviser: Sir, I beg to lay on the table a certificate of emergency, and to move that the Standing Orders relative to the publication, reading and commitment of the Bill be suspended so that it may be taken through all its stages at this meeting. I understand, however, as appears in the foot-note in the Orders of the Day, that the intention is today only to debate the motion for the second reading, and if and when that motion is passed, to adjourn this meeting to another day for the committee stage.

I beg to move that the Standing Orders be suspended.

The Financial Secretary: Sir, I second the motion.

The motion is agreed to.

The Legal Adviser: Sir, I beg to move that the War Taxation Bill be read a second time.

Sir, in rising to make that motion, I am reminded of an occasion some years ago in another part of this Empire. I was not a member of the legislature but a Judge on the Bench, and it fell to my lot to have to try a very difficult murder case. A man had been brutally murdered in his sleep by thieves who wished to rob him of his small savings. He happened to be a man who was very popular in his village and therefore the murder had raised a great deal of local feeling. Two men were charged but the evidence against them was purely circumstantial and it required very careful sifting and weighing. At the end of the fourth day of the trial all the evidence for the prosecution and defence had been given and, before adjourning the Court, I reminded the jury that theirs would be a heavy responsibility of giving a just decision in the matter, that they had heard all the evidence but that they had not yet heard the speeches of counsel on both sides, nor my own directions to them, and that it was essential that they should all, in accordance with the oaths that they had taken, keep an open mind on the matter until the moment came for them actually to give their decision. Sir, when I took my seat on the Bench the next morning my eye was attracted by something unusual in the aspect of the jury box. I looked again and saw in this box, which was supposed to hold twelve open-minded men, twelve jurors who had arrived in Court wearing black coats and black ties, out of respect, they later explained, for those whom their verdict would condemn to death.

Sir, I hope that some of my honourable and unofficial friends who have hitherto opposed this Bill have not arrived in black coats and black ties this morning, mentally if not physically. I am not suggesting, of course, that today I am in the position of being in any way a judge in the matter, nor, I would stress, am I in the position of being an advocate paid to express views which do not happen to be my own. (The President: Hear, hear). I merely claim to be one of the jury, one who, like themselves, is entitled to state his opinion on the question which we all have to resolve between us. It is obviously a question of the very greatest importance, one that calls for calm deliberation and the elimination of all prejudgments even at the last moment.

It is unfortunate that the discussion of this question should have proceeded hitherto in an atmosphere of controversy, an atmosphere which to my knowledge has even led in certain quarters (not necessarily connected with this Council) to unfounded allegations, such as the suggestion that the Secretary of State has directed this Government to impose income tax in the Federated Malay States. Your Excellency has already in this very Council refuted that suggestion and made it clear that the Secretary of State has in fact done no such thing. Let us, if possible, forget all controversy which has hitherto arisen: all of us as members of Council have a heavy responsibility to discharge; and I do ask all those who have expressed any opposition to the Bill, whatever prejudices or prejudgments they may have formed, today to reopen their minds and not to be afraid to study the question anew, for it is only really today, in open debate in this House, that they ought to decide this question, and decide it not only for themselves but on behalf of all those whom they represent.

As honourable members know, the Bill is the result of the labours of a Committee which was appointed to consider the matter. As honourable members may not all know, the deliberations of that Committee were not in fact confined to income tax alone. I was not a member of that Committee but I attended its deliberations and I can personally vouch for the fact that the members of the Committee examined the whole question, examined it impartially and as carefully as was possible in the circumstances, and studied not only direct taxation but all possible sources of war taxation. They did so far more deeply and far more impartially than they have, in some quarters at any rate, been given credit for. I also know that as a result of these deliberations the Committee were finally driven to the conclusion that some form of income taxation was essential if the Federated Malay States—I am speaking principally of the members who represented the Federated Malay States—were to make anything approaching its fair contribution to this war. I will try to follow very shortly the path of the Committee's reasoning.

===== Page 120 =====
Firstly, it was known that there was at any rate agreement on one point—even if on only one—and that was agreement that the Federated Malay States in so far as it concerns us, and Malaya as a whole, had up till now paid nothing like their proper contribution to this Empire's war expenditure. How much the Federated Malay States ought to pay it is very difficult to estimate in figures. I believe that a learned economist has suggested it might amount to $150,000,000 a year: a more modest figure of $10,000,000 is referred to in the Committee's Report; but I do not think anybody has suggested that that should be in any way the highest amount that it is desirable for the Federated Malay States to contribute. What I think is clear is that the Federated Malay States should pay, in this crisis in the Empire's history, the maximum that it can possibly afford to pay; and that, quite obviously, is a great deal more than $10,000,000 a year.

"How then," said the Committee at the beginning of its deliberations, "was this war taxation to be raised?" In a country like the Federated Malay States which possesses what is sometimes referred to as a customs barrier, one's thoughts naturally turn first to possible increases in customs duties, both import and export.

Now, as to export duties, it appeared to the Committee, and their feelings have been justified by what has been said in Council to-day, that there was again here at least a measure of universal agreement—which included the rubber and tin industries themselves—that these industries could and should bear the burden of increased export duty. They recommended such taxation in the way that you know and the Government has accepted the views that they put forward. They did so by first saying that these export duties should be increased but, when they came to deal with direct taxation, they said that those who paid these increased export duties should be exempted from such direct taxation. No one has suggested that there is any mathematical exactitude of comparison as between the payment of export duty and the payment of income tax. It would be idle to try to criticise the income tax Bill by figures showing that if any particular company had paid income tax it would have paid more or less than what it pays in export duty. That was not the idea. It is a totally different source of taxation and it was felt that once that taxation had been imposed and willingly borne those people who bore it might properly be exempted altogether from the other form of taxation. I think it is agreed that, at any rate at the figure recommended of 2½ per cent., such additional duties would not produce more than approximately $6,000,000 a year. What other sources were there? There was a possibility of increasing duties on alcohol and tobacco. These can be considered luxuries and such increases could obviously be considered and, I understand, are being considered by this Government. But here again, the increases which it would be possible to put on those items could not produce more than something in the nature of a million or two of additional revenue. Petrol again was examined. It was thought that there might be difficulties—some far-reaching effects on the transport of the country—in increases there, and careful consideration must obviously be given to aspect of the matter. In any case I understand that the most expert view available is that any permissible increase could not produce more than something in the nature of a million dollars at the most. There are, of course, many other important duties, but it was felt that in some cases these could not be increased without adversely affecting the poorer classes of the community—those least able to bear increased taxation—and others could not without bringing into play, if they were increased substantially, what is generally referred to as the law of diminishing returns: that is to say, if you increase taxation beyond a certain limit, people just stop buying that particular article and the net result is that you get less revenue than you would otherwise get had the rate of tax been kept at the lower level.

It therefore became apparent that some new source of taxation was imperative and that it must be a source which could produce really important sums of revenue and which would ensure—and I think you will agree that this is a most important point—that all persons in the country, except those of really limited means, should bear some part of the burden. Now, it seemed to the Committee, as it seems to me, that a tax on income is the only tax which can fairly meet both those requirements. But if this is so, why is there ever any opposition to that form of taxation? Let us try and analyse the objections, so far as I know them.

I will deal in a minute with the objections which have been expressed, but I suggest for the consideration of those members who have expressed objections, that the fundamental reason for objecting to income tax is really instinctive and psychological and not one that will bear the cold light of reason and logic. Being human beings, most of us don't mind paying more for our whisky so long as we are left to decide whether we buy a bottle or not. We do instinctively object (especially if we earn our income) to the Government reaching its hands into our till and taking, out of every $100, $10 in cash before we have had an opportunity of spending it for ourselves. That is putting the matter perhaps simply and crudely, but I think anybody who

===== Page 121 =====
Has given much thought will agree that that is really the basis of such objections as there are to income tax. I have said it was instinctive rather than reasoned and I suggest that that is borne out by the fact that throughout this vast Empire of ours practically every part of it has accepted income tax as part of its fiscal system. If honourable members are sufficiently interested I can even read to them a list of the various territories, from the United Kingdom and the big Dominions down to Protected States and Mandated Territories—important Colonies and even tiny colonies like Saint Lucia, British Honduras and Grenada which impose income taxation. Those who know me will appreciate that I am the last person who would suggest that because a law is good elsewhere the same law must necessarily be suitable to the Federated Malay States, but this almost universal acceptance throughout the Empire of income tax does surely suggest that any objections to it cannot be so fundamental as its opponents would have us believe.

Sometimes we are told that the objections are based on the mixture of races here. Perhaps some members do not know that some of the West Indies contain a very large proportion of Indians and Chinese also and even of Syrian merchants, and I can assure honourable members that a Syrian merchant has nothing to learn about the evasion of taxation. (Laughter.)

What are the other objections? There has been expressed a fear that once income tax is imposed it will become permanent. This Council knows that it cannot bind its successors in the future: it cannot say to Councils yet unborn in circumstances yet unknown: "You shall never in the future re-impose income tax" but so far as this Council is concerned the answer to that objection is clear and, I say, cannot be denied. It is, first, that the provisions of the Bill make it quite plain that the tax is imposed for war purposes only and, second, that the Bill is merely a Bill dealing with the year 1941. It can only be re-imposed in 1942 in the same terms or in similar terms if Council then chooses to impose it afresh.

A somewhat similar objection which has been made is that a fresh Government department will be formed and it is said that once a Government department is formed it sticks on like a limpet and will not allow itself to be removed, and that that, in itself, would mean that income tax would become permanent. If this Council requires details of the organisation necessary to work this Bill, or the cost of collection (which is another objection sometimes put forward) I have no doubt they will be given the information by my honourable friend the Financial Secretary, but I can say this of my own knowledge: that if this Bill is passed no new Government department will be necessary or will be formed. The administration will be under the control of the head of an existing Department; the necessary personnel will all either be engaged on a purely temporary basis or will be the personnel of established Government services such as the Colonial Legal Service, the Colonial Corps of Accountants or the General Clerical Service which, as soon as income tax ceases, can very soon be re-absorbed into other posts staffed by those general services. Perhaps it is not always realised how often moves are necessary, even in my comparatively small service, how many times in a year we require to fill posts or recruit fresh personnel owing to retirements, moves and promotions.

As to estimated cost, I am no expert and that must of course depend on a somewhat unknown result—the amount which will in fact be obtained—but I understand that even in the first year of collection—the only year if it is not renewed—the cost will certainly not be higher than something from two per cent to five per cent of the total collected.

Other objectors have said that there will be evasion. I am not going to say that there won't be. Of course there will be attempts at evasion as there always are, and always have been, against almost any kind of taxation. We all know how many people otherwise honourable think it not beneath their dignity to attempt to evade customs duties, and how many persons, otherwise honourable, when acting as executors, attempt to evade estate duty. That is a thing which must be foreseen in connection with nearly every form of taxation; but the provisions of this Bill have been deliberately simplified and made such as to render evasion difficult and I can assure honourable members that in my capacity as Public Prosecutor I will do my best to make evasion costly. (The President: Hear, hear.)

Then another objection which has been raised is that which is usually summarised in one word—inquisition. Now, the first answer to that is that no inquisition is necessary with the honest taxpayer; secondly, that the provisions which provide for the taxation of companies and partnerships are such as to obviate the necessity of much enquiry into the private affairs of individuals; thirdly, I will point out that clause 59 of the Bill imposes on all persons connected with income tax the absolute duty of secrecy, and makes them punishable with heavy fines if they break that oath of secrecy; fourthly, if I may say so with deference, this objection is one of instinct rather than of reason, an objection raised principally by the Chinese community.

===== Page 122 =====
I would remind my honourable friends that the same objections were raised in much the same way, principally by the Chinese, and to some extent by the Indian community, to the Registration of Businesses Bill. This Council nevertheless passed it, and I think I can say without fear of contradiction that its provisions are now being carried out quite smoothly, and I certainly can say that I myself know of no single complaint from any source that there has been anything in the nature of inquisition in connection with its provisions.

Another objection which has been raised is that this legislation would breed corruption. Again, I am not going to say that that objection can be totally disregarded or ought to be brushed aside. There would certainly be temptation in some cases but I can assure the Council that machinery has already been thought out which is based on experience and provides systems of counter-checking which should very much lessen the danger of any such corruption. Also, the recent experience of Government officers who were foolish enough to allow themselves to be bribed will, I trust, provide some discouragement of such practices in the future.

Lastly—perhaps almost in desperation if I may say so—the objectors have sought to examine the provisions of the Bill itself and to say that the burden is unevenly distributed by its provisions. I am afraid the answer is that no tax which human intelligence has yet devised has fallen as an even burden on everybody. Take the tax I have mentioned—a tax on whisky: if you are a teetotaller, you don't bear any portion of it; if you are, like myself, a very moderate drinker, you bear a very small portion; but if you are a man like a good friend of mine, and a good citizen, who said to me a few days ago: "To me whisky is a necessary medicine: I cannot go on without my whisky and however highly you tax it, I must buy it" it falls on him more heavily than on either of us. The same thing applies to taxes on textiles; the richer can more easily afford to pay more, however indirectly, than the poor who equally need them. Besides, if I may say so with all respect, any accusation that the provisions of this Bill will have an unequal effect comes, to my mind, very ill from persons who are themselves suggesting that all the taxation necessary should be extracted from the rubber and tin industries. What could be more uneven, even allowing for the fact that rubber and tin provide the real riches of this country, than that they should be the only ones to pay? Why should I not have to pay or any of us who either by General Orders or for other reasons cannot have any interest in rubber and tin in this country? It comes even more ill from other opponents who have suggested so-called alternative forms of taxation. It must be clear that no legislation can be imposed which can be wholly equitable in its incidence—the Committee had to aim at simplicity, and simplicity is incompatible in taxation with complete equity. The more you want a tax to be just the more complicated must be its provisions, and if there is in fact any unavoidable inequality the answer must be a rough and ready one (and these are rough and ready times) and that is that as the rates of taxation are so low, any injustice will be proportionately small. I should add that that is my opinion on the provisions as I knew them. I do not for a moment pretend that this is a perfect Bill and obviously if, in the light of experience of the working of it, it appears that any of its provisions do cause any unfairness, there is nothing easier than to amend them in the light of experience, even during the course of the year 1941. There is not a problem in the world which is all pros and no cons: if there were such, it would not be a problem at all.

Now, if I may say so with deference, the opponents of this tax have opposed it for some considerable time without suggesting any reasonable alternative sources of taxation. Some of the bitterest opponents have remained silent right up to this moment. When you have a good case you can disclose it: when your case is weak it may be good tactics to say nothing about it until the last moment. The Government has disclosed its case at the earliest possible moment, but the opponents have not until very recently suggested any alternatives, in public meeting, in the Press, or in petitions. When pressed to do so, some gentlemen who opposed it have put forward tentative suggestions of their own, notably my honourable and learned friend Mr. Erroll Shearn. Now, as his suggestions, I think, included all those put forward, some vaguely by other opponents and, in addition, some of his very own, I should like briefly to examine them. One of the alternative suggested is lotteries. I am not going to state any opinions to-day on that debatable question. I will only say this: whether lotteries be good or bad, would any one of you be satisfied if our main effort was based, not on any personal sacrifice by a majority, but purely by exploiting the gambling instincts of the population at large? I suggest that that would be a very strange patriotism. (Honourable Members: Hear, hear.)

Other suggestions made have been for increases in licences, fees and the like. Some of them, I fear, are impracticable, such as pawnbroker's licences since they are given for three years and sold by tender. As regards a tax on money-lenders, though it would cause me no heart-burning to help to impose one, I am confident that the tax, however skilfully the legislation was drafted, would merely be passed on

===== Page 123 =====
To the unfortunate borrower. Some of the suggestions, if I may state a personal opinion, are worthy of consideration, such as a possible increase in Court fees which in this country are much lower than they are in England, but, unfortunately, none of them will produce any very substantial results. My honourable and learned friend I think himself realised that point—that these other sources of taxation could not produce anything really substantial and he must therefore go further afield, and he has in a letter to the Press indicated others. Shortly put, what are they? First, a tax on salaries; second, a tax on profits of local companies; third, a tax on land values; fourth, a tax on loans and other smaller investments. But what are those taxes? They are nothing less than taxes on certain sources of income—in other words, they are income tax.

Now what about the charge of unevenness? How can it be said that it would be just to tax the income of a man which comes from one source but not the income of a man which comes from the other? I am sure that all honourable members have read in the Press a letter from my obviously learned but modestly anonymous friend “Klyne Street” who pointed out this anomaly. I need not stress it but I may say that it struck not only myself but one of the bitterest champions of the opposition to income tax. Generally, that sort of solution was really that which was sought in Hongkong. It was studied by the Committee but it was rejected because of the really ludicrous injustices which it would bring about.

What did these proposals really amount to? They amounted to an admission that income tax was necessary. That is clear. They amounted, however, to a suggestion that we should only tax the honest man and the man who could not be dishonest because his income could be readily ascertainable, and that we should exempt the dishonest man who could fake his books and make it difficult for us to ascertain what his income really was. I can think of no more unjust system of taxation.

Gentlemen, it was along those lines that the Committee came to the realisation that half measures were impracticable, and therefore recommended the re-adoption, in substance and with necessary modifications, of legislation which had stood the test of practical experience for three years in the last war when it had been enacted in the Colony and had been in existence between 1917 and 1920. I have it on the authority of Mr. Roland Braddell that that legislation resulted in no more than two cases in which tax-payers thought it necessary to appeal to the Court of Appeal.

Gentlemen, let us examine very shortly the provisions of the Bill which is before us. I have no doubt you have studied it. I have no doubt that before any of you who are thinking of opposing have come to that conclusion, you have studied it minutely, and so I need only state its principal provisions quite shortly.

First of all, its rates are very low indeed. If you compare them with the rates at present obtaining at Home they are almost miserably low.

Secondly, it exempts persons whose incomes are below $4,800 a year. I have heard criticisms that bachelors will be unnecessarily exempt. If this is really so, we may possibly later amend it, but I would in passing point out that there are bachelors who have widowed mothers, sisters or other relatives to support and other responsibilities.

Then it taxes—this is one of the most important aspects—companies, associations and business partnership alike; it taxes the company on the income of the company; it taxes the partnership on the income of that business itself. That was done for simplicity and in order, as far as possible, to obviate this fear, as I have said, of inquisition.

Then it provides freedom from double taxation, not only where income tax is paid elsewhere, as I have said, but for those who are already fairly heavily taxed by means of export duties on certain products. The rate of taxation is based quite fairly on total income, but the only income which is in fact taxed is what I may shortly describe as local income.

There is a right of appeal to a Board which is presided over by a Judge of the Supreme Court and the tax-payer may further appeal to the Privy Council. If Government appealed then Government would pay the costs of the respondent whatever happened to that appeal. The Federal Council itself can relieve against any undue hardship, and, last but by no means least important, the proceeds are formally stated to be raised for war purposes only and the Bill dies at the end of 1941.

It may not be an ideal Bill but it is something which is urgently required, and it aims at approaching as nearly as possible the happy medium between simplicity and a fair measure of equality of burden. I am personally sorry that I have had no opportunity of discussing the whole question round a table and at some length with all the Unofficial members. I am quite confident that if that is possible even in the future, they will and must all eventually arrive at the conclusion that, whatever can be said against income taxation, it is the only practical form of war taxation available to us to-day, the only form which will make everyone pay who ought to pay and which will produce as much as ought to be produced.

What I do suggest members must not take the responsibility of doing is to vote against this Bill if they have nothing ready and sufficient to replace it. The need is urgent and if any members continue their opposition it may be of interest to them.

===== Page 124 =====
To learn with whom they will be allied in such opposition, I will quote from a thoroughly reliable source: "Opponents of income tax would probably be surprised, if not embarrassed, to learn that their opposition brings them into alliance, temporarily it is to be hoped, with the Malayan Communist Party which, in an article published in their newspaper 'Vanguard News,' also vigorously opposed this tax on the ground that its primary object was to assist towards the successful prosecution of the war!" Gentlemen, the opposition of honourable members may not have the same basis but I would warn them that it might have the same result if it were successful.

We must in the Federated Malay States bear our share, and we must in very decency bear a really full share of this Empire's burden. (The President: Hear, hear.) I realise that we are sincerely anxious, all of us, to do so, yet we could be accused today of living in selfish complacency—living perfectly normal, peace-time lives, denying ourselves none of our ordinary luxuries, none of our comforts, and none of our ordinary pleasures, while our fellow subjects of this Empire, our personal friends and even our relatives in England, face heavy financial sacrifices, personal inconveniences, and even death with grim but very cheerful courage.

Any safety we have here is due to the power of the Empire of which we form part, and for which those at Home are suffering while we ourselves are not. I am sincerely and personally convinced that this legislation, purely as a war measure, is essential if we are to bear our proper share of our Empire's defence; and it is therefore not as a servant of this Government but rather as an ordinary member of this Council, and an ordinary subject of that Empire, that I urge all my fellow members to speed this legislation on its course.

Sir, I have the honour to move that this Bill be now read a second time. (Applause.)

The Financial Secretary: Sir, in rising to second this motion, I wish to state shortly what the cost of collecting the taxes imposed by this Bill would amount to. The question of staff has been gone into carefully and the annual cost under Personal Emoluments and Other Charges should not exceed $150,000. There would also have to be some initial special expenditure in the provision of office accommodation and equipment.

To estimate what is the probable revenue is much more difficult. From the data available, I should put it at not less than $4,000,000. That would mean that the cost of collection would be rather less than 4 percent. In the Colony, the Financial Secretary has put the cost of collection of their income tax at about 2 per cent, but then they expect to collect about $10,000,000, and when once your organisation is in working order it costs very little more to collect $10,000,000 than to collect $4,000,000. I think that 4 per cent is a very reasonable price to pay for the collection of a tax.

Sir, I second the motion. (Applause.)

The President proposes the question that the Bill be now read a second time.

Raja Uda bin Raja Muhammad: Your Excellency, I have followed very closely the various arguments for and against the proposal to introduce income tax in this country which have appeared in the Press from time to time, and this morning I have listened with very great interest to the speech of the Honourable the Legal Adviser. My own view on this subject is this: in principle, a tax on incomes appears to be the most equitable form of taxation. And when it is imposed for the purpose of raising funds to prosecute a war in defence of our freedom, its imperfections should not be magnified. At the same time, it would not be wise policy needlessly to disturb the country in order to obtain what is after all a trifling sum in comparison with the cost of the war.

There is no doubt that the present prosperity of Malaya flows directly from the course of the war, and there are many substantial incomes derived from profitable occupations other than the production of tin and rubber, which do not appear to be contributing a fair share to the war effort.

It is always difficult to prevent the burden of any taxation falling ultimately on the poor. That there is a disinclination—perhaps a natural disinclination—for the individual to pay direct taxes is shown by the attempts to avoid paying customs dues at the ports even though the amount involved is well within the capacity of the individual to pay. But the proposals before us are not severe and I do not therefore believe that there will be many people who would attempt to evade their personal responsibility by passing it on to someone else.

Sir, income tax is new to these States and much of the opposition to it is due probably to the uncertainty of its effect in a country with so many and diverse activities. There are uncanny parts in the Bill before us which I think need adjustment, but with a sympathetic attitude towards the taxpayer and with goodwill on both sides, I for one cannot see that this country will ever have cause to regret this method of helping Great Britain to secure victory—a victory on which our own liberty and everything dear to us depend. (Applause.)

===== Page 125 =====
Mr. E. D. Shearn: Your Excellency, I am afraid I shall occupy the time of this Council for some considerable period, but before I open the case against income tax I should like to pay a sincere tribute to my honourable and learned friend for the great skill he portrayed here in making the best of a singularly bad case. If he has not recently had experience of advocacy, he can at least comfort himself that his hand has not lost its cunning. There is one slip, though, that I think he made in his apologia for this singularly bad Bill and that was the reference to the rates of duty being so small. I was forcibly reminded of what Midshipman Easy's nurse said when she was taxed with the production of a child in circumstances which she found rather difficult to explain.

Now we have before this Council a concrete proposal to introduce into these States income tax of a definite character and at definite rates. This proposal has found concrete form after some 15 months or more have elapsed since the commencement of this war. If the tax is eventually introduced it is thought by Your Excellency's Joint Committee which recently reported that with extra taxation of certain of the tin and rubber produced in the Federated Malay States an additional $10,000,000 will be raised in the Federated Malay States for the year 1941. Taking the figures of the 1941 estimates which have been accepted by this Council, the proposed extra taxation on tin and rubber at 2½ per cent on the sale price of these commodities will yield $6,000,000 in the year 1941. The proposal, therefore, is that for this year not more than $4,000,000 shall be raised by this income tax.

All the opponents of this tax are at one in thinking that Your Excellency whole-heartedly believes that the imposition of the tax is wise and is in the best interests of the Empire. Having paid that tribute we respectfully but unhesitatingly proclaim our disagreement with Your Excellency's views. The proposal to introduce income tax has had these results if nothing else, namely, that it has sown throughout the Federated Malay States the seeds of discord and disharmony and the financial war effort of the Federated Malay States has been most seriously cramped and impaired ever since this ill-considered proposal was first mooted. Some critics, and their number is not small, nor are they persons of no account in this country, have advanced the view that Government has deliberately designed to make it appear that without income tax as advocated by Your Excellency these States cannot adequately contribute to the Empire's War Effort. These critics are not unmindful of the attitude of Government towards the proposal of a War Loan. They are of the opinion that Government was dilatory as regards the raising of it, and they do not forget that, although the motion which I proposed in April or May last was carried without any dissentient vote, it was not until September that the loan of $20,000,000 was floated, and it was not until October that the money was obtained.

If income tax in its first year is estimated to yield in the Federated Malay States not more than $4,000,000, I can at least claim to have done something towards letting the Imperial Government have in the year 1940 considerably more money than would have been transmitted had the proposals put forward by Your Excellency early in 1940 been accepted and we had contributed merely the monies to be obtained from income tax and had not raised the loan I pressed for. Government has to date appointed three Committees to advise on the subject of income tax. The first one was a Joint Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States Committee appointed in the year 1939. That Committee considered the question of income tax but advised Your Excellency that revenue should be obtained from other sources. The report of that Committee has never been published. Subsequently, Your Excellency made a broadcast address advocating income tax. The Federal Council would have nothing to do with this proposal but in April or May last decided that a War Loan should be raised. Thereafter a Committee was appointed to advise as to the terms, etc., of the loan.

It is thought by many that, so far as Government is concerned, the appointment of that Committee was inspired by the hope that by a careful selection of the members comprising the Committee there might be secured some pronouncement to help in the campaign which was then being waged behind the scenes by Government to obtain acceptance of Your Excellency's income tax proposals. That hope was not entirely unjustified. Certain of the members of that Committee were prepared to subject themselves inevitably to considerable ridicule by advocating the imposition of income tax to service a loan of $20,000,000 repayable in some 12-19 years. That means that less than $2,000,000 would be required each year. The majority report of that Committee which advocated income tax contained this never to be forgotten passage:

"We, most of us, agree that so far as the Federated Malay States are concerned an income tax, whatever its theoretical merits may be, in practice would be neither a very equitable nor an economical form of taxation."

===== Page 126 =====
The efforts of this Committee appear to have encouraged Government to make yet another try, and the Joint Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States Committee which has recently reported was appointed by Government.

I do not propose to deal in detail with the Bill to which the Committee has given its blessing. So far as the Federated Malay States are concerned I am resolutely and absolutely opposed in principle to the Bill becoming law. There was in the remarks made by my honourable and learned friend the Legal Adviser, a somewhat unfortunate reference to juries. Some of us consider that some of the juries appointed to consider this matter were "packed juries". The gentlemen who constituted this Committee were all known to have accepted Your Excellency's proposal that there should be an income tax and the terms of reference were to study problems connected with income tax and they were told that their deliberations need not be confined to the Colony Draft Bill for income tax but that they were free to formulate other proposals for taxation on similar lines. I ask honourable members to note the words "free to formulate proposals for taxation on similar lines". The freedom of this Committee appears to have been so strictly limited as scarcely to merit the word "freedom" at all. It is, therefore, idle to claim that the report of the Committee provides evidence that income tax is acceptable to the Federated Malay States. Your Excellency is aware that throughout the Federated Malay States there is very strong opposition to this measure. If Government denies the existence of the opposition in the Federated Malay States to income tax then it will be but yet another example of that attitude of Government which is not uncommon when faced with an unwelcome situation, viz., not to deal with the situation but to pretend that the situation does not exist. I maintain that that attitude was adopted for years by Government towards the problem of bribery and corruption in the Mines Department and in other departments of Government. I can, if necessary, give other instances of this attitude of Government when faced with an unwelcome situation but I do not consider it of importance to do so.

The petition opposing income tax which has recently been signed by and on behalf of a number of associations, bodies and persons throughout the Federated Malay States makes it difficult for Government to proceed on the basis that the proposal to introduce income tax into the Federated Malay States meets with general support throughout these States. If income tax is imposed it will be contrary to the wishes of the majority of tax-payers in the Federated Malay States and contrary, I think, to the views of the majority of the Unofficial Members of this Council. While we oppose the tax we unreservedly agree that the Federated Malay States must make a far larger contribution to the Empire's war finances than hitherto.

I ask myself two questions: Firstly, why is Government so determined upon having an income tax in the Federated Malay States and, secondly, why do I find myself so firmly opposed to the proposal that we should have income tax?

I answer my first question in this way: Government has realized that income tax is imperative in the Colony of the Straits Settlements. Government considered that it was difficult to have income tax in the Colony and not in the Federated Malay States. An awkward situation might arise as regards the salaries of Government servants and there might be a flight of capital from the Colony to the Federated Malay States.
