We in the Federated Malay States realize only too well that the High Commissioner for the Malay States resides principally in Singapore and that a number of the Senior officers of Government who are to-day present in this chamber also reside in the Colony. I have previously submitted and to-day from conviction I stoutly maintain that the interests of the Federated Malay States in a number of matters are subordinated to the interests of the Colony. This is an unfortunate consequence of that decentralization policy which in fact so successfully centralizes control in Singapore. Less and less it would seem does the Governor of the Straits Settlements learn from the High Commissioner of the Malay States the essential differences between the two territories. More and more it would appear does the Governor of the Straits Settlements adhere to the view that Malaya is one country and what is sauce for the Singapore goose is sauce for the Federated Malay States gander. It does not appear to be sufficiently appreciated that the inhabitants of the Federated Malay States pay heavy duties on imported goods and that the inhabitants of the Colony do not pay these duties. It does not appear to be sufficiently realized that because of the essential differences, both economic and fiscal, between the Colony of the Straits Settlements and the Federated Malay States there are sources of revenue open to the tax gatherer in the Federated Malay States which are not open to his opposite number in the Colony. I answer my first question then that the insistence by Government upon income tax being imposed in the Federated Malay States is dictated by the consideration that such imposition will facilitate Government's task in the Colony. Incidentally we now have the unedifying picture of a British Colony with practically no import duties proposing to have income tax at a similar rate to protected States which have heavy import duties. This appears to be exacting an unfair price for protection.

===== Page 127 =====
As regards my second question, I am so firmly opposed to income tax being introduced into the Federated Malay States because, in the first place, the proposal necessitates the creation of a new Government department or the enormous expansion of an existing Government department, and this new department or the particular branch of the expanded department will perforce have to scrutinize books of account kept in vernacular languages. The contact in these circumstances between the employees of the department and the public will, as certainly as night follows day, give rise to an increase of the bribery and corruption which is rife throughout Government service in the Federated Malay States. I can perhaps claim to know as much as or perhaps more than most people as to the corrupt practices of Government servants. The details that I have learnt as Chairman of the Mines Commission are nuanced in the extreme, and I, in common with most unofficials, know that corruption is not limited to the Mines Department. I have discussed the question with officers of Government who have come to these States from other parts of the Colonial Empire, and I have been told that the position here is infinitely worse than in the other parts of the Colonial Empire with which they are familiar. If, Sir, you challenge this, I say the answer is that exalted position of necessity entails a certain amount of isolation. Those who enter the arena are more familiar with the blood and dust of every day life than those who sit on high. If, Sir, the answer put forward is that those in control have their advisers, then I rejoin that though there are among both official and unofficial advisers those who dispense soothing syrup rather than prescribe the candy of candour, I know that there are among Your Excellency's advisers more than one who would, if asked, give Your Excellency their candid opinions as to the widespread existence of this evil. Now, Sir, neither of those Committees which have advocated income tax have referred to the problem of bribery and corruption nor have they attempted to advise Your Excellency how the evil is to be prevented or even checked. It is too difficult, so no doubt they pretended that the problem does not exist. Personally, I find it difficult to understand how any Government claiming not merely that it exists but that in the proper sense of the word that it governs can seek unnecessarily to inflict on these unfortunate States a further horde of corrupt subordinates and to create for them ideal conditions to enable them to batten on the Asiatic tax-payers of the Federated Malay States.

And I answer my second question, which is why I am so firmly opposed to income tax in the Federated Malay States, also by saying that in the Federated Malay States there is no necessity for income tax in the year 1941. At the risk of being found guilty of repetition and re-iteration, I put before this Council these considerations and figures:

Rubber.—In August 1939, just before the war, the average price of rubber in Singapore was about 28 cents per lb. and the export release was 57½ per cent. To-day the price is around 36 cents per lb. and the export release is 100 per cent. Everyone knows that enormous profits have been and are to-day being made by the sale of Federated Malay States rubber and that a great deal of that profit is due to the war. The rubber of producers who do not pay income tax in the United Kingdom has been subject to a war tax of 2½ per cent. on the sale price of rubber, i.e., 1/40th, or less than one cent per lb. Could anyone object if that war tax is not doubled but trebled, especially when it is remembered that the war has conferred on producers of rubber two benefits? It has not only increased the price of the commodity but it has almost doubled the output. The war tax on rubber at 2½ per cent. on the price of the commodity is estimated for the year 1941 to produce $3,000,000. If the war tax, which is less than one cent a lb., is trebled, an extra $6,000,000 a year is produced. The figures above mentioned are without imposing further taxation on those producers who pay income tax in the United Kingdom.

Tin.—Tin was quoted in Singapore on August 1, 1939, around $113.28 per pikul and on August 31, 1939, around $116 per pikul. The domestic release was 45 per cent. To-day the price is around $133 per pikul and the release is 100 per cent. There has been a war tax of 2½ per cent. on the sale price of the commodity in respect of the tin of producers who do not pay income tax in the United Kingdom. We are all familiar with the enormous profits which have been and are being obtained by tin producers in the Federated Malay States and that profit again is largely due to the war. The increase in price is from, say, $116 a pikul to $133 a pikul, is $17 a pikul, 2½ per cent. on $133.75 = $3.32 per pikul. At present only $3.32 per pikul has been given up by reason of the war tax of 2½ per cent. Can valid objection be raised if the tax be trebled and not doubled and $9.96 per pikul out of $17 per pikul be given up, especially when it is remembered that the war has conferred on producers of tin also two benefits? It has not only increased the price of the commodity but it has more than doubled the output. The war tax on tin at 2½ per cent. on the price of the commodity is estimated for the year 1941 to produce $3,000,000. If the war tax, which is $3.32 per pikul out of an extra profit of $17 per pikul, is trebled, an extra $6,000,000 is produced. Again the figures above mentioned are without imposing further taxation on those producers who pay income tax in the United Kingdom.

===== Page 128 =====
There are, in my opinion, a number of import and other duties which are fairly capable of being increased by at least 20 per cent. I have submitted to Government a detailed list of those duties. For the year 1941, the duties included in this list are estimated to produce $28,000,000. If the duties are raised by 20 per cent, over $5,600,000 will be obtained. At present, a bottle of whisky costs about $4.15 and of this sum approximately $2 is for duty. Can anyone object to pay an extra 40 cents a bottle—making the total price about $4.55 a bottle, i.e., just under 10s. 8d.? 

In answer to the suggestion that increased import duties would increase the cost of living to the labourer and other persons who it may be alleged cannot afford to pay more for their living expenses, I say three things:

(a) The increased duties tax one or two items only which are purchased by the labouring classes.

(b) Prices are controlled in the Federated Malay States and by price control the incidence of the tax can be made to fall on the seller.

(c) Wages have increased enormously. Chinese coolies on rubber estates and mines are earning $1-$1.20 and more a day. I remember not so long ago when they were paid 50 or 80 cents a day. I am informed that 30-35 cents a day covers the necessities of life of a Chinese coolie.

There may be divergent views as regards certain of the new taxes which I have suggested to Government. A list of them appeared in an article I contributed to the Malay Mail and which was published on January 7 last. I stated when I sent to Government the list of the new taxes that I would support that the list was neither imperative nor exhaustive. This is neither the time nor the place to discuss in detail these suggested new taxes. Let it suffice that it is clear that there are sources of revenue still untouched and from which money can be obtained without a general income tax necessitating the setting up of an elaborate new tax gathering department with attendant opportunities for bribery and corruption.

In suggesting certain of the new taxes I was at pains to endeavour to effect two things:

(1) The investigation of vernacular books by employees of a new Government department would not be necessary.

(2) Government would be assisted by taxing salaries and profits of Federated Malay States Companies.

I would, however, stress that without any of the new taxes selected by me Government could obtain in the year 1941:

From further taxation on rubber ................................... $6,000,000
From further taxation on tin ............................................. 6,000,000
From increased import duties, etc. ..................................... 5,600,000

$17,600,000

This is a decided improvement on the $10,000,000 suggested by the Joint Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States Committee and practically no further expenditure would be occasioned by the collection of this additional $17,600,000.

Over and beyond the new taxes, in my opinion, money can be obtained by further Loan Issues, and I am indebted to the remarks made by the Financial Secretary who told us that the cost of collecting will be four per cent, whereas we can borrow money at three per cent. I say therefore that income tax, with the attendant increase of bribery and corruption, is not necessary in the Federated Malay States in order to enable these States to make in the year 1941 extremely handsome contributions to the Imperial Treasury far in excess of what will be contributed if we have income tax.

Thirdly, I answer my second question by pointing out that we have no experts in income tax legislation nor in the administration of such a tax. I believe that it is "intended" to obtain the services of trained collectors. These officers are, of course, essential at the outset. When this form of taxation was introduced into Ceylon the Bill was, I understand, scrutinized by experts from the British Income Tax Department and the Ceylon Income Tax Department had, and, I think, still has two Income Tax Experts from the Department in London, each having more than 12 years experience of income tax administration, to assist in the running of the Department and the training of the personnel of that Department.

===== Page 129 =====
There are a number of additional reasons why income tax should not be introduced into the Federated Malay States. Other speakers will doubtless refer to them. I have heard not from one but from a number of Chinese businessmen of a method whereby the tax collector, endeavoring to obtain a tax on the profits of a business run by a Chinese proprietor or by Chinese partners, would find at the end of a long and tedious examination of the books that an apparently prosperous business yielded no taxable profits. The method of evasion explained to me is exceedingly simple but it is legal and cannot, so far as I can see, be guarded against by legislation, nor can it be countered even by the most enthusiastic tax gatherer.

There are doubtless other devices equally legal and some no doubt of more doubtful character. But Sir Andrew Caldecott knew what he was talking about when he said in the year 1933 in this Council:

“If an income tax were imposed in these States it would be at least eighteen months to two years before machinery could be placed in working order and as accounts are kept in so many different languages I am afraid that machinery would prove so complex and the difficulties so insuperable that very little would result out of the tax.”

I yield to no one in my anxiety to be of such assistance as I may to my country in the hour of trial. I am more than anxious that these States which have been ridden by Government on the curb, so far as their financial contribution to the Empire’s War Effort is concerned, should be given their head and afforded a fair chance to render that assistance which they properly should do. I do not think this will be achieved by the introduction of the income tax of the Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States Joint Committee or any other income tax. I venture to suggest that Your Excellency and other Government spokesmen are asking us to worship a shibboleth. What I suggest is required is that we should realize actualities and not be led astray by plausible but, in the conditions of these States, financially unsound theories.

Now, Your Excellency, I had intended to end my remarks here but I am asked by my honourable friend Mr. Palmer to inform this Council that he is opposed to this Bill. He is not going to address this Council as unfortunately he is not in a fit condition of health to do so. He wishes me to tell this Council that he opposes this Bill.

There is one other matter, and I sincerely wish I had not got to speak on this—the matter of voting upon this Bill. I was told by Your Excellency yesterday afternoon, and I heard it with the deepest regret, that it is Your Excellency’s proposal to allow the official members of this House to vote on this measure affecting the fiscal policy of the country—there is going to be what is somewhat euphemistically called a free vote. The previous pledge given by Your Excellency was that the official vote would not be used. That was given in this House on April 1, 1940. The official vote is not being used, but the votes of the officials are going to be used. It is known by one and all how determined Your Excellency is to force this measure on the Federated Malay States and I ask myself, and the public will ask, is it likely that there will be many officials so courageous as to vote against it, or is this a free vote if the freedom of the official to vote against the measure is the freedom of a man looking down the wrong end of a six-shooter.

In a matter of this kind it is the unofficial who should have the right to decide. Your Excellency took that view last year when Your Excellency assured us that the decision was in the hands of the unofficials, and when Your Excellency wrote us a note, so that we may be aware of the position as Your Excellency saw it. Your Excellency said that the decision is in the hands of the unofficials. Either that was an honest assurance or it was not. I hope on reflection sounder counsels will still prevail and this matter will be left to be decided by the unofficial members. My hope is that Your Excellency will keep faith with us and with the public.

I have demonstrated that the position in its essentials is no different from what it was when Your Excellency gave that assurance. All the money that the Malayan Governments can contribute to the Imperial Treasury can be found without income tax. There is something more vital involved than the manner in which money is to be raised in these States, and that is the honour and good name of British administration. Let us never forget that in this war we are fighting for principles against the unprincipled. We are fighting for freedom against oppression.

Raja Kechil Tengah, Perak: Sir, I rise to support this Bill—because I am sure that it is only by means of taxation on the lines which this Bill implements that our maximum war effort can be achieved. The making of such an effort on behalf of the Empire is, I feel, the desire of every responsible and right-thinking person living in this Federation. Amongst my own people in my State no voice is raised against this measure.

===== Page 130 =====
My experience in the Treasury suggests that there are sources of potential revenue that can only be reached by means of this tax. If it is true that large sources can be raised by means other than income tax, then by all means let us adopt those other means in addition to but not instead of the present tax. (Honourable Members: Hear, hear.)

I give the Bill my wholehearted support—as I and my fellow countrymen do—for the great cause, for which it is to provide the necessary sinews of war. (Applause).

Mr. W. G. C. Blunn: Sir, the Honourable the Legal Adviser and my honourable friend Raja Uda have added a notable contribution to the many able and impassioned appeals which we have heard and read in favour of income tax for this country and if our heads were not made of sterner stuff than our hearts I am sure there would be unanimous support for the motion which is now before the Council.

This Bill, however, provides for a form of taxation which in my sincere opinion is entirely unsuited to conditions in the Federated Malay States.

Since February of last year, when I first gave expression in this Chamber to my opposition to a tax on incomes, the need for a sustained and intensified war effort on the part of the Colonial Empire and Protectorates has become even more apparent than it was then, but nothing which has since been said or has since occurred has shaken my conviction that the introduction of income tax in the Federated Malay States at this time would be a blunder of the first magnitude.

On the contrary, much has occurred in the interval to strengthen me in that conviction. I refer, of course, to the serious irregularities which have been brought to light in connection with the awarding of certain military contracts in the Colony and more recently by the Commission of Enquiry into the working of the Mines Department here; also to a case of corruption in the Estates Duty Office, Federated Malay States.

The opinion is very generally held that corruption is prevalent in many other Government departments and until the whole situation has been thoroughly investigated and cleaned up, it is extremely undesirable that this Government should set up a new department or, if the Honourable the Legal Adviser prefers it, a new organisation which would provide so many opportunities for corruption as would an Income Tax Department.

I would ask this Council seriously to consider the extortion and corruption which would most certainly accompany any attempt to investigate the incomes of thousands of small shopkeepers of various nationalities who pay rents of $25 a month and upwards throughout the country.

The cost of collection may not seem high if judged purely from a monetary standpoint but when measured in terms of public morality the price, in my opinion, is prohibitive.

It is unfortunate perhaps that advocates of income tax have been prone to impute disloyal and selfish motives to those who do not see eye to eye with them as to the means which should be employed to accomplish the purpose which we have commonly in view, but I feel bound to say that, in the opposite camp too, serious suspicions are entertained as to the sincerity of motive underlying the Government's apparent anxiety to impose this particular form of taxation in these States whilst more obvious and more generally acceptable means of raising revenue for war purposes in much shorter time have been consistently neglected despite the plea which has been made this morning for urgency.

It is widely felt that the patriotic appeal has been misused for political ends. The Financial Secretary, Straits Settlements, is reported to have said that income tax is needed in the Colony as a permanent measure to replace the diminishing revenue from opium and, it is asked, what better opportunity could there be than is provided by the war to foist income tax upon the Federated Malay States so as to protect the Colony against any difficulties or disadvantages which might conceivably arise out of unilateral action?

It must be remembered that the whole fiscal policy of this Federation and the conditions which govern that policy are entirely different from those existent in the Colony.

===== Page 131 =====
This is essentially a producing country wherein export and import duties yielding many millions of dollars annually are rightly accepted as standard practice. The Colony, on the other hand, depends largely upon its entrepôt trade and is necessarily concerned in maintaining the freedom of its ports.

In pursuance of a free trade policy, the Government of the Straits Settlements is denied recourse to many of the lucrative sources of revenue which are so readily available to the Government of the Federated Malay States. This point is clearly illustrated by a comparison between the respective budget figures of the two Administrations.

Addressing the Legislative Council of the Straits Settlements on October 14 last, His Excellency the Officer Administering the Government intimated that the Colony was expected to show a deficit for 1940 of nearly $18,000,000, whereas from the answers to the Hon'ble Mr. Shearn's questions today it would appear that, in the Federated Malay States, the latest figures available indicate a probable surplus of about $21½ million for the same period.

The difference of over $39 million in favour of these States is accounted for by Federal Revenue from Customs duties.

The Colony's estimate of revenue from all sources in 1941 is just over $47 million. In the Federal Budget for 1941, Customs duties alone are conservatively estimated to yield $42 million. This figure excludes revenue from Chandu and yet represents 48 per cent of the estimated total from all sources, Federal and State, of $87 million.

Now the Joint Committee's estimate of gross annual yield from a tax on incomes is $10 million in the Colony and a like sum in the Federated Malay States, the latter including the increased duties on rubber and tin. It is not difficult to understand why the Federation's system of taxation does not commend itself to the Colony but the plain fact remains that the Federated Malay States could easily exceed this figure, without incurring the attendant evils of a hastily improvised and admittedly imperfect income tax scheme; a scheme, moreover, which would involve the setting up of a large and complicated organisation for the administration, mostly by amateurs, of what we are asked to believe is to be a purely temporary measure.

Every additional 2½ per cent ad valorem duty on tin and rubber alone, if applied without exemptions, might be expected to yield $10,000,000 annually, as may be seen from the printed answers to my questions laid on the table today.

The present price of rubber is approximately eight cents per lb higher than it was a month or two before the war and there is nearly 70 per cent more rubber being released for export. Thus the producer who sold 100 lbs of rubber before the war for $28 can now sell 170 lbs for about $60. Allowing for all-in cost there is a net gain of, probably, $15, which every 2½ per cent ad valorem duty reduces by only $1.50.

The case with regard to tin is much the same as explained by my honourable friend Mr. Shearn.

A point which appears to have been overlooked and to which I would like to draw attention is that the war taxes on tin and rubber are levied on receipts and not directly on profits. They have their counterpart in the emergency regulations which control the selling prices of imported goods. The latter of course promote the war effort indirectly and benefit the local consumer but they impose a much more drastic restriction on profits than do the export taxes on rubber and tin.

The willingness with which the mining and planting industries have accepted the war taxes hitherto is, I am sure, very generally appreciated, but to say that these industries are being called upon to share a disproportionately heavy burden is to misunderstand the actual significance of these taxes in their relation to the general policy of price control, as distinct from any direct tax on profits.

I must challenge the Honourable the Legal Adviser's assertion that there have been no alternative suggestions put forward by members of this Council who are opposed in principle to income tax. In addition to the various suggestions which I put forward in this Chamber as long ago as August 20 and November 21 last, I submitted to Government on December 28 some further taxation proposals under several different heads giving my reasons in support of each suggestion. These included a tax on house property with protection to tenants against increases in rent and also a tax on the dividends of public companies registered in the Federated Malay States with which, of course, I would include interest on debentures, fixed deposits and Government and Municipal loans. Provision could be made for relief in cases of hardship.

===== Page 132 =====
Such taxes, it is true, might be regarded as income tax in an abbreviated form, but there is this distinction: they would apply only to interest on invested capital or what is commonly though perhaps rather loosely described as "unearned increment." The important point is that the collection of such taxes would not involve the creation of a separate department.

If considered necessary, provision could be made to prevent the passing of dividends by public companies for purposes of tax evasion. The objection that a portion of the taxes on dividends earned in these States would be collected in the Colony would not be a serious one so long as the proceeds were used exclusively for war purposes.

I need not detain honourable members by going into all the other suggestions which I have made for alternative taxation. I think I have said enough to show that the estimated yield from an income tax, so far as these States are concerned, could be appreciably exceeded by other forms of taxation which could be quite simply and equitably applied.

Finally, the very fact that the income tax question here has given rise to such bitter controversy should be a warning to Government to avoid its introduction at this time, when the closest unity amongst all the various races domiciled in these States is of far more importance than the raising of $4,000,000, which is the figure indicated this morning by the Honourable the Financial Secretary.

I wish to associate myself with the concluding remarks of my honourable friend, Mr. Shearn, and to express the hope that it may yet please Your Excellency to honour Your Excellency's pledge that this matter should be settled by the vote of the unofficial members. (Applause).

Dr. Khong Kam Tak: Sir, the Chinese community in the Federated Malay States, whilst willing, nay eager, to support any suitable form of taxation for the prosecution of the war, are nevertheless opposed to the imposition of income tax. Their views and objections have been conveyed to Government by letter and by petition, and I need not therefore take up the time of this Council by going over them again. As one of their representatives on this Council, I feel it my duty to oppose this Bill. (Applause).

Dr. S. R. Krishnan: Your Excellency, apart from any view which I may personally hold on this subject, I would be failing in my duty to the public as its representative if I do not bring to Your Excellency's notice the objections to the imposition of income tax in the Federated Malay States.

I have been approached by such influential public bodies as the Associated Chinese Chamber of Commerce, Malaya, the Chettiar's Chamber of Commerce, Federated Malay States, the Indian Merchants' Association, Selangor, and others to oppose this Bill, and I have no other alternative except to oppose on their behalf. I need not go very fully into the reasons for the objections. The full details are stated in the petitions to the High Commissioner and to the Secretary of State for the Colonies separately.

I can assure Your Excellency that our patriotism and loyalty to the local and Imperial Governments and our pledge to undergo any sacrifice towards the successful prosecution of the war remain as high and true as ever. (Applause.)

Mr. J. D. Mead: Your Excellency, I came here today with no set speech, but, after hearing the speeches of those who oppose this Bill, I have not been able to find myself in sympathy with them. It may be that in some callings and professions one is forced into contact with what one might call the lowest strata of humanity, who cannot judge between right and wrong, but I think that the estimate of their fellow-citizens which the opponents of the Bill have expressed today is a very poor one. The basis of their opposition to this tax, as I see it, is that it is bound to meet with evasion and corruption. Well, Sir, I believe, having regard to the spirit in which people are facing the issue of the war today, that that will not be so, especially if the leaders of communities carry out their responsibilities and see that their members understand the rights and wrongs of the case. In their endeavour to strengthen their case against the Bill, several references have been made by its opponents to the mining industry, the terms generally employed being "enormous profits made by mining companies." No reference has been made to the fact that during the last ten years there have been more lean years than fat ones. (Applause.)

Mr. Yong Shook Lin: I join in the pleas of praise bestowed by previous speakers upon my honourable and learned friend, the Legal Adviser, for his oration in introducing the Income Tax Bill. So great an oration was worthier of a bigger occasion and a better

===== Page 133 =====
Bill. The Legal Adviser's speech does great credit to his heart; but even if great thoughts come from the heart, it is better that they should emerge from the head. The speech was exceedingly clever and should serve, for many years to come, as a classic example of the truth that "policy sits above conscience".

The Bill before the Council is as inexpert and clumsy a piece of work as could have been contrived. It is full of injustices both patent and latent, and would be ineffectual in achieving its objects. It is little more than "a thing of shreds and patches"—a mass of disjecta membra. If it were to be enacted and enforced, it would have to be altered, remoulded, patched, painted and varnished so that, at last, no trace would be left of the scattered remains whose funeral rites we today attend.

For the third time since the beginning of last year, I express my firm opposition to income tax as a measure of taxation for the Federated Malay States. My previous speeches were made when there was no Income Tax Bill before this Council and when the subject of income tax was discussed at great length in private conversations, in meetings of associations and public bodies, in the columns of the Press of the Malay Peninsula, and was debated in a tense atmosphere. Arguments for and against income taxation have been advanced since the beginning of last year in great numbers and with great vigour and emphasis. After having read or heard those arguments and weighed them in an atmosphere of calm, the impression left in my mind and in the minds of many others was that that was the last the public, at least in the Federated Malay States, would hear of income tax even as a measure of war taxation. That impression was justified, in that I felt that the arguments advanced by the opponents of income tax were so thorough and sound that they were unanswerable. I felt at last that the Government, faced with the opposition of those of the public who would have to pay income tax if imposed, would have obtained an impression similar to that of mine, and would have dropped the very idea of the imposition of income tax even as a measure of war taxation and would have looked round for other sources of taxation. It appears that, to get in income tax at whatever cost and expense and despite public opposition, is the one aim and the only object of Government. In refusing to accept income tax and in opposing the Bill now before this Council, I do so on behalf of the Chinese community of the Federated Malay States and, particularly, of those classes of the Chinese community which would be affected by the Bill, if it were to become law. The refusal to accept income tax as a measure of taxation is embodied in every resolution passed at the beginning of last year and towards the end of last year, by meetings, representatives of Chinese Chambers of Commerce, associations, guilds, societies and Chinese business houses in every State of the Federated Malay States, whose members would be affected by the Bill if it were passed.

Sir, in your broadcast address of March 3rd last, you were good enough to give those who would be affected by the imposition of income tax, the opportunity of expressing their view on the subject of income tax, and I am sure that the public owes Your Excellency a debt of gratitude for that opportunity. In refusing to accept income tax as a measure of taxation and in opposing the Bill, I say, on behalf of the Chinese community, that the Chinese community is not the less patriotic or the less loyal to the Federal Government or to any of the State Governments or to the Imperial Government in spite of the reference to Communism by my honourable and learned friend the Legal Adviser in his introductory speech this morning. On numerous occasions, they have affirmed and proved their loyalty and their desire to support any fair and reasonable taxation for the prosecution of the war. They appreciate deeply the protection and security afforded to them, and are conscious of their duty in connection with the present war. They realise that the utmost should be done to assist in the successful prosecution of the war and they will not hesitate to give their support to any measures for imposing just and reasonable taxation; but the system under which they carry on business, with accounts kept according to Chinese principles of business, renders income tax a very unfair and objectionable measure of taxation. That is one of the principal objections to income tax even as a measure of war taxation. They say, in effect, that they are willing to pay their share of any just and reasonable taxes but that, if income tax be imposed as a measure of taxation, they would encounter great difficulties in making their accounts to conform to English principles of accounting, in filling in returns, in answering queries, in submitting to inquisition and in getting equitable assessments.

I have not the slightest doubt that inquisition, objectionable to the public, would be resorted to. Inquisition is inseparable from the fair collection of income tax. If inquisition could be resorted to in the case of banks, which are public limited liability companies, in the Colony of Hong Kong, it would be resorted to here in the case of companies, associations and persons. I would read an extract from a report of [POSSIBLY MISSING].

===== Page 134 =====
September 29th, 1940, which has been sent to me from Hongkong: "Since sending in our form on July 9th, nothing has been heard from the War Tax Commissioner. The Banks are receiving from him long queries—in one instance, several foolscap sheets." The recommendation of the Joint Committee on income tax in para. 17 of their report, for the computation by the Collector and two or more assessors of the income or total income of any Company, association or person where the necessary accounts have not been satisfactorily kept, is not new. The Joint Committee are under a delusion if they consider that that is a remedy. The Collector and his assessors assume the existence of a state of affairs and in so doing arbitrarily assume a state of affairs to the detriment of the tax-payer whose accounts have not been satisfactorily kept; or who keeps no accounts at all, issue an assessment for the maximum amount and leave the tax-payer to disprove the correctness of the assessment made by proving the negative. That, Sir, is the system which the Joint Committee recommends and that is the system in England where income tax has been in existence for about 140 years. That is the system which would enable the Collector and his assessors to extract every ounce of blood from the tax-payer, and that is the system which would give rise to injustice after injustice.

If I may be allowed to do so I will read a short extract from the book on Income Tax by the authority, Mr. Jarvis, who says: "But assuming that the assessor is satisfied with the first return of a trader's profits, he is always an optimist, and imagines that every business is not only a success, but a growing success, and the next year the trader will find that he is assessed at an increased estimate, and left to his remedy to get it reduced. The appeal from the assessor is to the surveyor of taxes, who will consider whether or not the difference between the parties can reasonably be adjusted. If he cannot settle it there and then, and calls for more strict proof of the case set up in his defence by the person recklessly assessed the appeal must be fought out between the party displeased with the assessment and the surveyor of taxes before the Commissioner of Income Tax." I ask that the difficulties of the Chinese Community which are real and not merely imaginary be appreciated.

With all the inconvenience and expense to the business man who might not be even liable to pay any income tax at all, ways and means would be devised by the unscrupulous to tempt officers of the department concerned to lessen their inconveniences, to obtain early exemption so that they could go on with their business or even to obtain a reduction of the tax which might be payable. I am told by an accountant that in a Colony where income tax was first introduced there was great competition for posts in that department and that whoever got a post in that department was sure of a good income.

The President: Which Colony?

Mr. Yong Shook Lin: Ceylon. In a former speech of mine in this Council opposing income tax I referred to the existence of bribery and corruption in these States. That was before the appointment of the Commission of Enquiry into the Mines Department. The convictions against certain officers of that department support in toto the allegation of the existence of bribery and corruption in these States. It is the argument of every opponent of income tax that with bribery and corruption rife in these States the Government is not entitled to set up a new department or the costly extension of an existing department. A question for answer is this. Is it proposed to scrap the Federated Malay States income tax department after the war is over? Perhaps Your Excellency would be able to answer this question.

Income tax as a source of revenue is more liable to evasion—both legitimate and fraudulent—than any other form of taxation. There is no tax in England which is more often evaded or tried to be evaded than income tax. Accounts here are kept in so many different languages that it would be well nigh impossible for the department to prevent evasions, and success which must attend evasions at the commencement would lead to more and more evasions. Facts must be regarded as facts, and it is, in my view, unwise to disregard events which are bound to happen. The assertion has been made on more occasions than one, that if A evades the payment of his tax, there is no reason why B should not do his duty by paying his tax. But human nature being what it is, evasions breed dissatisfaction and discontent amongst the honest who have to pay, and a tax can be regarded as equitable only if the persons who have to pay it regard it as equitable. If A can avoid a tax which he is liable to pay, how can B, who was to pay, regard the tax as equitable, however patriotic or loyal he may be? The recommendation of the Joint Committee in para. 23 of their report is that every consideration and assistance should be given to businesses which keep accounts in languages other than English. I say that this recommendation is idealistic in the extreme, suitable for a 17th century community but impracticable in 1941. How would it be possible for the Department to give assistance in methods of keeping accounts in languages other than English?

===== Page 135 =====
Income tax as a measure of taxation was regarded by the War Loan Committee as a very inequitable and uneconomical form of taxation. The majority report of the War Loan Committee of the Federated Malay States stated that it would not be satisfactory to see income tax introduced in the Straits Settlements and not in the rest of Malaya. If that be the object of bringing forward this Bill in this Council today, then I must say from the Federated Malay States point of view that such object is open to the gravest criticism and deserves to be denounced.

To impose income tax as a measure of taxation in the Federated Malay States just because the Straits Settlements must have it would, to borrow the words of the Officer Administering the Government when he replied to the proposal for the formation of a War Council for Malaya in the Legislative Council on October 14th last, "shatter the unity and good feeling which exist throughout Malaya and would cripple the war efforts this country is making." The greatest unity and cooperation is now required than at any other period in the history of the Federated Malay States. To force this Bill through would cause a division of the people into two semi-hostile camps. Feeling is running very high, and I ask that the cause be removed and that the Bill be withdrawn before it is too late to prevent occurrence of the evil of which I give solemn warning.

For a period of thirteen months, the atmosphere of the Federated Malay States has been overcast by this cloud of income tax. Income tax as a means of enabling a contribution to be made to the Imperial Government was the original intention. The majority report of the Federated Malay States War Loan Committee made it a means of enabling the Federated Malay States to follow the Straits Settlements. A Joint Committee now recommends that income tax should be applied for defraying the expenses incurred in connection with the prosecution of the present war. In other words, the question of direct contribution to the Imperial Government for the prosecution of the war on the grounds of patriotism and loyalty has narrowed itself down to one of obtaining additional revenue to defray the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the war.

Sir, I now turn to constructive proposals. Firstly, the resolutions passed this morning for the increase in the ad valorem export duties on rubber and tin by 2½ per cent have carried out two of the main recommendations of the minority report of the Federated Malay States War Loan Committee. This increase of 2½ per cent would yield, as my honourable friend who preceded me said, about $6 million per annum. It will be possible to obtain further revenue from these two sources by a further increase in the export duties.

Secondly, I suggest an increase in the import duty on intoxicating liquors, tobacco, and luxuries to yield a further $2 million. Even if the law of diminishing returns were to come into operation, a little less of liquor and tobacco would not do the public any harm.

Thirdly, I suggest an increase in the import duty on petroleum by 10 cents which would yield on the figures for 1999, about $1 million. My honourable and learned friend the Legal Adviser said that such a tax would affect the transport of the country and the poor adversely. The answer to that is this question: Has the increase in the price of 10 cents per gallon immediately after the war affected the transport of the country and the poor adversely?

Fourthly, I suggest a further increase of 50 per cent on the tax payable on all private motor cars which would yield about $4 million.

Fifthly, I suggest a further increase of 10 per cent on all sweepstakes which would yield about $150,000.

Sixthly, I suggest a new tax of, say, two months' rent of all houses within Sanitary Board areas payable as to one month's rent by proprietors and as to the other, one month's rent by tenants, lessees, or occupiers. In the case of vacant lands, proprietors are to pay 1/12th of the annual values thereof. The existing machinery of collection with perhaps a slight increase in temporary clerical staff will suffice for collection. This will yield a substantial sum.

I am absolutely convinced that income tax would be not only an unsuitable form of taxation for this country but a bad thing for the country. Bad, that is to say, not merely for the people who at the moment would have to pay the tax but for all the people, including the Malay people.

If the Government and their advisers are still of the opinion that income tax is an appropriate and proper form of taxation for imposition in the Federated Malay States, I say to the Government and their advisers, in the words which Oliver Cromwell spoke to the Parliamentarians—"I beseech you to think it is possible you may be mistaken."

I was profoundly shocked when it was announced by my learned and honourable friend Mr. Shearm, that Your Excellency would use the votes of the officials to pass this Bill. That intention is in breach of the spirit of the official public declaration made by Your Excellency in this Council and especially in breach of the written confidential communication previously made by Your Excellency to the unofficial members of this Council. No language that I can command is adequate to express the horror which I am sure the public of the Federated Malay States will feel when it becomes known that Your Excellency's promise has been broken, and I say that that broken promise will go down as a stigma in the annals of

===== Page 136 =====
British Colonial administration. With all the earnestness I possess, I humbly implore Your Excellency not to use the votes of the Officials. (Applause.)

The President: Let us adjourn for lunch as there are many more speeches and continue at 2.30 p.m.

Council adjourns.

Council resumes at 2.30 p.m.

Colonel C. Rae: Sir, I had not intended to enter into the debate on this Bill and I have not come prepared with a speech and I thought that any intervention on my part would be still more unnecessary after hearing the very eloquent and elaborate explanation of the object and purposes of the Bill propounded by the Honourable the Legal Adviser; but there have been numerous speeches against this Bill during the morning session and those speeches have inspired me to say at least a few words on the matter before this House.

The speeches of some of my colleagues reminded me somewhat of the beating of wings against the air and these wings have been beating the same tune for the last 12 or 15 months. In some cases the wings were as of the wings of a bat rather than of the wings of more desirable birds.

In fact I deeply resent as a member of the Commissions to which Mr. Shearn saw fit to refer, the implications and remarks he thought fit to make. That tone in debates in this House has been quite foreign to me during the 11 years in which I have been a member of this assembly.

The whole of the arguments brought forward by the opponents of this Bill seem to me to fall down at once if we put just this one proposition. They always start off by saying "What should our contribution to the war effort be?" I must admit that in the early days of the war, as you know very well, Sir, I put it up myself that Government should say what amount this country should find and we unofficials would tell you how to find it. I have entirely changed that point of view.

The answer to my mind, after having served on these various Commissions and spent a considerable amount of time on the subject of taxation for war purposes and more particularly in regard to income tax, is that the contribution of the Federated Malay States to the war effort should be our last dollar short of insolvency. (Applause) and if we take that attitude, as I do, then any form of taxation that is legitimate should be put into force in order to raise the maximum amount of money possible for the purpose of prosecuting this war effort, and I have no doubt that in spite of what members have said here this morning that that is the feeling of the great majority of the thinking people of this Federation. (Applause). I know that it is so in my own State of Perak. After all Perak produces almost as much wealth as all the other States put together and the opinion there is quite as good as, if not very much better than, that of the small coterie who take it upon themselves to say how much or how little or how we should produce the wealth that will go to keep us all free men. (Applause.)

I will admit that this Bill before the House has its faults. The Commission that sat on it had no illusion about that. I will admit, and I am sure other members of the Commission will admit, that there are dishonest scoundrels and patriotic scoundrels (Laughter) who will escape paying the money due under this Bill, but we cannot help that. There are a great many honest people in this country and I beg leave to differ from the general tone of my honourable colleagues this morning. I think the majority of us are really quite honest and are only too willing to make our proper and adequate contribution under this Enactment.

The whole object has been to keep the Bill as simple as possible. Of course it is in no sense—no doubt the legal members will admit—an Income Tax bill. But it is a bill which is going to quite a lot of money, after all $4,000,000 is not an inconsiderable sum, and, in my opinion, we will raise considerably more than that. I should not be surprised if, when this bill is working properly, we should get at least double that amount. The whole object was to keep it as simple as possible and to introduce as few complications as consistent with reasonable efficiency. The minute you start to introduce any refinements—I was quite shocked when I began to investigate the subject—you find you don't add half a dozen or 20 clerks but that an army of clerks will be required to administer the scheme.

I am quite satisfied in my own mind, after having investigated the matter as fully as possible as a member of the Commission, that the Financial Secretary's figure for collecting the tax is well within the mark.

===== Page 137 =====
Mr. Shearn, a gentleman usually very accurate in his financial remarks, I thought made a slight mistake this morning when he said that at 4 per cent., the cost of collecting, it would be cheaper to float a loan at 3 per cent. I cannot quite follow that. After all this tax is only for the duration of the war and for the purpose of prosecuting the war, and any little faults it may have, as has been said by other speakers, can be easily adjusted either in the course of this year or when the bill is enacted in 1942.

I do not think I need weary the House any further except to say that originally I was bitterly opposed to income tax as Your Excellency knows and I have been converted to the idea of imposing income tax as the result of investigation and after satisfying myself of the facts of the case.

I have not accepted any slogan but have investigated the whole subject myself and I am quite satisfied that an income tax on the lines which your committee recommended can be collected in this country, can be collected at a cheap rate and can be made to yield quite a considerable sum towards the satisfaction of what should be our slogan "our last dollar short of insolvency." (Applause.)

The Secretary for Chinese Affairs: Sir, I do not desire to debate the main principles of the Bill that is before the House but there is one point that was made in his speech by my honourable friend Mr. Shearn this morning that I feel should not be left unanswered from the official side.

Mr. Shearn was at great pains to try and say that the votes of the officials were the same thing as the use of the official vote in this Council. He went further than that and suggested that the officials would, if Your Excellency took a certain view, sympathise fully with that view.

Now, Sir, we officials are members of this Council on the same affirmation as the unofficial members of this Council and we do our best to give public subjects the same unbiased consideration as our unofficial colleagues do. And I would remind Mr. Shearn and the two unofficials who supported him on this point that twice in the Legislative Council of the Colony, during the term of office of Your Excellency's predecessor, Sir Cecil Clementi, the Government was defeated by the officials' vote.

One of those was an occasion in one sense closely akin to this, because the subject of debate was His Excellency the Governor's solution of the amount of military contribution payable to the Home Government. And I will take it a step further and remind honourable members that on one occasion in this very House last year, when the War Loan Bill was being debated, the question of whether the interest on that loan should be tax free or otherwise was left to the free vote of the House and four or five officials now sitting at this table voted against the High Commissioner.

I submit, Sir, therefore, that there is no justification whatever for the accusation that has been made this morning that the vote of the officials is the same thing as the official vote.

If I may be allowed to say one other thing, Sir, it is that the logical conclusion to many of the points made this morning by the unofficials who opposed the Bill seems to me to be that what is wanted is an excess profits duty in addition to income tax. (Applause.)

The Legal Adviser: Sir, when I was faced with the black-coated jury of which I spoke this morning, I had the powers of a Judge and that included the power to discharge them without their giving a verdict and to summon a different jury, to give an unprejudiced verdict. As I have no such power today, and as I do not myself believe in the somewhat left-handed compliments as to my powers of cratery which were handed out to me this morning, I will not waste much of your time or my own in any final effort to persuade my honourable friends who opposed this Bill. Nor will I take time to do more than point out in passing that though 4 per cent. is more than 3 per cent., you have to pay back a loan but you don't have to pay back income tax. Nor will I upbraid my learned and honourable friend for the fact that in trying to make our flesh creep with scandals ancient and modern he should have seen fit to drag once again into the fierce glare of publicity the misfortunes of Midshipman Easy's nurse.

I will, however, stress one point in order to clear away a misapprehension which I had hoped would not have existed in view of my opening speech, and that is that the Joint Committee had no fixed written terms of reference but was authoritatively informed that it could, as it did, examine all sources of taxation from which a war contribution could be made.

===== Page 138 =====
One other thing—I should like to deal with my honourable and learned friend's speech in the form of a question: I should like to know, not necessarily now but sometime soon, what is this wonderful Chinese method of evading income tax of which several of his patriotic clients have informed him? He has told us that it is a perfectly legal method and therefore there cannot be any question of professional confidence being broken if he does tell me. (Laughter.)

In spite of the criticism that has been levelled at the drafting of the Bill (of which I am after all only the adoptive father) I should like to make an effort, by legislation, to prevent that evasion of the duty even though my learned friend thinks that it is impossible to do so. I have no doubt that he will very kindly inform me of this method, if not here, then in the privacy of my chambers.

One other point I wish to make without commenting on it in any way but merely as to a point of procedure. Honourable members who have spoken on the question of what members of this Council should, or should not, vote have, I think, overlooked a Standing Order of this Council. I refer to Standing Order 20 which reads:

"A division shall ordinarily be taken by a show of hands. Any member, however, may require that a division be taken by vote; in which case every member present (except the President who may at his option abstain from voting)—it is only the President who may abstain from voting—"shall in turn give his vote, the Clerk of Council recording each vote..."

So far as I need deal with the remarks made by my other honourable and learned friend, there are only two points which I wish to clear up. He has asked—I do not know whether the question was genuine or rhetorical—"Would this Government department in fact be disbanded?" I am authorised to say: "Certainly it would be, and will be, disbanded as soon as income tax comes to an end." As I said this morning, there will be no difficulty whatever in absorbing those members of the staff who are not being engaged on a purely temporary basis.

Then I think it was the same speaker who mentioned that when a ten cent increase on the duty on petrol had been levied it did not interfere with transport. That is so, but that is because the question was fully examined and fully calculated out and it was found that just that much increase need not and would not give rise to any increase in bus fares or other public transportation costs.

For the rest, I have nothing, I think, to reply to except certain personal criticisms which leave my head—soft though it is said to be—"bloody but unbowed".

There is, however, just one more point to which I must make reference: that is the comment made by my learned and honourable friend Mr. Shook Lin when he said that my speech showed the truth of the maxim that policy overrides conscience. To that I must take some exception. I said this morning—I say it again, and I expect to be believed when I say it (The President: Hear, hear.) that I am personally convinced that, unless we have income tax in the Federated Malay States, many persons who ought to contribute will not contribute; and that, by itself, to my mind is sufficient justification for passing this Bill. (Applause.)

The President: It is a thousand pities that on this matter of direct taxation there should be a difference of opinion, especially just at this time, when the people of Great Britain are standing shoulder to shoulder in readiness for the assault which is going to determine the fate of the world, and when active war is more close to the shores of Malaya than ever before. It is just on 17 months since we went to war with Germany, and I imagine it is clear enough to everyone that without compulsion, in the shape of direct taxation, we shall never get that maximum contribution for defence which is required from the people of the Federated Malay States. The reasons why direct taxation should be introduced are overwhelming: I am going to consider for a few moments what there is to be said for the other side. I shall try to be very moderate in what I say: these grave days are a time for co-operation and friendship, not for strife and rancour.

By way of introduction, I would remind you that this taxation has been recommended by an experienced and impartial Committee, of whom seven were unofficials and four were officials, representative of the Federated Malay States and of the Straits Settlements. All these men have lived in this country for many years, and have won for themselves positions of eminence and respect, in commerce and industry and in the practice of the law. I am entitled to maintain that these men know just as much of conditions in this country as do those who think differently from them: I maintain that in making their recommendations these men took into full account any difficulties that might be expected to arise in the collection of direct taxation from the various communities resident in the Federation. Yet, they have come to the

===== Page 139 =====
Deliberate conclusion that direct taxation is both practicable and justifiable and fair: not only so, but they prepared a bill to give effect to it, in substance this bill which is before us today. In essentials this bill follows the lines of the Colony War Tax Ordinance of 1919, which worked successfully. With far less information than we have today in the Federated Malay States, between 1917 and 1922 the Government of the Colony was able to collect some $27¼ million in direct taxation at the trifling cost of 1.57 percent of the gross proceeds, and only two cases arising out of the legislation were taken to the Supreme Court.

No legislation is perfect, and no one claims that this bill is perfect; but at least it is based on a law which enabled direct taxation to be collected smoothly and cheaply, and some of those who have advised this present bill had actual experience themselves of the working of the Ordinance on which it is based. So then, we have a body of men who know what they are talking about, recommending direct taxation and advocating this Bill.

As against these facts, the opponents of the bill rely for the most part on vague apprehensions, nebulous surmises. It is feared, for instance, that the introduction of direct taxation will be an incentive to bribery and corruption. This might equally be said of indirect taxation such as a Customs tariff which might be described as an invitation to smuggling; yet nobody objects to it. Let us think for a moment of the people who are going to be affected. First of all the Malays, but very few of them will be liable to pay the tax. That is the great merit of direct taxation; it requires of the rich man according to his means but it lets the poor man go free. No other sort of taxation in the world will ensure this. Then, the Indian communities, of whom again only a small proportion will pay. But in India income tax has been smoothly collected for many years. Then, there are the British. I hope it is not suggested that any of them will try and wiggle out of their obligations. Nor, I feel sure, will other Europeans resident in these States. Most of them have been accustomed to direct taxation from the day they were born, and most of them recognise its fairness. I must not forget the Chinese. It may be news to some of you that income tax is in force also in China. It was introduced only in 1936, but already its receipts take the third place in the National Treasury's income and are now one of the main sources of revenue, higher than the consolidated, the tobacco and the stamp duties, and second only to the Customs and salt revenues. The "China Fortnightly" from which I take this information, describes the increase in income tax revenue in this short period of time as impressive.

So then, in all the countries around us, in India, Burma, Ceylon, Siam, the Netherlands East Indies, Hongkong and China, direct taxation is well known. In Ceylon the receipts are some fifteen million rupees and the cost of collection is 2¼ percent of the net revenue. In the Netherlands East Indies the receipts are some forty million guilders. In many other countries of the Empire, where Indians and Chinese are among the residents, such as the Dominions of Canada and Australia, the Colonies of Kenya, Trinidad and other British West Indian Islands, direct taxation has been successfully administered for many years. I had personal experience of it in Nyasaland, when I was Governor there, where there was a considerable Indian community. It caused no hardship there, nor did it breed corruption. Unless, therefore, we are to suppose that the Governments of all these countries of mixed communities have ignored the real interests of the people in their search for revenue—a supposition which I assume most of us would flatly reject—we must conclude that the people of Malaya are so much more clever in trickery and corruption that we are unable to do here what is being done perfectly well by our neighbours next door. I do not imagine many people will accept that proposition: I certainly do not. There is no reason whatever why the introduction of direct taxation into the Federation should create difficulties and evils which have not been successfully faced in the Far East and elsewhere and cannot be successfully faced here. I have been a pupil in the school of Colonial administration now for more than thirty years and I say that, in my opinion, the standard of administration in Malaya generally is high. I have no fears that we shall fail.

There is another point. Responsible people in this country, whether officials or unofficials, have always been accustomed to take a real interest in the welfare of the people. It was only the other day that in the Legislative Council of the Colony a resolution for the introduction of lotteries was rejected by every official member and by a clear majority of unofficial members on the ground that lotteries are harmful. In a few days that same Council will vote on the principle of direct taxation: it will be interesting to see the results because, if the bill goes through, it will be a clear indication that the Legislative Council considers direct taxation to be not harmful.

I know of course that all the old arguments will be trotted out, how that I have been ordered by the Secretary of State to pass this legislation, which I have denied

===== Page 140 =====
Publicly more than once, I now deny once again that all those who vote in favour are yes-men, while those who vote against are bold and public-spirited independents, and that I am a dictator and can do as I like. All utter rubbish, of course, and everybody knows it.

Then again, it is claimed that the money can be obtained by other means. Now, there are only two forms of taxation: direct and indirect. If you refuse to have direct taxation, then I must ask you how indirect taxation will touch the class of person, salaried or otherwise, who has a comfortable income and simply will not contribute toward his own protection. The limited subscriptions to the War Fund show that such a class of person exists. If this class of person is not touched, and touched with some success, how can any indirect taxation yield our maximum contribution to the war chest? These words which I have just quoted are not mine; they are part of a letter published in the "Malay Mail" in October last.

Those people who say that direct taxation is unnecessary are either ignorant of the real nature of war or have deliberately shut their eyes to it. How can anyone say that we ought to give so much and no more, at a time when the British Government is asking the United States to lend material because it will soon be unable to pay cash? What would the honourable the second Chinese member, who opposes this bill, say if I were to turn round on him and suggest that the Chinese in Malaya have contributed sufficiently to the China War Relief Fund and that future remittances must be reduced? This Government of the Federation, indeed all the Malayan Governments, have given their full sympathy to the desire of the Chinese to help their country: without that sympathy the Fund could never have flourished as it has. And now, when my people are in desperate straits, the honourable member refuses to accord to them that sympathy and assistance which we have so freely given to his. It seems a very one-sided arrangement to me.

And while I am on this same topic, I ask how can anyone possibly estimate the financial liabilities which the war will impose on the Federation or indeed on Malaya as a whole. Quite apart from expenditure on purely local defence, quite apart from our future commitments in regard to food reserves and other things, let me remind you of the awful havoc the invader has created in so many countries in Europe. Roads, railways, bridges, harbours, schools, and hospitals—indeed whole cities—laid in ruins. They will have to be rebuilt. Thousands of refugees fleeing before the invader have to be housed and fed. Thousands of people bombed out of their homes, without a single possession in the world and nowhere to go; they too have to be housed, fed, and clothed. It is useless to say that these things cannot happen here: they can. The people of Norway, Denmark, Holland, and Belgium said that, yet these things did happen to them, and they may happen to you. I am not exaggerating. I am not being an alarmist. I am telling you only the truth which I presume you are no more afraid to hear than our people at home. I tell you plainly that this country may yet have to face a state of war of which the cost is utterly incalculable. It would be mad self-complacency, wishful thinking of the most reckless kind, not to recognize this and to make full provision for it, financial and otherwise, within the time available.

A further argument brought against direct taxation is that it is being attempted here merely to facilitate the imposition of this tax in the Straits Settlements. That is quite untrue. This bill is introduced here because it is right and just that the people of the Federated Malay States should make their maximum contribution towards the prosecution of the war.

It is alleged, too, that the collection will entail what is called an inquisition, but which in fact would be no more than an examination of the accounts of persons who are believed to be attempting to defraud the revenue. As long ago as last March I gave a public assurance that there shall be no sort of inquisition, and that no taxpayer shall be treated differently from another by reason of his race. I repeat that assurance.

There is also the old story that once direct taxation is introduced here it will stay forever. There is not the slightest foundation for such an allegation, and I think it is a pity that anyone should lend himself to it. It can only be for the purpose of forging opposition, and I am bound to say that I regard such tactics as not very creditable. Income Tax was introduced in the Colony in 1917 and was abolished at the end of 1922 in response to public demand. One of those who led this demand was Mr. Roland Braddell, one of the most doughty exponents of constitutional procedure whom this country has ever possessed. He would be the very last to surrender or neglect any of the rights and liberties possessed by the people. Yet, Mr. Braddell himself was a member of the Committee whose deliberations brought into existence this bill which we are now discussing. What then are the safeguards which he with

===== Page 141 =====
The rest of the Committee imposed and which this Government has accepted? They are, first, that this legislation will lapse at the end of this year unless it is replaced by another Enactment re-imposing it; and secondly, that the net proceeds of the tax shall be applied to defraying the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the present war and for no other purpose. The Committee then says: "Having regard to these provisions we consider it cannot honestly be maintained that the introduction of this war tax is a preclude to incorporating income tax as a permanent part of the fiscal system." Neither Mr. Braddell nor any member of the Committee would have signed a report containing these words if they had not been sure, and I think that other people might well ascribe to the Government this piece of elementary good faith. Incidentally, those who oppose direct taxation because of their anxiety about the future are apparently quite confident that this war is going to end in a British victory because, if it does not, no one will have any money to be anxious about. I submit that the very least they can do is to help to contribute to win that victory. Surely, in the circumstances of to-day, fears for the future cannot be allowed completely to override the urgent needs of the present.

I have already said that no one claims that this bill is perfect and no doubt there are anomalies. But not nearly so serious as has been suggested. The very most that a man can pay under this bill is 8 per cent. of his income or 1s. 7d. in the  , which is just under 70 cents in every $8.50. And if he earns no more than $4,800 a year he pays nothing at all. At home, a man with a wife and child and an earned income of that amount, i.e., after allowing him every advantage, has to pay  . 16s. 6d. ($547) in tax, which is 11  per cent. over all his total income. I fear that most of those who are so vociferous in regard to alleged inequalities under this bill have not really thought the matter out, or they have lost their sense of proportion and values.

To sum up, while I regret most keenly that public opinion should be divided on this question at such a time, the Government of the Federated Malay States has decided to introduce this bill because it is convinced that money necessary for the prosecution of the war can be obtained by direct taxation and by no other method. It is convinced, too, that there is very considerable support for direct taxation, especially from the European community. It is also convinced that it can administer this bill as efficiently as it was administered by the Colony over twenty years ago, and at least as efficiently as similar legislation is administered in the countries adjacent to us. Holding these views, it is no more than our bounded duty to all those who are fighting for us and keeping us safe that this legislation should be introduced.

I am sure that no one who has misgivings in regard to the effects of the impact of direct taxation on the people of the Federated Malay States will contend that there is any other system of taxation whereby he personally will have to pay as much as he will have to pay under a system of direct taxation. I am equally sure that everyone desires that this Federation should yield its maximum contribution to the prosecution of the war. I do most earnestly ask, therefore, that the principle of direct taxation, without which our maximum contribution is impossible shall be accepted by the adoption of this bill. I would then gladly agree to the appointment of a Committee, during this current year, to examine the anomalies and inconsistencies which in the light of experience may be shown to exist, and generally to make the legislation more scientific and up-to-date. And in the meantime, if this bill is passed, I do ask that everyone shall help to make it a success, shall assist  -not hinder  -those who are required to administer it, remembering always that it is not for ourselves that the Federation is working but for the great cause of the freedom of the world. If we keep that steadily in mind, then I submit that objections, some of which are based only on apprehension and not on fact, will be placed in their proper perspective and accepted as of minor importance.

Yesterday afternoon, at the close of a conversation with the Hon'ble Mr. Shearn and the Hon'ble Mr. Blunn, I explained to them the circumstances in which the Secretary of State has withdrawn the instruction of his predecessor to me that officials should not vote on this measure. I gave them this explanation fully, and without being asked for it. I considered it only fair to them that I should put all my cards on the table. I am exceedingly surprised and sorry that this morning they should have used only part of what I said, so that it might appear  indeed it would appear unless contradicted  that I have been guilty of a breach of faith. I deny utterly anything of the kind. I have been here now for six years, and I am proud to think that whatever my mistakes and faults I am not generally regarded as one who is ready to break his word. I will now give to honourable members the explanation which I gave to Messrs. Shearn and Blunn. Honourable members will have noted the preamble to this bill, that "it is desirable to impose additional taxation for the purpose of contributing towards the expenses incurred in the prosecution

===== Page 142 =====
of the present war," and will realise that it follows the wording of paragraph 9 (b) of the Joint Committee's Report. They have also heard from the Legal Adviser that His Majesty's Government considers that the first call on Malaya's war taxation should be for the purpose of Malayan defence and that it attaches the utmost importance to maintaining these defences at the highest stage of efficiency. It is clear, therefore, that the Federal Government is relieved of any obligation to remit to the Imperial Government any portion of the proceeds derived from a system of direct taxation: we are indeed exhorted to use them all, if need be, for local defence.

The instruction given to me by Mr. Malcolm MacDonald a year ago was that he "could not regard it as appropriate to make use of the official majority to impose taxation having as its object contributions to His Majesty's Government over and above the revenue needs of Malaya." In the altered circumstances which I have just explained, I felt entitled to ask the Secretary of State whether this instruction still stands. He has replied that it does not and that he has no objection to official members voting as they wish.

I may as well place on record, though it should not be necessary, that no sort of pressure has been placed by me or, to my knowledge, by any other person on the official members of this Council to vote in favour of this bill. Indeed, I think I am right in saying that I have discussed it only with the Federal Secretary and the Legal Adviser, and I have no idea how the other official members will vote. If it is said that, just because I myself have spoken in favour of the bill, the official members will feel bound to vote in support, then I reply that to be of any real value to me or to this country a Government official must have independence of thought and the courage of his convictions, and from my six years' association with them I know that the official members of this Council are, to say the least, not lacking in these qualities. It must be remembered, too, that financially they will be much more affected by the result of this debate than the average taxpayer.

There will be a free vote on this motion.

The President puts the motion.
