===== Page 178 =====
THE WAR TAXATION ENACTMENT, 1941.

This Enactment was drafted in accordance with the recommendations of a Joint Committee appointed by the Government of the Federated Malay States and that of the Straits Settlements. It contains the provisions which that Committee recommended should constitute the law if the Legislature decided to introduce Income Tax in the Federated Malay States as a war measure.

The Enactment is based largely upon the War Tax Ordinance 1919 of the Straits Settlements and imposes taxation upon the incomes of companies, associations and persons at the rates set out in the schedule. It is so drafted that it will apply and be in force in the year 1941 only, and the Preamble together with section 64 make it clear that the proceeds of such taxation can be applied only in defraying expenditure incurred for the prosecution of the war.

The Enactment taxes the income of companies, and also the income of partnerships and other "associations" as such; that is to say that a trading partnership pays income tax directly on its own profits as does a limited company. The shareholders of a company and the partners of a partnership are, however, exempted from paying taxation on that part of their income which is derived by them as such shareholders or partners.

Section 7 of the Enactment deals with classes of income which are exempted from taxation and these include income on which Income Tax is payable in any part of the British Empire and income derived from the cultivation of rubber or tin in the Federated Malay States if such rubber or tin is liable to the payment of the special Export Tax recently imposed as a war measure.

The Enactment had to be prepared rapidly and its provisions constituted an attempt to combine, so far as was possible in all the circumstances, simplicity with comparative equality of burden. It was realised that such an ideal could not be fully attained and that when the Enactment was actually put into operation there was every likelihood of practical difficulties and inconsistencies being encountered and discovered. In the short time during which the Enactment has been in force problems in connection with the construction and application of its provisions have already arisen but there ought to be no insuperable difficulty in solving these problems by means of suitable amendments to the Enactment itself or of rules made under the provisions of Section 63.

Legal Adviser
Federated Malay States.

Kuala Lumpur,
17th March, 1941.

===== Page 184 =====
Report on an Ordinance for imposing additional taxation for war purposes.

[No. 3 of 1941].

This Ordinance carries into effect in the Colony the recommendations of the report of the Joint Committee on Income Tax in the Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States.

The Ordinance is based upon the War Tax Ordinance, 1919 (No. 3 of 1919) and provides for the imposition in the year 1941 of a war tax on profits and incomes for one year only which will lapse unless continued by another Ordinance reimposing it.

It also provides that the net proceeds of the tax shall be applied to defraying the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the present war and for no other purposes.

The rates of tax are set out in the Schedule to the Ordinance.

The appropriate rate for each taxpayer is calculated upon the basis of the total income, but tax at that rate is levied upon the "income" only. Since hardship may arise in cases where a person's total income slightly exceeds one of the limits, provision is made that relief in such case may be prescribed by rule.

The principal deductions allowed from income are set out in section 7.

The Committee considered very carefully the question of marriage and family allowances and came reluctantly to the conclusion that it is impracticable to devise a satisfactory system for such allowances having regard to the existence of polygamous communities and the complexity and uncertainty of the marriage laws and customs of some such communities. In order to compensate for this the Ordinance provides that no person whose total income is less than $400 a month shall be liable to pay the tax and the initial rates of tax are kept low. Husband and wife are separately assessed.

Deductions which may not be made are set out in section 8.

Part IV contains the machinery for assessment and collection of the tax. Every individual in receipt of a total income of $4,800 or more is required to notify the Collector that he is chargeable with tax and, on receiving the proper forms, to make a full and accurate return of his total and taxable income. Companies and associations are also required to make returns of their income but it is provided that companies and associations whose whole income is liable to income tax elsewhere in the Empire or to the special War Export Duty on rubber and tin are exempted from making such returns. These exemptions will avoid a great deal of unproductive labour. The Collector may call for fuller returns and for evidence to support the returns.

As regards salaries and pensions and business begun on or after the 1st January, 1941, the assessment is made on the income for the current year, and in other cases, generally, on the income received by each company, association, or person during its or his last financial year.

===== Page 185 =====
Special provision is made for ascertaining the taxable income in respect of goods sold on consignment on behalf of a non-resident person: the Collector may, failing other satisfactory evidence, estimate the profits at not more than 5 per centum of the sale price.

Section 17 provides that where the Collector considers that either --

(a) the income or total income of any company, association or person cannot be readily ascertained, or

(b) that the necessary accounts have not been satisfactorily kept,

he and two or more assessors may compute the income and total income on the basis either of prescribed rates of profits or what they deem to be the usual rate of profit on the turnover of the trade or business carried on, or on such other basis as such Collector and Assessors consider reasonable. The Committee considered that this section would prove of great value in difficult and unusual cases and should go far to allay the frequently expressed fear that precise ascertainment of profits would not be possible with certain methods of bookkeeping.

The amount assessed as payable is notified to the taxpayer and must be paid within 21 days of the notice or before the 1st of September whichever is the later date. Provision is however made for collection of the tax at source so far as is practicable; thus an employer deducts tax from salaries as they are paid and accounts quarterly to the Collector; companies pay tax on their profits and recoup themselves from dividends; associations pay on their profits before distribution to the partners. Any necessary adjustments will be made between the Collector and the taxpayer.

Part V provides for an appeal against assessment to a Board of Commissioners comprising a High Court Judge and two or more lay Commissioners and a further appeal on a point of law to the Court of Appeal by way of case stated.

Attorney-General,
Straits Settlements.

Attorney-General's Chambers,
Singapore, 13 February, 1941.

21st January, 1941.

An Ordinance for imposing an additional duty on rubber for war purposes.

[21st January, 1941].

It is hereby enacted by the Governor of the Straits Settlements with the advice and consent of the Legislative Council thereof as follows:

1. This Ordinance may be cited as the War Tax (Additional Rubber Duty) Ordinance, 1941.

2.—(1) In addition to the duty imposed by section 8 of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, there shall be charged, levied, and paid on rubber produced in and to be exported from the Colony during the year 1941 a further duty of an amount equal to two and a half per centum of the value of such rubber.

(2) The provisions of section 3 so far as they are applicable and of sections 9 to 14 of the War Duties Ordinance, 1940, shall have effect mutatis mutandis in relation to the additional duty imposed by this section as if it were the duty imposed by section 8 of that Ordinance.

3. The net amount of the duty collected under this Ordinance after deduction of the expenses of and incidental to its collection, together with any fines and penalties imposed and paid in relation thereto, shall be

===== Page 188 =====
Applied to defraying the expenses incurred in the prosecution of the war and for no other purpose.

4.—(1) The Proper Officer may, on production to him of proof to his satisfaction that any rubber liable to the duty imposed by section 2 was purchased by and delivered to any dealer or miller before the 20th of January, 1941, exempt any such rubber from the payment of such duty.

(2) Any claim for exemption under this section shall be lodged with the Proper Officer on or before the 23rd January, 1941.

Passed this 20th day of January, 1941.

H. P. BRYSON,
Clerk of Councils.

Dear MacLeod,

Would you please refer to your letter No. T.7/2 of the 19th March 1941 about du Toit's request for exemption from Straits Settlements income tax for the Union Trade Commissioner and Assistant Trade Commissioner at Singapore on the basis of reciprocity.

I am not quite sure what the "reciprocal arrangement now standing between the United Kingdom and the Union Governments" amounts to, but I do not prima facie see how it can be binding on the Straits Settlements Government.

I should explain, however, that income tax has been introduced in the Straits Settlements very recently as a means of raising revenue for war expenditure. The Ordinance has only recently passed the local Legislative Council and has not yet been referred to the Secretary of State, so that we do not know its exact terms as passed. We are aware, however, that under one clause of it the Governor-in-Council has power to declare various persons, classes or companies to be exempt from liability to the tax and to assessment under the Ordinance, and that it is the intention of the Straits Settlements Government to use their powers under this clause to exempt from income tax in the Straits Settlements those incomes which already pay income tax in this country. The Department of Overseas Trade approached us on behalf of the British Trade Commissioner and his staff at Singapore, and so have the Ministry of Information on behalf of the staff of their Far Eastern Bureau. In both cases the argument which was put forward was that in addition to the salary of these officials their allowances etc., which are free from United Kingdom income tax, were in fact regarded as part of the emoluments of the post, and if they became liable to income tax in the Straits Settlements the cost would ultimately fall on the United Kingdom Government.

===== Page 198 =====
I am not sure whether similar considerations apply in the case of the Union Trade Commissioners, but if this is in fact the case, and I dare say du Toit can confirm it, I think that the Straits Settlements authorities will be prepared to meet any representations made to them on the point. Do you think that we should clear this up before we pass du Toit's representations on to the Governor?

Yours sincerely,

We understand, however, that incurs already liable to tax in another part of the Empire at a lower rate than the Straits Settlements rate (1c). $4,800 – $6,000 at 2%, $6,000 – $7,200 at 3%, $7,200 – $8,400 at 4%, $8,400 – $12,000 at 5%, $12,000 – $16,000 at 6%, $16,000 – $20,000 at 7%, and over $20,000 at 8% will be exempt from the Straits Tax. Does the Union Trade Commission pay Union Income Tax on this salary and is the rate higher than the figures quoted?

We also know that the Governor in Council has discretionary powers to declare expenses exempt from liability to the tax and the purpose is to exercise this power on behalf of the D.O.T.'s Trade Commission staff at Singapore. Their incursions are exempt from tax under the arrangements of the Singapore Government but they do not pay U.K. income tax in their affairs. It was emphasized that they do not pay U.K. income tax in their affairs. Their allowances were in effect treated as part of representations that their allowances would fall on the U.K. Exchequer, to be adjusted accordingly so that the real burden would fall on the U.K. Exchequer.

Can you say if the same arguments apply in the case of the Union staff?

I am sorry to tell you that the 20 imaginary draft of the Union staff would be helpful to clear up that point before referring du Toit's request to Singapore.

===== Page 199 =====
Dear Monson,

I enclose a copy of a letter which we have received from du Toit requesting the exemption from payment of local income tax of the Union Trade Commissioner and Assistant Trade Commissioner at Singapore (both of whom are apparently liable for tax under a War Tax Ordinance of 1941), on the basis of reciprocity.

I send on the letter semi-officially because it is prefaced by an argument which is unsound in that Union Trade Commissioners abroad no longer come under the jurisdiction of the Union High Commissioner in London - though I believe that this was the case at one time - and therefore the exemptions provided under United Kingdom legislation do not apply in these cases.

From that point of view I think, therefore, that du Toit's request can only be considered as "covered by the reciprocal arrangement now obtaining between the United Kingdom and Union Governments" in a very general sense.

In the circumstances, you will no doubt wish to consider whether the Malayan authorities should be approached on the point whether they would be willing to grant exemption on a reciprocal basis, without special reference to the "principle" mentioned in du Toit's letter, and it seems that it would be best that the reply should go direct to the Union High Commissioner's Office, with a copy to us.

I am sending a copy of this correspondence to Mr. P.L. Smith at the Treasury for information.

W.B.L. MONSON, ESQ.

===== Page 200 =====
Dear Dixon,

As you are no doubt aware, all members of the personal staff of the High Commissioner, ordinarily resident outside the United Kingdom, are exempt from income tax under the Finance Act, 1923. Similarly, the salaries and emoluments payable to all persons holding office in the Union as officials of the Imperial Government, British Colonies or possessions, are exempt from Union income tax, provided that such persons are not ordinarily resident in South Africa.

The Union Trade Commissioner at Singapore has recently approached the Union Government concluding that he and the Assistant Trade Commissioner are liable to pay income tax under War Tax Ordinance 1941. The Union authorities

R.L. Dixon, Esq.,
Dominions Office,
Downing Street, S.W.1.

===== Page 201 =====
Authorities will, therefore, be glad to learn whether the Trade Commissioner and Assistant Trade Commissioner are considered to be covered by the reciprocal arrangement now obtaining between the United Kingdom Government and the Union Government. If it is not the case, the Union Government will be glad if exemption may be granted.

Yours sincerely,

(Signed) F.J. du Toit.

MALAYAN WAR TAX BILL TO AID BRITAIN

APPROVAL BY COUNCIL
FROM OUR CORRESPONDENT
SINGAPORE, FEB. 3

The Legislative Council today approved by 22 votes to one the War Tax Bill imposing an income tax up to a maximum of 8 per cent. The only dissentient was Mr. F. D. Bisseker, Unofficial Member for Penang, who later stated that he would not oppose the application of the tax. Consequently, it could be said that there is unanimous support for the successful application of the Bill.

The Governor, Sir Shenton Thomas, stated that the Straits Settlements Government, in common with all Colonial Governments, were recently asked to consider the feasibility of an excess profits tax. He would say at present no more than that there appeared to be many difficulties in the way.

The Bill provides for the payment of the yield of the tax to the Imperial Government for war purposes. Annual incomes of under 376 are exempt; 2 per cent is payable on incomes between 376 and 720, after which the rate will be increased until a maximum of 8 per cent is reached on annual incomes of over 3,400.

===== Page 211 =====
(1241). No. 74. Berlin. German submarines destroyed a convoy with fifteen ships totaling one hundred and ten thousand tons as well as British auxiliary cruiser "Caledonia" (seventeen thousand tons).

(1244). No. 75. Berlin. On December second, German submarines attacked and destroyed a large convoy bound for England. Although the convoy was strongly protected and notwithstanding energetic resistance, fifteen ships totaling over one hundred ten thousand gross register tons as well as British auxiliary cruiser "Caledonia" (17,000 tons), which was one of the vessels protecting the convoy, were sunk. It is probable that two other ships belonging to the convoy and totaling sixteen thousand gross register tons were also sunk.

(1249). No. 71. Vichy. Marshall Petain arrived at Marseilles on official visit Tuesday morning.

(1250). No. 72. Madrid. Supreme army council, which consists besides War Minister General Varela of Generals Orgaz, Saliquet, Davila, and Kindelan, met Monday evening with War Minister in chain. Under Secretary of State in Ministry of War General Alonzo Vega was also present in his capacity as Secretary of Council.

(1250). No. 73. Moscow. Report here from Tashkent, which is capital of Soviet Republic Uzbekistan, states that first German Uzbek dictionary has now been published there and will be employed in all secondary schools.

===== Page 214 =====
DEFENCE OF MALAYA

"ACTIVE WAR CLOSER THAN EVER"

FROM OUR CORRESPONDENT

SINGAPORE, JAN. 31

Sir Shenton Thomas, the High Commissioner, in a speech to the Federal Council at Kuala Lumpur today on the Income-tax Bill, said he regretted the differences of opinion which had arisen on taxation "when active war is more close to the shores of Malaya than ever before."

No one, said Sir Shenton Thomas, could possibly estimate the financial liability which the war would impose on the Federated Malay States and Malaya as a whole. Without exaggerating or being an alarmist he wanted to tell the Council plainly that this country might yet have to face a state of war the cost of which would be incalculable. It would be mad self-complacency and wishful thinking of the most reckless kind not to recognize this and make full provision for it, financial and otherwise, within the time available.

The British Government, the High Commissioner explained, considered that the first call on Malayan war taxation should be for Malayan defence, and the utmost importance was attached to maintaining the defences of the Colony in the highest state of efficiency. The Government were therefore relieved of the obligation to remit to London the proceeds of direct taxation. The Secretary of State therefore had no objection to the official members of the Council voting as they wished.

The Bill secured a second reading by 20 votes to eight, one official member and seven unofficial members voting against and five unofficial and 15 official members voting in favour.

===== Page 215 =====

Protest will be forwarded to you as soon as it is received. Meantime Federal Council adjourned till the 11th February when I propose to take the Bill through remaining stage unless instructed to the contrary after you have considered protest.

CODE TELEGRAM

FROM Governor Straits Settlements

D. 16th January 1941.
R. 18th January 1941, 07:05 hrs. Received in Registry

No. 17 Government House Confidential.

Wish to make the following representations in regard to Income Tax in Malaya.

(1) Original proposal was to remit all proceeds to Imperial Government for prosecution of the war and in consequence your predecessor requested (see your telegram No. 246 of 15th March 1940 Confidential) that official majority should not be used to force legislation through the Legislative Council or Federal Council. When I left for England last April public opinion was not sufficiently in favour to justify legislation.

(2) Subsequently the O.A.G. appointed joint committee representing both governments and consisting of four officials and seven unofficials including two unofficial members of the Legislative Council and one unofficial member of the Federal Council.

(3) Report of this committee has been received and unanimously recommends legislation on the lines of Straits Settlements War Tax Ordinance 1919 and Bills are about to be introduced in the Legislative Council and the Federal Council.

(4) Committee recommends "net proceeds of tax shall be applied to defraying the expenses incurred in prosecution of the present war" and preamble of the bills is to this effect. It is intended to use the proceeds for local war expenditure which is running into many million dollars apart from our heavy commitments in the shape of food reserves etc. and I have advised all Malayan governments that assistance to the Imperial Government for the time being can best be rendered by setting aside money for local war expenditure instead of sending it to England.

(5) As the proceeds of Income Tax are to be used for Malayan war effort it seems that request of your predecessor - see (1) above - is no longer appropriate. May I be informed that it is withdrawn and that official members of both councils are free to vote as they please when the bills are debated.

(6) It has now been decided - see F.M.S. Confidential telegram No. 104 - to allow some ten days between introduction and final stage of bills in both councils. In the Legislative Council unofficials will support the bill and indeed it may be passed unanimously. In the Federal Council I hope four bare unofficial majority. But in any case it is clearly the duty of Malayan Governments to make all necessary provision for defence and moreover it is not possible for officials to abstain from voting without suspension or standing orders. I may say in addition to Income Tax I propose to impose further indirect taxation.

===== Page 221 =====
FROM Governor, Straits Settlements

D. 16th January, 1941.
R. 17th January, 18:25 hrs.

No. 16 Government House Confidential.

My telegram No. 29 was sent by Governor's Deputy. I do not maintain that the proposed export duty in lieu of income tax is satisfactory, but it has been recommended by the joint committee representing Straits Settlements and Federated Malay States, which has also pronounced in favour of income tax and has drafted legislation to impose it, which is being introduced in Legislative Council on 20th January and Federal Council on 30th January. This is a great advance on previous opinions and it is probable that in the Legislative Council the bill will be passed unanimously and in the Federal Council it appears that unofficial members will support it by a bare majority. I hope therefore that income tax will be in force in the colony and Federated Malay States during February, but any substantial alteration in the bill such as would be required by adherence to the view expressed in your telegram will mean that deliberation will have to commence afresh and may be unsuccessful. Submit that it is better to secure the major issue of income tax first, leaving consideration of minority issues till later. Unless I hear to the contrary, I propose to proceed with legislation as recommended by the committee.

Bournemouth.

16th January, 1941.

URGENT.

Sir,

I am directed by Mr. Harcourt Johnstone to enclose herewith for the information of the Secretary of State for the Colonies a copy of a telegram received from His Majesty's Trade Commissioner at Singapore (Mr. R.B. Willmot) on the question of the Government of the Straits Settlements war tax bill.

Mr. Willmot is liable to United Kingdom income tax on his salary and bonus only, but in addition, he receives local allowance (£275 per annum), representation allowance (£250 per annum), and house rent allowance (£148 per annum). Mr. Thorogood, Assistant to H.M. Trade Commissioner, is liable to United Kingdom tax on his salary only and in addition receives a local allowance (£450 per annum).

The rates of local allowance, representation allowance, and house rent allowance payable to members of H.M. Trade Commissioner Service have been fixed in consultation with His Majesty's Treasury on the basis that they are not subject to deduction of income or similar tax, and Mr. Johnstone would therefore be glad if H.M. Trade Commissioner at Singapore and his Assistant could be exempted from the provisions of the draft war tax bill shortly to be introduced to the Legislative Council in the Straits Settlements.

I am,
Sir,
Your obedient Servant,
The Under-Secretary of State,
Colonial Office.

===== Page 223 =====
Following for Edgecumbe Establishment. Government of the Straits Settlements have published a draft war tax bill which will be introduced at the meeting of the Legislative Council on January 20th. The bill allows exemption of any income subject to income tax in the British Empire and also exemption of allowances paid out of navy, army, or air force funds to personnel of H.M. Forces. However, allowances of Thorogood and myself, which are fixed at a rate bearing in mind they are not liable to tax in the United Kingdom, are not exempt in the draft bill. I have taken up the matter semi-officially with the acting Financial Secretary, who states that deductions in respect of allowance paid out of navy, army, and air force funds are allowed because this is a war tax and these persons are engaged in the defence of the country, and he does not consider similar deductions should be allowed to civil officials.

We already have the service granted of duty-free liquor and tobacco which is not granted to us. Although the tax is called the war tax, it is virtually a tax on income, from which form of taxation this colony has been immune up to now. When I was in India, I was exempt from payment and immune from income tax on all my emoluments, and I consider we should be so exempt here. Please make representation through the Colonial Office before the draft bill is submitted to the Legislative Council. We are already paying infinitely more in United Kingdom income tax than anyone with similar emoluments is liable to pay under the local scheme.

Willmot.

Received at 8/1

CODE TELEGRAM

FROM Governor STRAITS SETTLEMENTS.

D. 14th January, 1941
R. 14th January 1941, 17.45 hrs.

Received in Registry

No. 29 S.S. CONFIDENTIAL.

Your telegram No. 50: War Tax Bill will be effective from January 1st. Hence it is fair that rubber and tin producers should begin to pay additional duties on the earliest possible date this year.

It is admitted that export duty is a rough and ready substitute for an income tax. This comment might be applied to the whole of the Bill but it must be emphasized that the Bill is designed to provide temporary taxation for war purposes and does not profess to follow lines of more permanent income tax legislation, e.g., as that in Ceylon which was [corrupt group] committee. The proposal was made on recommendation of the committee. It is an adaptation of Section 7 (c) 1919 War Tax Ordinance. The Committee was of the opinion that the Bill would meet with less opposition if additional export duties were imposed in lieu of tax on profits of these industries. The proposal has no relation to an excess profits tax which will be dealt with generally in reply to your telegram No. 252.

Presumption is correct. Provision exempting United Kingdom companies from additional export duties is contained in the Bill.

Do not understand suggestion of increased discrimination. Position of United Kingdom companies remains unchanged. Malayan companies pay more towards war but even then not as much as United Kingdom companies. Effect is to reduce margin of difference.

===== Page 227 =====
Dear Ashley & Clarke,

Sept 41

I enclose a copy of a telegram from the High Commissioner for the Malay States, asking whether there is any suggestion or protest grounds to an increase in the export duty on iron ore in the states of Kelantan and Tanggan from its present levels of 10% above and 12½% above. You will see that as Ashley is the only exporter, the High Commissioner possibly appears a little [UNCLEAR].

===== Page 228 =====
Approaches about their reactions to such an increase.

We, for our part, see no reason why the proposed increase in duty should show us to be worse. Japan can have no guarantee on freely grounded and stationed, fully attested to increase their revenue to meet war expenditure. This applies whether or not it is decided to increase the duties on matter and time to which the Government also refers, and in which the care or present and acting further information before us will approve the increase.

Could you tell us more about this to Kesaroke?

I am sending an enquiry of this to Kesaroke.

(Signed) G. E. J. GENT.

===== Page 230 =====
All circumstances which have been fixed at present levels by reference to the fixed trust are not subject to taxation. If they were, in creating neglect, they would in fact have to incur the tax, and the effect of their becoming so liable in the Straits Settlements would be little worse than to pay the tax. This, I am confident, clearly would not be your intention.

I would be grateful, therefore, if you could consider the possibility of amending at a suitable opportunity the Straits Settlements Income Tax Bill in such a way that offices of the D.O.T. remaining in the territory at Singapore shall not pay local income tax on any part of the emoluments derived directly from that country, whether these emoluments are important or are not subject to this tax at some or all of the U.K.

CODE TELEGRAM

FROM Governor STRAITS SETTLEMENTS

D. 10th January, 1941.
R. 11th 11.30 hrs.

No. 1 U.F.M.S. CONFIDENTIAL.

My telegram No. 10 S.S. Johore Kelantan Trengganu, the only Malayan States exporting and imposing export duty on iron ore, are considering increasing the duty from 10% ad valorem to 12½% ad valorem. Would there be any political objection to this proposal? Although the only exporters are Japanese, I suggest that if the increase is announced at the same time or shortly after increases in duties on rubber and tin, no charge of discrimination against Japan could be upheld.

===== Page 232 =====
My dear Eastwood,

We have received from our Trade Commissioner, Willmot, in Singapore a telegram of which the substantive portion reads:

"Following for Edgcumbe Establishment. Government of the Straits Settlements have published draft war tax bill which will be introduced at meeting of Legislative Council January 20th. Bill allows exemption of any income subject to income tax in British Empire and also exemption of allowances paid out of Navy, Army, Air Force fund to personnel of H.M. Forces but allowances of Thorogood and myself which are fixed at rate bearing in mind they are not liable to tax in the United Kingdom are not exempt in draft bill. I have taken up the matter semi-officially with acting Financial Secretary who states that deductions in respect of allowance paid out of Navy, Army and Air Force funds are allowed because this is a war tax and these persons are engaged in defence of country and he does not consider similar deductions should be allowed to civil officials.

3 Service already granted of duty free liquor and tobacco which is not granted to us. Although tax is called the war tax it is virtually a tax on income from which form of taxation this colony has been immune up to now."

My Minister desires me to point out that our Trade Commissioners in the Empire receive, in addition

to/

C. G. Eastwood, Esq., Colonial Office.

===== Page 233 =====
To their substantive pay, (1) local allowance, (2) house rent allowance, and (3) representation allowance. All these allowances are, in fact, part of the emoluments attaching to the posts.

Local allowance is subject to deduction of tax at source; this allowance takes the place, with our overseas officers, of the additional pay awarded to United Kingdom Civil Servants at home when rates of pay were last consolidated.

House rent allowance and representation allowance are, as already stated, regarded as part of the emoluments attaching to the appointment. They are fixed at their present levels by reference to the fact that they are not subject to taxation. If they were subjected to income tax, either at source or overseas, these two allowances would have to be increased. It will be seen, therefore, that if the Government of the Straits Settlements exacted income tax on these allowances, H.M. Government here would have to increase the rent allowance and representation allowance to make good the tax paid to the Local Administration. In other words, it would be H.M. Government in the United Kingdom that would bear the tax: they would thus, in fact, be called upon to contribute to the revenue of the Local Administration. My Minister feels that this is clearly not the wish of the Officer Administering the Government of the Straits Settlements: if it were, provision would not have been made (as it appears to have been) that no local income tax should be levied on the emoluments subjected to income tax at source in the United Kingdom.

In India, where there is income tax, the Trade Commissioner is not taxed upon his rent allowance and representation allowance.

My Minister would be glad if arrangements could be made to move the Officer Administering the Government of the Straits Settlements to amend the Bill which is to

===== Page 234 =====
be introduced before the Legislative Council on the 20th January in such a way that officers appointed from this country to represent this Department shall not pay local income tax on any part of their emoluments derived directly from H.M. Government in this country, whether those emoluments are, or are not, subjected to income tax at source in the United Kingdom.

If the Officer Administering the Government of the Straits Settlements should so desire, arrangements could be made to notify him annually of the total emoluments paid by H.M. Government to each of the two officers (Mr. Willmot and Mr. Thorogood) representing this Department in Singapore.

Whilst the point may be irrelevant, I might mention that our representatives are paying far more in income tax (deducted here) than Colonial officers on similar scales of pay will be called upon to pay in local income tax.

I am sending a copy of this letter to Syers of the Treasury.

Yours sincerely,

G.B. Blaker

DRAFT TELEGRAM (code)

GOVERNOR,
STRAITS SETTLEMENTS.

Important No. 50

Your telegram No. 10 S.S.
Proposed additional duty on rubber and tin.
I note that companies paying this duty would be exempt from proposed income tax in S.S. and F.M.S. In these circumstances it seems to me premature to introduce additional duty prior to passage of Income Tax Legislation through Legislative and Federal Councils.

In any case, I regard export duty as a very rough and ready substitute for an income tax and would not welcome its adoption as a precedent for the future, if machinery for the more accurate adjustment of taxation to real profits is to be set up. Moreover, if proposal is made because you consider that the income tax proposed would not take sufficient percentage of the companies' profits, I would regard an excess profits tax as I suggested in my circular telegram No. 252, as the most suitable procedure.

===== Page 236 =====
I presume that under the proposals companies paying income tax in the United Kingdom would continue to be free from liability to export duty; otherwise their position will be prejudiced since they could get relief in this country if they paid income tax in Malaya but would not be able to obtain deduction from United Kingdom income tax of any export duty paid in Malaya. If this presumption is correct, however, there will be substantially increased discrimination between United Kingdom and non-United Kingdom companies.

I should like further detailed explanations in the light of the above comments if it is desired to proceed with this proposal.
